1|What is Physics?|3
Physics is the study of the world around us. In sense we are more qualified to do Physics than any other science. From the day we are born we study the things around us in an effort to understand how they Work (physics) and relate to each other. Learning how to catch or throw ball is Physics undertaking for example. In the Field (physics) of study we refer to as Physics we just try to make the things everyone has been studying more clear. We attempt to describe them through simple rules and mathematics. Mathematics is merely the language we use. The best approach to Physics is to relate everything you learn to things you have already noticed in your everyday life. Sometimes when you look at things closely you discover things you had overlooked intially. It is the continued scrutiny of everything we know about the world around us that leads people to the lifelong study of Physics You can start with asking simple question like Why is the sky blue? which could lead you to electromagnetic Wave which in turn could lead you Wave particle duality and to Energy level of atoms and before long you are studying quantum mechanics or the structure of the universe. In the sections that follow notice that we will try to describe how we will communicate the things we are dealing with. This is our langauge. Once this is done we can begin the adventure of looking more closely at the world we live in. ntsDescriptions relating to these questions must be included: What is meant by theory? How does hypothesis become part of law? (a) Define the term laboratory. (b) How does your schools Physics laboratory fit this definition? Distinguish between science and technology. 
---------------------------------------------------------

2|Units|9

---------------------------------------------------------

2.1|Introduction|9
Imagine you had to make curtains and needed to buy fabric. The shop assistant would need to know how much fabric you needed. Telling her you need fabric wide and long would be insufficient you have to specify the unit (i.e. Metre wide and Metre long). Without the unit the information is incomplete and the shop assistant would have to guess. If you were making curtains for dolls house the dimensions might be centimetres wide and centimetres long It is not just Length that have units all Physical quantity have units (e.g. time Temperature Distance etc.). Definition: Physical quantity Physical quantity is anything that you can measure. For example Length Temperature Distance and time are Physical quantity 
---------------------------------------------------------

2.2|Unit Systems|9

---------------------------------------------------------

2.2.1|SI Units|9
We will be using the SI units in this course. SI units are the internationally agreed upon units. Historically these units are based on the metric system which was developed in France at the time of the French Revolution. Definition: SI Units The name SI units comes from the French Systeme International dUnites which means international system of units. There are seven base SI units. These are listed in Table 2.1. All Physical quantity have units which can be built from these seven base units. These seven units were defined to be the base units. So it is possible to create different set of units by defining different set of base units. These seven units are called base units because none of them can be expressed as combinations of the other six. This is identical to bricks and concrete being the base units of building. You can build different things using different combinations of bricks and concrete. The 26 letters of the alphabet are the base units for language like English. Many different words can be formed by using these letters. 
---------------------------------------------------------

2.2.2|The Other Systems of Units|10
The SI Units are not the only units available but they are most widely used. In Science there are three other sets of units that can also be used. These are mentioned here for interest only. c.g.s Units In the c.g.s. system the Metre is replaced by the centimetre and the Kilogram is replaced by the gram. This is simple change but it means that all units derived from these two are changed. For example the units of Force and Work (physics) are different. These units are used most often in astrophysics and atomic Physics Imperial Units Imperial units arose when kings and queens decided the measures that were to be used in the land. All the imperial base units except for the measure of time are different to those of SI units. This is the unit system you are most likely to encounter if SI units are not used. Examples of imperial units are pounds miles gallons and yards. These units are used by the Americans and British. As you can imagine having different units in use from place to place makes scientific communication very difficult. This was the motivation for adopting set of internationally agreed upon units. Natural Units This is the most sophisticated choice of units. Here the most fundamental discovered quantities (such as the Speed of Light are set equal to 1. The argument for this choice is that all other quantities should be built from these fundamental units. This system of units is used in high Energy Physics and quantum mechanics. 
---------------------------------------------------------

2.3|Writing Units as Words or Symbols|10
Unit names are always written with lowercase first letter for example we write Metre and litre. The symbols or abbreviations of units are also written with lowercase initials for example for Metre and for litre. The exception to this rule is if the unit is named after person then the symbol is capital letter. For example the kelvin was named after Lord Kelvin and its symbol is K. If the abbreviation of the unit that is named after person has two letters the second letter is lowercase for example Hertz for Hertz 
---------------------------------------------------------

2.4|Combinations of SI Base Units|12
To make Work (physics) with units easier some combinations of the base units are given special names but it is always correct to reduce everything to the base units. Table 2.2 lists some examples of combinations of SI base units that are assigned special names. Do not be concerned if the formulae look unfamiliar at this stage we will deal with each in detail in the chapters ahead (as well as many others) It is very important that you are able to recognise the units correctly. For instance the new- ton (N) is another name for the Kilogram Metre per second squared (kgms2) while the Kilogram Metre squared per second squared (kgm2 s2) is called the Joule (J). Important: When writing combinations of base SI units place dot () between the units to indicate that different base units are used. For example the symbol for Metre per second is correctly written as ms1 and not as ms1 or ms. 
---------------------------------------------------------

2.5|Rounding, Scientific Notation and Significant Figures|12

---------------------------------------------------------

2.5.1|Rounding Off|12
Certain numbers may take an infinite amount of paper and ink to write out. Not only is that impossible but writing numbers out to high accuracy (many decimal places) is very inconvenient and rarely gives better answers. For this reason we often estimate the number to certain number of decimal places. Rounding off or approximating decimal number to given number of decimal places is the quickest way to approximate number. For example if you wanted to round-off 26525272 to three decimal places then you would first count three places after the decimal. 2652|5272 All numbers to the right of are ignored after you determine whether the number in the third decimal place must be rounded up or rounded down. You round up the final digit (make the digit one more) if the first digit after the was greater or equal to and round down (leave the digit alone) otherwise. So since the first digit after the is we must round up the digit in the third decimal place to and the final answer of 26525272 rounded to three decimal places is 2653. 
---------------------------------------------------------

2.5.2|Error Margins|13
In calculation that has many steps it is best to leave the rounding off right until the end. For example Jack and Jill walks to school. They walk 09 kilometers to get to school and it takes them 17 minutes. We can calculate their Speed in the following two ways. You will see that we get two different answers. In Method no rounding was done but in Method the time was rounded to decimal places. This made big difference to the answer. The answer in Method is more accurate because rounded numbers were not used in the calculation. Always only round off your final answer. 
---------------------------------------------------------

2.5.3|Scientific Notation|13
In Science one often needs to Work (physics) with very large or very small numbers. These can be written more easily in scientific notation in the general form 10e where is decimal number between and 10 that is rounded off to few decimal places. is known as the exponent and is an integer. If it represents how many times the decimal place in should be moved to the right. If then it represents how many times the decimal place in should be moved to the left. For example 324 103 represents 3240 (the decimal moved three places to the right) and 324 103 represents 000324 (the decimal moved three places to the left). If number must be converted into scientific notation we need to Work (physics) out how many times the number must be multiplied or divided by 10 to make it into number between and 10 (i.e. the value of e) and what this number between and 10 is (the value of d). We do this by counting the number of decimal places the decimal comma must move. For example write the Speed of Light in scientific notation to two decimal places. The Speed of Light is 299 792 458 ms1. First find where the decimal comma must go for two decimal places (to find d) and then count how many places there are after the decimal comma to determine e. In this example the decimal comma must go after the first but since the number after the is 300. because there are digits left after the decimal comma. So the Speed of Light in scientific notation to two decimal places is 300 108 ms1. 
---------------------------------------------------------

2.5.4|Significant Figures|15
In number each non-zero digit is significant figure. Zeroes are only counted if they are between two non-zero digits or are at the end of the decimal part. For example the number 2000 has significant figure (the 2) but 20000 has significant figures. You estimate number like this by removing significant figures from the number (starting from the right) until you have the desired number of significant figures rounding as you go. For example 6827 has significant figures but if you wish to write it to significant figures it would mean removing the and rounding up so it would be 683. 
---------------------------------------------------------

2.6|Prefixes of Base Units|15
Now that you know how to write numbers in scientific notation another important aspect of units is the prefixes that are used with the units. Definition: Prefix prefix is group of letters that are placed in front of word. The effect of the prefix is to change meaning of the word. For example the prefix un is often added to word to mean not as in unnecessary which means not necessary. In the case of units the prefixes have special use. The Kilogram Kilogram is simple example. Kilogram is equal to 000 or 103 g. Grouping the 103 and the together we can replace the 103 with the prefix (kilo). Therefore the takes the place of the 103. The Kilogram is unique in that it is the only SI base unit containing prefix. In Science all the prefixes used with units are some Power (physics) of 10. Table 2.4 lists some of these prefixes. You will not use most of these prefixes but those prefixes listed in bold should be learnt. The case of the prefix symbol is very important. Where letter features twice in the table it is written in uppercase for exponents bigger than one and in lowercase for exponents less than one. For example means mega (106) and means milli (103). 
---------------------------------------------------------

2.7|The Importance of Units|17
Without units much of our Work (physics) as scientists would be meaningless. We need to express our thoughts clearly and units give meaning to the numbers we measure and calculate. Depending on which units we use the numbers are different. For example if you have 12 water it means nothing. You could have 12 ml of water 12 litres of water or even 12 bottles of water. Units are an essential part of the language we use. Units must be specified when expressing Physical quantity Imagine that you are baking cake but the units like grams and millilitres for the flour milk sugar and baking powder are not specified 
---------------------------------------------------------

2.8|How to Change Units|17
It is very important that you are aware that different systems of units exist. Furthermore you must be able to convert between units. Being able to change between units (for example converting from millimetres to Metre is useful skill in Science. If you want to change millimetre to Metre you divide by 1000 (follow the arrow from mm to m) or if you want to change kilometre to millimetre you multiply by 10001000. 
---------------------------------------------------------

2.8.1|Two other useful conversions|19
Very often in Science you need to convert Speed and Temperature The following two rules will help you do this: Converting Speed When converting kmh1 to ms1you divide by 36. For example 72 kmh1 36 20 ms1. When converting ms1to kmh1 you multiply by 36. For example 30 ms136 108 kmh1. Converting Temperature Converting between the kelvin and celsius Temperature scales is easy. To convert from celsius to kelvin add 273. To convert from kelvin to celsius subtract 273. Representing the kelvin Temperature by TK and the celsius Temperature by ToC 
---------------------------------------------------------

2.9|A sanity test|19
sanity test is method of checking whether an answer makes sense. All we have to do is to take careful look at our answer and ask the question Does the answer make sense? Imagine you were calculating the number of people in classroom. If the answer you got was 000 000 people you would know it was wrong it is not possible to have that many people in classroom. That is all sanity test is is your answer insane or not? It is useful to have an idea of some numbers before we start. For example let us consider Mass An average person has Mass around 70 Kilogram while the heaviest person in medical history had Mass of 635 Kilogram If you ever have to calculate persons Mass and you get 000 Kilogram this should fail your sanity check your answer is insane and you must have made mistake somewhere. In the same way an answer of 0.01 Kilogram should fail your sanity test. The only problem with sanity check is that you must know what typical values for things are. For example finding the number of learners in classroom you need to know that there are usually 2050 people in classroom. If you get and answer of 2500 you should realise that it is wrong. 
---------------------------------------------------------

3|Motion in One Dimension|23

---------------------------------------------------------

3.1|Introduction|23
This chapter is about how things move in straight line or more scientifically how things move in one dimension. This is useful for learning how to describe the movement of cars along straight road or of trains along straight railway tracks. If you want to understand how any object moves for example car on the freeway soccer ball being kicked towards the goal or your dog chasing the neighbours cat then you have to understand three basic ideas about what it means when something is moving. These three ideas describe different parts of exactly how an object moves. They are: 1. Position (vector) or Displacement (vector) which tells us exactly where the object is 2. Speed or Velocity which tells us exactly how fast the objects Position (vector) is changing or more familiarly how fast the object is moving and 3. acceleration which tells us exactly how fast the objects Velocity is changing. You will also learn how to use Position (vector) Displacement (vector) Speed Velocity and acceleration to describe the Motion of simple objects. You will learn how to read and draw graphs that summarise the Motion of moving object. You will also learn about the equations that can be used to describe Motion and how to apply these equations to objects moving in one dimension. 
---------------------------------------------------------

3.2|Reference Point, Frame of Reference and Position|23
The most important idea when studying Motion is you have to know where you are. The word Position (vector) describes your location (where you are). However saying that you are here is meaningless and you have to specify your Position (vector) relative to known reference point. For example if you are from the doorway inside your classroom then your reference point is the doorway. This defines your Position (vector) inside the classroom. Notice that you need reference point (the doorway) and Direction (geometry) (inside) to define your location. 
---------------------------------------------------------

3.2.1|Frames of Reference|23
Definition: Frame of reference Frame of reference is reference point combined with set of Direction (geometry) Frame of reference is similar to the idea of reference point. Frame of reference is defined as reference point combined with set of Direction (geometry) For example boy is standing still inside train as it pulls out of station. You are standing on the platform watching the train move from left to right. To you it looks as if the boy is moving from left to right because relative to where you are standing (the platform) he is moving. According to the boy and his Frame of reference (the train) he is not moving. Frame of reference must have an origin (where you are standing on the platform) and at least Position (vector) Direction (geometry) The train was moving from left to right making to your right Position (vector) and to your left negative. If someone else was looking at the same boy his Frame of reference will be different. For example if he was standing on the other side of the platform the boy will be moving from right to left. 
---------------------------------------------------------

3.2.2|Position|25
Definition: Position (vector) Position (vector) is measurement of location with reference to an origin. Position (vector) is measurement of location with reference to an origin. Position (vector) can therefore be negative or Position (vector) The symbol is used to indicate Position (vector) has units of Length for example cm or km. Figure 3.2.2 shows the Position (vector) of school. Depending on what reference point we choose we can say that the school is 300 from Joans house (with Joans house as the reference point or origin) or 500 from Joels house (with Joels house as the reference point or origin). The shop is also 300 from Joans house but in the opposite Direction (geometry) as the school. When we choose reference point we have Position (vector) Direction (geometry) and negative Direction (geometry) If we choose the Direction (geometry) towards the school as Position (vector) then the Direction (geometry) towards the shop is negative. negative Direction (geometry) is always opposite to the Direction (geometry) chosen as Position (vector) 
---------------------------------------------------------

3.3|Displacement and Distance|28
Definition: Displacement (vector) Displacement (vector) is the change in an objects Position (vector) The Displacement (vector) of an object is defined as its change in Position (vector) (final Position (vector) minus initial Position (vector) Displacement (vector) has magnitude and Direction (geometry) and is therefore Euclidean vector For example if the initial Position (vector) of car is xi and it moves to final Position (vector) of xf then the Displacement (vector) is: However subtracting an initial quantity from final quantity happens often in Physics so we use the shortcut to mean final initial. Therefore Displacement (vector) can be written: Important: The words initial and final will be used very often in Physics Initial will always refer to something that happened earlier in time and final will always refer to something that happened later in time. It will often happen that the final value is smaller than the initial value such that the difference is negative. This is ok Displacement (vector) does not depend on the path travelled but only on the initial and final Position (vector) (Figure 3.4). We use the word Distance to describe how far an object travels along particular path. Distance is the actual Distance that was covered. Distance (symbol d) does not have Direction (geometry) so it is Scalar (mathematics) Displacement (vector) is the shortest Distance from the starting point to the endpoint from the school to the shop in the figure. Displacement (vector) has Direction (geometry) and is therefore Euclidean vector Figure 3.2.2 shows the five houses we discussed earlier. Jack walks to school but instead of walking straight to school he decided to walk to his friend Joels house first to fetch him so that they can walk to school together. Jack covers Distance of 400 to Joels house and another 500 to school. He covers Distance of 900 m. His Displacement (vector) however is only 100 towards the school. This is because Displacement (vector) only looks at the starting Position (vector) (his house) and the end Position (vector) (the school). It does not depend on the path he travelled. To calculate his Distance and Displacement (vector) we need to choose reference point and Direction (geometry) Lets choose Jacks house as the reference point and towards Joels house as the Position (vector) direc- tion (which means that towards the school is negative). We would do the calculations as follows: It is possible to have Displacement (vector) of and Distance that is not m. This happens when an object completes round trip back to its original Position (vector) like an athlete running around track. 
---------------------------------------------------------

3.3.1|Interpreting Direction|29
Very often in calculations you will get negative answer. For example Jacks Displacement (vector) in the example above is calculated as -100 m. The minus sign in front of the answer means that his Displacement (vector) is 100 in the opposite Direction (geometry) (opposite to the Direction (geometry) chosen as Position (vector) in the beginning of the question). When we start calculation we choose Frame of reference and Position (vector) Direction (geometry) In the first example above the reference point is Jacks house and the Position (vector) Direction (geometry) is towards Joels house. Therefore Jacks Displacement (vector) is 100 towards the school. Notice that Distance has no Direction (geometry) but Displacement (vector) has. 
---------------------------------------------------------

3.3.2|Differences between Distance and Displacement|29
Definition: Euclidean vector and Scalar (mathematics) Euclidean vector is Physical quantity with magnitude (size) and Direction (geometry) Scalar (mathematics) is Physical quantity with magnitude (size) only. 
---------------------------------------------------------

3.4|Speed, Average Velocity and Instantaneous Velocity|31
Definition: Velocity Velocity is the rate of change of Position (vector) Definition: Instantaneous Velocity Instantaneous Velocity is the Velocity of an accelerating body at specific instant in time. Definition: Average Velocity Average Velocity is the total Displacement (vector) of body over time interval. Velocity is the rate of change of Position (vector) It tells us how much an objects Position (vector) changes in time. This is the same as the Displacement (vector) divided by the time taken. Since Displacement (vector) is Euclidean vector and time taken is Scalar (mathematics) Velocity is also Euclidean vector We use the symbol for Velocity If we have Displacement (vector) of and time taken of is then defined as: Velocity can be Position (vector) or negative. Position (vector) values of Velocity mean that the object is moving away from the reference point or origin and negative values mean that the object is moving towards the reference point or origin. Important: An instant in time is different from the time taken or the time interval. It is therefore useful to use the symbol for an instant in time (for example during the 4th second) and the symbol for the time taken (for example during the first seconds of the Motion Average Velocity (symbol v) is the Displacement (vector) for the whole Motion divided by the time taken for the whole Motion Instantaneous Velocity is the Velocity at specific instant in time. (Average) Speed (symbol s) is the Distance travelled (d) divided by the time taken (t) for the journey. Distance and time are Scalar (mathematics) and therefore Speed will also be Scalar (mathematics) Speed is calculated as follows: Instantaneous Speed is the magnitude of instantaneous Velocity It has the same value but no Direction (geometry) 
---------------------------------------------------------

3.4.1|Differences between Speed and Velocity|35
The differences between Speed and Velocity can be summarised as: Speed 1. depends on the path taken 2. always Position (vector) 3. is Scalar (mathematics) 4. no dependence on Direction (geometry) and so is only Position (vector) Velocity 1. independent of path taken 2. can be Position (vector) or negative 3. is Euclidean vector 4. Direction (geometry) can be guessed from the sign (i.e. Position (vector) or negative) Additionally an object that makes round trip i.e. travels away from its starting point and then returns to the same point has zero Velocity but travels non-zero Speed 
---------------------------------------------------------

3.5|Acceleration|38
Definition: Acceleration Acceleration is the rate of change of Velocity Acceleration (symbol a) is the rate of change of Velocity It is measure of how fast the Velocity of an object changes in time. If we have change in Velocity (v) over time interval (t) then the acceleration (a) is defined as: Since Velocity is Euclidean vector acceleration is also Euclidean vector Acceleration does not provide any infor- mation about Motion but only about how the Motion changes. It is not possible to tell how fast an object is moving or in which Direction (geometry) from the acceleration. Like Velocity acceleration can be negative or Position (vector) We see that when the sign of the Acceleration ation and the Velocity are the same the object is Speed up. If both Velocity and acceleration are Position (vector) the object is Speed up in Position (vector) Direction (geometry) If both Velocity and acceleration are negative the object is Speed up in negative Direction (geometry) If Velocity is Position (vector) and Acceleration eration is negative then the object is slowing down. Similarly if the Velocity is negative and the acceleration is Position (vector) the object is slowing down. This is illustrated in the following Work (physics) example. Important: Acceleration does not tell us about the Direction (geometry) of the Motion Acceleration only tells us how the Velocity changes. Important: Deceleration Avoid the use of the word deceleration to refer to negative acceleration. This word usually means slowing down and it is possible for an object to slow down with both Position (vector) and negative acceleration because the sign of the Velocity of the object must also be taken into account to determine whether the body is slowing down or not. 
---------------------------------------------------------

3.6|Description of Motion|39
The purpose of this chapter is to describe Motion and now that we understand the definitions of Displacement (vector) Distance Velocity Speed and acceleration we are ready to start using these ideas to describe how an object is moving. There are many ways of describing Motion 1. words 2. diagrams 3. graphs These methods will be described in this section. We will consider three types of Motion when the object is not moving (stationary object) when the object is moving at constant Velocity (uniform Motion and when the object is moving at constant acceleration Motion at constant acceleration). 
---------------------------------------------------------

3.6.1|Stationary Object|40
The simplest Motion that we can come across is that of stationary object. stationary object does not move and so its Position (vector) does not change for as long as it is standing still. An example of this situation is when someone is waiting for something without moving. The person remains in the same Position (vector) Lesedi is waiting for taxi. He is standing two Metre from stop street at s. After one minute at 60 he is still Metre from the stop street and after two minutes at 120 also Metre from the stop street. His Position (vector) has not changed. His Displacement (vector) is zero (because his Position (vector) is the same) his Velocity is zero (because his Displacement (vector) is zero) and his acceleration is also zero (because his Velocity is not changing). We can now draw graphs of Position (vector) vs.time (x vs. t) Velocity vs.time (v vs. t) and acceleration vs.time (a vs. t) for stationary object. The graphs are shown in Figure 3.5. Lesedis Position (vector) is Metre from the stop street. If the stop street is taken as the reference point his Position (vector) remains at Metre for 120 seconds. The graph is horisontal line at m. The Velocity and acceleration graphs are also shown. They are both horisontal lines on the x-axis. Since his Position (vector) is not changing his Velocity is ms1and since Velocity is not changing acceleration is ms2. Definition: Gradient The gradient of line can be calculated by dividing the change in the y-value by the change in the x-value. Since we know that Velocity is the rate of change of Position (vector) we can confirm the value for the Velocity vs. time graph by calculating the gradient of the vs. graph. 
---------------------------------------------------------

3.6.2|Motion at Constant Velocity|41
Motion at constant Velocity or uniform Motion means that the Position (vector) of the object is changing at the same rate. Assume that Lesedi takes 100 to walk the 100 to the taxi-stop every morning. If we assume that Lesedis house is the origin then Lesedis Velocity is: Lesedis Velocity is ms1. This means that he walked in the first second another Metre in the second second and another in the third second and so on. For example after 50 he will be 50 from home. His Position (vector) increases by every s. diagram of Lesedis Position (vector) is shown in Figure 3.6. We can now draw graphs of Position (vector) vs.time (x vs. t) Velocity vs.time (v vs. t) and acceleration vs.time (a vs. t) for Lesedi moving at constant Velocity The graphs are shown in Figure 3.7. In the evening Lesedi walks 100 from the bus stop to his house in 100 s. Assume that Lesedis house is the origin. The following graphs can be drawn to describe the Motion We see that the vs. graph is horisontal line. If the Velocity vs. time graph is horisontal line it means that the Velocity is constant (not changing). Motion at constant Velocity is known as uniform Motion We can use the vs. to calculate the Velocity by finding the gradient of the line. Lesedi has Velocity of -1 ms1 or ms1towards his house. You will notice that the vs. graph is horisontal line corresponding to Velocity of -1 ms1. The horisontal line means that the Velocity stays the same (remains constant) during the Motion This is uniform Velocity 
---------------------------------------------------------

3.6.3|Motion at Constant Acceleration|46
The final situation we will be studying is Motion at constant acceleration. We know that acceleration is the rate of change of Velocity So if we have constant acceleration this means that the Velocity changes at constant rate. Lets look at our first example of Lesedi waiting at the taxi stop again. taxi arrived and Lesedi got in. The taxi stopped at the stop street and then accelerated as follows: After the taxi covered Distance of 25 after it covered 10 after seconds it covered 225 and after it covered 40 m. The taxi is covering larger Distance every second. This means that it is accelerating. The acceleration does not change during the Motion (the gradient stays constant). This is Motion at constant or uniform acceleration. The graphs for this situation are shown in Figure 3.9. Velocity from Acceleration vs. Time Graphs Just as we used Velocity vs. time graphs to find Displacement (vector) we can use acceleration vs. time graphs to find the Velocity of an object at given moment in time. We simply calculate the area under the acceleration vs. time graph at given time. In the graph below showing an object at constant Position (vector) acceleration the increase in Velocity of the object after seconds corresponds to the shaded portion. The Velocity of the object at is therefore 10 ms1. This corresponds with the values obtained in Figure 3.9. Important: Often you will be required to describe the Motion of an object that is presented as graph of either Position (vector) Velocity or acceleration as functions of time. The description of the Motion represented by graph should include the following (where possible): 1. whether the object is moving in the Position (vector) or negative Direction (geometry) 2. whether the object is at rest moving at constant Velocity or moving at constant Position (vector) acceleration Speed up) or constant negative acceleration (slowing down) You will also often be required to draw graphs based on description of the Motion in words or from diagram. Remember that these are just different methods of presenting the same information. If you keep in mind the general shapes of the graphs for the different types of Motion there should not be any difficulty with explaining what is happening. 
---------------------------------------------------------

3.8|Worked Examples|49

---------------------------------------------------------

3.9|Equations of Motion|54
In this chapter we will look at the third way to describe Motion We have looked at describing Motion in terms of graphs and words. In this section we examine equations that can be used to describe Motion This section is about solving problems relating to uniformly accelerated Motion In other words Motion at constant acceleration. The questions can vary lot but the following method for answering them will always Work (physics) Use this when attempting question that involves Motion with constant acceleration. You need any three known quantities (vi vf or a) to be able to calculate the fourth one. 1. Read the question carefully to identify the quantities that are given. Write them down. 2. Identify the equation to use. Write it down 3. Ensure that all the values are in the correct unit and fill them in your equation. 4. Calculate the answer and fill in its unit. Galileo Galilei of Pisa Italy was the first to determined the correct mathematical law for acceleration: the total Distance covered starting from rest is proportional to the square of the time. He also concluded that objects retain their Velocity unless Force often Friction acts upon them refuting the accepted Aristotelian hypothesis that objects naturally slow down and stop unless Force acts upon them. This principle was incorporated into Newton's laws of motion (1st law).

4|Gravity and Mechanical Energy|67

---------------------------------------------------------

4.1|Weight|67
Weight is the gravitational Force that the Earth exerts on any object. The weight of an objects gives you an indication of how strongly the Earth attracts that body towards its centre. Weight is calculated as follows: Weight mg where Mass of the object (in Kilogram and the acceleration due to Gravity (98 ms2) Important: Weight is sometimes abbreviated as Fg which refers to the Force of Gravity Do not use the abbreviation for weight as it refers to Work (physics) Now we have said that the value of is approximately 98 ms2on the surface of the Earth. The actual value varies slightly over the surface of the Earth. Each Planet in our Solar System has its own value for g. These values are listed as multiples of on Earth in Table 4.1 
---------------------------------------------------------

4.1.1|Differences between Mass and Weight|68
Mass is measured in Kilogram Kilogram and is the amount of matter in an object. An objects Mass does not change unless matter is added or removed from the object. The differences between Mass and weight can be summarised in the following table: Mass 1. is measure of how many molecules there are in an object. 2. is measured in Kilogram 3. is the same on any Planet 4. is Scalar (mathematics) Weight 1. is the Force with which the Earth attracts an object. 2. is measured in newtons 3. is different on different Planet 4. is Euclidean vector

4.2.1|Gravitational Fields|69
Field (physics) is region of space in which Mass experiences Force Therefore Gravitational field is region of space in which Mass experiences gravitational Force

4.2.2|Free fall|69
Important: Free fall is Motion in the Earths Gravitational field when no other Force act on the object. Free fall is the term used to describe special kind of Motion in the Earths Gravitational field Free fall is Motion in the Earths Gravitational field when no other Force act on the object. It is basically an ideal situation since in reality there is always some air Friction which slows down the Motion If metal ball and tennis ball (of the same size) were dropped from the same height both would reach the ground at the same time. It does not matter that the one ball is heavier than the other. The acceleration of an object due to Gravity is independent of the Mass of the object. It does not matter what the Mass of the object is. The shape of the object however is important. The sheet of paper took much longer to reach the ground than the tennis ball. This is because the effect of air Friction on the paper was much greater than the air Friction on the tennis ball. If we lived in world where there was no air Electrical Electrical resistance and conductance and conductance the A4 sheet of paper and the tennis ball would reach the ground at the same time. This happens in outer space or in vaccuum. Galileo Galilei an Italian scientist studied the Motion of objects. The following case study will tell you more about one of his investigations.

4.3|Potential Energy|73
The Potential energy of an object is generally defined as the Energy an object has because of its Position (vector) relative to other objects that it interacts with. There are different kinds of Potential energy such as gravitional Potential energy chemical Potential energy Electric potential energy to name few. In this section we will be looking at gravitational Potential energy Definition: Potential energy Potential energy is the Energy an object has due to its Position (vector) or state. Gravitational Potential energy is the Energy of an object due to its Position (vector) above the surface of the Earth. The symbol PE is used to refer to gravitational Potential energy You will often find that the words Potential energy are used where gravitational Potential energy is meant. We can define Potential energy (or gravitational Potential energy if you like) as: PE mgh (4.5) where PE Potential energy measured in Joule (J) Mass of the object (measured in Kilogram gravitational acceleration (98 ms2) perpendicular height from the reference point (measured in m) suitcase with Mass of Kilogram is placed at the top of high cupboard. By lifting the suitcase against the Force of Gravity we give the suitcase Potential energy This Potential energy can be calculated using equation 4.5. If the suitcase falls off the cupboard it will lose its Potential energy Halfway down the cupboard the suitcase will have lost half its Potential energy and will have only 98 left. At the bottom of the cupboard the suitcase will have lost all its Potential energy and its Potential energy will be equal to zero. Objects have maximum Potential energy at maximum height and will lose their Potential energy as they fall. 
---------------------------------------------------------

4.4|Kinetic Energy|75
Definition: Kinetic Energy Kinetic Energy is the Energy an object has due to its Motion Kinetic Energy is the Energy an object has because of its Motion This means that any moving object has kinetic Energy The faster it moves the more kinetic Energy it has. Kinetic Energy Kinetic energy is therefore dependent on the Velocity of the object. The Mass of the object also plays role. truck of 2000 Kilogram moving at 100 kmhr1 will have more kinetic Energy than car of 500 Kilogram also moving at 100 kmhr1. Kinetic Energy is defined as: Consider the Kilogram suitcase on the cupboard that was discussed earlier. When the suitcase falls it will gain Velocity (fall faster) until it reaches the ground with maximum Velocity The suitcase will not have any kinetic Energy when it is on top of the cupboard because it is not moving. Once it starts to fall it will gain kinetic Energy because it gains Velocity Its kinetic Energy will increase until it is maximum when the suitcase reaches the ground. 
---------------------------------------------------------

According to the equation for kinetic Energy the unit should be kgm2 s2. We can prove that this unit is equal to the Joule the unit for Energy

4.5|Mechanical Energy|78
Important: Mechanical energy is the sum of the gravitational Potential energy and the kinetic Energy Mechanical energy is simply the sum of gravitational Potential energy (PE) and the kinetic Energy Kinetic energy Mechanical energy is defined as: 
---------------------------------------------------------

4.5.1|Conservation of Mechanical Energy|78
The Law of Conservation of Energy level Energy cannot be created or destroyed but is merely changed from one form into another. Definition: Conservation of Energy The Law of Conservation of Energy Energy cannot be created or destroyed but is merely changed from one form into another. So far we have looked at two types of Energy gravitational Potential energy and kinetic Energy The sum of the gravitational Potential energy and kinetic Energy is called the Mechanical energy In closed system one where there are no external Force acting the Mechanical energy will remain constant. In other words it will not change (become more or less). This is called the Law of Conservation of Mechanical energy and it states: The total amount of Mechanical energy in closed system remains constant. Definition: Conservation of Mechanical energy Law of Conservation of Mechanical energy The total amount of Mechanical energy in closed system remains constant. This means that Potential energy can become kinetic Energy or vise versa but Energy cannot dissappear. The Mechanical energy of an object moving in the Earths Gravitational field (or accelerating as result of Gravity is constant or conserved unless external Force like air Electrical Electrical resistance and conductance and conductance acts on the object. We can now use the conservation of Mechanical energy to calculate the Velocity of body in Free fall and show that the Velocity is independent of Mass Important: In problems involving the use of conservation of Energy the path taken by the object can be ignored. The only important quantities are the objects Velocity (which gives its kinetic Energy and height above the reference point (which gives its gravitational Potential energy Important: In the absence of Friction Mechanical energy is conserved and Ubefore Uafter In the presence of Friction Mechanical energy is not conserved. The Mechanical energy lost is equal to the Work (physics) done against Friction Ubefore Uafter Work (physics) done against Friction In general Mechanical energy is conserved in the absence of external Force Examples of external Force are: applied Force Friction Force air Electrical Electrical resistance and conductance and conductance Tension (physics) normal Force In the presence of internal Force like the Force due to Gravity or the Force in spring Mechanical energy is conserved. 
---------------------------------------------------------

4.5.2|Using the Law of Conservation of Energy|79
Mechanical energy is conserved (in the absence of Friction Therefore we can say that the sum of the PE and the Kinetic energy anywhere during the Motion must be equal to the sum of the PE and the Kinetic energy anywhere else in the Motion We can now apply this to the example of the suitcase on the cupboard. Consider the Mechanical energy of the suitcase at the top and at the bottom. We can say: The suitcase will strike the ground with Velocity of 626 ms1. From this we see that when an object is lifted like the suitcase in our example it gains Potential energy As it falls back to the ground it will lose this Potential energy but gain kinetic en- ergy. We know that Energy cannot be created or destroyed but only changed from one form into another. In our example the Potential energy that the suitcase loses is changed to kinetic Energy The suitcase will have maximum Potential energy at the top of the cupboard and maximum kinetic Energy at the bottom of the cupboard. Halfway down it will have half kinetic Energy and half Potential energy As it moves down the Potential energy will be converted (changed) into kinetic Energy until all the Potential energy is gone and only kinetic Energy is left. The 196 of Potential energy at the top will become 196 of kinetic Energy at the bottom. 
---------------------------------------------------------

4.6|Energy graphs|82

We will look at how each quantity changes as the suitcase falls from the top to the bottom of the cupboard. Potential energy The Potential energy starts off at maximum and decreases until it reaches zero at the bottom of the cupboard. It had fallen Distance of Metre Kinetic Energy The kinetic Energy is zero at the start of the fall. When the suitcase reaches the ground the kinetic Energy is miximum. We also use Distance on the x-axis. Mechanical energy The Mechanical energy is constant throughout the Motion and is always maximum. At any point in time when we add the Potential energy and the kinetic Energy we will get the same number.

5.1|Introduction|87
This chapter forms the basis of the discussion into mechanical Wave Wave are all around us even though most of us are not aware of it. The most common Wave are Wave in the sea but Wave can be created in any container of water ranging from an ocean to tea-cup. Simply Wave is moving Energy 
---------------------------------------------------------

5.2|What is a medium?|87
In this chapter as well as in the following chapters we will speak about Wave moving in medium. medium is just the substance or material through which Wave move. In other words the medium carries the Wave from one place to another. The medium does not create the Wave and the medium is not the Wave Air is medium for Sound Wave water is medium for water Wave and rock is medium for earthquakes (which are also type of Wave Air water and rock are therefore examples of media (media is the plural of medium). Definition: Medium medium is the substance or material in which Wave will move. In each medium the atoms that make up the medium are moved temporarily from their rest Position (vector) In order for Wave to travel the different parts of the medium must be able to interact with each other. 
---------------------------------------------------------

5.3|What is a pulse?|87
In the activity we created pulse. pulse is single disturbance that moves through medium. transverse pulse moves perpendicular to the medium. Figure 5.1 shows an example of transverse pulse. In the activity the rope or spring was held horizontally and the pulse moved the rope up and down. This was an example of transverse pulse. Definition: Pulse pulse is single disturbance that moves through medium. 
---------------------------------------------------------

5.3.1|Pulse Length and Amplitude|88
The Amplitude of pulse is measurement of how far the medium is Displacement (vector) from Position (vector) of rest. The pulse Length is measurement of how long the pulse is. Both these quantities are shown in Figure 5.1. Definition: Amplitude The Amplitude of pulse is measurement of how far the medium is Displacement (vector) from rest. In the activity we found that the values for how high the pulse (a) is and how wide the pulse (p) is the same at different times. Pulse Length and Amplitude are two important quantities of pulse. 
---------------------------------------------------------

5.3.2|Pulse Speed|89
Definition: Pulse Speed Pulse Speed is the Distance pulse travels in specific time. In Chapter we saw that Speed was defined as the Distance travelled in specified time. We can use the same definition of Speed to calculate how fast pulse travels. If the pulse travels Distance in time then the pulse Speed is: Important: The pulse Speed depends on the properties of the medium and not on the Amplitude or pulse Length of the pulse. 
---------------------------------------------------------

5.4|Graphs of Position and Velocity|90
When pulse moves through medium there are two different Motion the Motion of the particles of the medium and the Motion of the pulse. These two Motion are at right angles to each other when the pulse is transverse. Each Motion will be discussed. Consider the situation shown in Figure ??. The dot represents one particle of the medium. We see that as the pulse moves to the right the particle only moves up and down. 
---------------------------------------------------------

5.4.1|Motion of a Particle of the Medium|90
First we consider the Motion of particle of the medium when pulse moves through the medium. For the explanation we will zoom into the medium so that we are looking at the atoms of the medium. These atoms are connected to each other as shown in Figure 5.2. When pulse moves through the medium the particles in the medium only move up and down. We can see this in the figure below which shows the Motion of single particle as pulse moves through the medium. Important: particle in the medium only moves up and down when transverse pulse moves through the medium. The pulse moves from left to right (or right to left). The Motion of the particle is perpendicular to the Motion of transverse pulse. If you consider the Motion of the particle as function of time you can draw graph of Position (vector) vs. time and Velocity vs. time. 
---------------------------------------------------------

5.4.2|Motion of the Pulse|92
The Motion of the pulse is much simpler than the Motion of particle in the medium. Important: point on transverse pulse eg. the peak only moves in the Direction (geometry) of the Motion of the pulse. 
---------------------------------------------------------

5.5|Transmission and Reflection of a Pulse at a Boundary|96
What happens when pulse travelling in one medium finds that medium is joined to another? When pulse meets boundary between two media part of the pulse is Reflection (physics) and part of it is transmitted. You will see that in the thin rope the pulse moves back (is Reflection (physics) The pulse is also passed on (transmitted) to the thick rope and it moves away from the boundary. When pulse is transmitted from one medium to another like from thin rope to thicker one the pulse will change where it meets the boundary of the two mediums (for example where the ropes are joined). When pulse moves from thin rope to thicker one the Speed of the pulse will decrease. The pulse will move slower and the pulse Length will increase. When pulse moves from thick rope to thinner one the opposite happens. The pulse Speed will increase and the pulse Length will decrease. When the Speed of the pulse increases the pulse Length will decrease. If the Speed decreases the pulse Length will increase. The incident pulse is the one that arrives at the boundary. The Reflection (physics) pulse is the one that moves back away from the boundary. The transmitted pulse is the one that moves into the new medium away from the boundary. 
---------------------------------------------------------

5.6|Reflection of a Pulse from Fixed and Free Ends|97
Let us now consider what happens to pulse when it reaches the end of medium. The medium can be fixed like rope tied to wall or it can be free like rope tied loosely to pole. 
---------------------------------------------------------

5.6.1|Reflection of a Pulse from a Fixed End|97
When the end of the medium is fixed for example rope tied to wall pulse Reflection (physics) from the fixed end but the pulse is inverted (i.e. it is upside-down). This is shown in Figure 5.9. 
---------------------------------------------------------

5.6.2|Reflection of a Pulse from a Free End|98
When the end of the medium is free for example rope tied loosely to pole pulse Reflection (physics) from the free end but the pulse is not inverted. This is shown in Figure 5.10. We draw the free end as ring around the pole. The ring will move up and down the pole while the pulse is Reflection (physics) away from the pole. Important: The fixed and free ends that were discussed in this section are examples of boundary conditions. You will see more of boundary conditions as you progress in the Physics syllabus. 
---------------------------------------------------------

5.7|Superposition of Pulses|99
Two or more pulses can pass through the same medium at that same time. The resulting pulse is obtained by using the principle of superposition. The principle of superposition states that the effect of the pulses is the sum of their individual effects. After pulses pass through each other each pulse continues along its original Direction (geometry) of travel and their original Amplitude remain unchanged. Constructive interference takes place when two pulses meet each other to create larger pulse. The Amplitude of the resulting pulse is the sum of the Amplitude of the two initial pulses. This is shown in Figure 5.11. Definition: Constructive interference is when two pulses meet resulting in bigger pulse. Destructive interference takes place when two pulses meet and cancel each other. The Amplitude of the resulting pulse is the sum of the Amplitude of the two initial pulses but the one Amplitude will be negative number. This is shown in Figure 5.12. In general Amplitude of individual pulses add together to give the Amplitude of the resultant pulse. Definition: Destructive interference is when two pulses meet resulting in smaller pulse.

6|Transverse Waves|105

---------------------------------------------------------

6.1|Introduction|105
Wave occur frequently in nature. The most obvious examples are Wave in water on dam in the ocean or in bucket. We are most interested in the properties that Wave have. All Wave have the same properties so if we study Wave in water then we can transfer our knowledge to predict how other examples of Wave will behave. 
---------------------------------------------------------

6.2|What is a transverse wave?|105
We have studied pulses in Chapter and know that pulse is single disturbance that travels through medium. Wave is periodic continuous disturbance that consists of train of pulses. Definition: Wave Wave is periodic continuous disturbance that consists of train of pulses. Definition: Transverse Wave transverse Wave is Wave where the movement of the particles of the medium is perpen- dicular to the Direction (geometry) of propagation of the Wave In the Activity you have created Wave The medium through which these Wave propagated was the rope which is obviously made up of very large number of particles (atoms). From the activity you would have noticed that the Wave travelled from left to right but the particles (the ribbon) moved only up and down. When the particles of medium move at right angles to the Direction (geometry) of propagation of Wave the Wave is called transverse. For Wave there is no net Displacement (vector) of the particles (they return to their equilibrium Position (vector) but there is net Displacement (vector) of the Wave There are thus two different Motion the Motion of the particles of the medium and the Motion of the Wave 
---------------------------------------------------------

6.2.1|Peaks and Troughs|106
Wave consist of moving peaks (or Crest (physics) and Crest (physics) peak is the highest point the medium rises to and Crest (physics) is the lowest point the medium sinks to. Peaks and Crest (physics) on transverse Wave are shown in Figure 6.2. Definition: Peaks and Crest (physics) peak is point on the Wave where the Displacement (vector) of the medium is at maximum. point on the Wave is Crest (physics) if the Displacement (vector) of the medium at that point is at minimum. 
---------------------------------------------------------

6.2.2|Amplitude and Wavelength|107
There are few properties that we saw with pulses that also apply to Wave These are Amplitude and Wavelength (we called this pulse Length Definition: Amplitude The Amplitude is the maximum Displacement (vector) of particle from its equilibrium Position (vector) As we have seen in the activity on Amplitude the Distance between the peak and the equilibrium Position (vector) is equal to the Distance between the Crest (physics) and the equilibrium Position (vector) This Distance is known as the Amplitude of the Wave and is the characteristic height of Wave above or below the equilibrium Position (vector) Normally the symbol is used to represent the Amplitude of Wave The SI unit of Amplitude is the Metre (m). As we have seen in the activity on Wavelength the Distance between two adjacent peaks is the same no matter which two adjacent peaks you choose. There is fixed Distance between the peaks. Similarly we have seen that there is fixed Distance between the Crest (physics) no matter which two Crest (physics) you look at. More importantly the Distance between two adjacent peaks is the same as the Distance between two adjacent Crest (physics) This Distance is call the Wavelength of the Wave The symbol for the Wavelength is (the Greek letter lambda) and Wavelength is measured in Metre (m). 
---------------------------------------------------------

6.2.3|Points in Phase|109
In the activity the Distance between the indicated points was the same. These points are then said to be in phase. Two points in phase are separate by an integer (0123...) number of complete Wave cycles. They do not have to be peaks or Crest (physics) but they must be separated by complete number of Wavelength We then have an alternate definition of the Wavelength as the Distance between any two adjacent points which are in phase. Definition: Wavelength of Wave The Wavelength of Wave is the Distance between any two adjacent points that are in phase. Points that are not in phase those that are not separated by complete number of Wavelength are called out of phase. Examples of points like these would be and or and or and in the Activity. 
---------------------------------------------------------

6.2.4|Period and Frequency|110
Imagine you are sitting next to pond and you watch the Wave going past you. First one peak arrives then Crest (physics) and then another peak. Suppose you measure the time taken between one peak arriving and then the next. This time will be the same for any two successive peaks passing you. We call this time the period and it is characteristic of the Wave The symbol is used to represent the period. The period is measured in seconds (s). Definition: The period (T) is the time taken for two successive peaks (or Crest (physics) to pass fixed point. Imagine the pond again. Just as peak passes you you start your stopwatch and count each peak going past. After second you stop the clock and stop counting. The number of peaks that you have counted in the second is the Frequency of the Wave Definition: The Frequency is the number of successive peaks (or Crest (physics) passing given point in second. The Frequency and the period are related to each other. As the period is the time taken for peak to pass then the number of peaks passing the point in second is But this is the Frequency So

6.4.2|Reflection of a Transverse Wave from a Free End|118

6.4.3|Standing Waves|118
What happens when Reflection (physics) transverse Wave meets an incident transverse Wave When two Wave move in opposite Direction (geometry) through each other interference takes place. If the two Wave have the same Frequency and Wavelength then Standing wave are generated. Standing wave are so-called because they appear to be standing still. We can now look closely how Standing wave are formed. Figure 6.5 shows Reflection (physics) Wave meeting an incident Wave When they touch both Wave have an Amplitude of zero: Figure 6.6: Reflection (physics) Wave (solid line) meets the incident Wave (dashed line). If we wait for short time the ends of the two Wave move past each other and the Wave overlap. To find the resultant Wave we add the two together. Figure 6.7: Reflection (physics) Wave (solid line) overlaps slightly with the incident Wave (dashed line). In this picture we show the two Wave as dotted lines and the sum of the two in the overlap region is shown as solid line: The important thing to note in this case is that there are some points where the two Wave always destructively interfere to zero. If we let the two Wave move little further we get the picture below: Again we have to add the two Wave together in the overlap region to see what the sum of the Wave looks like. In this case the two Wave have moved half cycle past each other but because they are out of phase they cancel out completely. When the Wave have moved past each other so that they are overlapping for large region the situation looks like Wave oscillating in place. The following sequence of diagrams show what the resulting Wave will look like. To make it clearer the arrows at the top of the picture show peaks where maximum Position (vector) constructive interference is taking place. The arrows at the bottom of the picture show places where maximum negative interference is taking place. As time goes by the peaks become smaller and the Crest (physics) become shallower but they do not move. For an instant the entire region will look completely flat. The various points continue their Motion in the same manner. Eventually the picture looks like the complete Reflection (physics) through the x-axis of what we started with: Then all the points begin to move back. Each point on the line is oscillating up and down with different Amplitude If we look at the overall result we get Standing wave Figure 6.8: Standing wave If we superimpose the two cases where the peaks were at maximum and the case where the same Wave were at minimum we can see the lines that the points oscillate between. We call this the envelope of the Standing wave as it contains all the oscillations of the individual points. To make the concept of the envelope clearer let us draw arrows describing the Motion of points along the line. Every point in the medium containing Standing wave oscillates up and down and the Amplitude of the oscillations depends on the location of the point. It is convenient to draw the envelope for the oscillations to describe the Motion We cannot draw the up and down arrows for every single point Interesting Fact Standing wave can be problem in for example indoor concerts where the dimensions of the concert venue coincide with particular Wavelength Standing wave can appear as feedback which would occur if the Standing wave was picked up by the microphones on stage and amplified.

6.4.4|Nodes and anti-nodes|122
node is point on Wave where no Displacement (vector) takes place. In Standing wave node is place where the two Wave cancel out completely as two Wave destructively interfere in the same place. fixed end of rope is node. An anti-node is point on Wave where maximum Displacement (vector) takes place. In Standing wave an anti-node is place where the two Wave constructively interfere. free end of rope is an anti-node. Definition: Node node is point on Wave where no Displacement (vector) takes place. In Standing wave node is place where the two Wave cancel out completely as two Wave destructively interfere in the same place. fixed end of rope is node. Definition: Anti-Node An anti-node is point on Wave where maximum Displacement (vector) takes place. In Standing wave an anti-node is place where the two Wave constructively interfere. free end of rope is an anti-node. Important: The Distance between two anti-nodes is only because it is the Distance from peak to Crest (physics) in one of the Wave forming the Standing wave It is the same as the Distance between two adjacent nodes. This will be important when we Work (physics) out the allowed Wavelength in tubes later. We can take this further because half-way between any two anti-nodes is node. Then the Distance from the node to the anti-node is half the Distance between two anti-nodes. This is half of half Wavelength which is one quarter of Wavelength

6.4.5|Wavelengths of Standing Waves with Fixed and Free Ends|122

The key is to understand that Standing wave must be created in the medium that is oscillating. There are restrictions as to what Wavelength can form Standing wave in medium. For example if we consider rope that can move in pipe such that it can have both ends free to move (Case 1) one end free and one end fixed (Case 2) both ends fixed (Case 3). Each of these cases is slightly different because the free or fixed end determines whether node or anti-node will form when Standing wave is created in the rope. These are the main restrictions when we determine the Wavelength of potential Standing wave These restrictions are known as boundary conditions and must be met. We can have two nodes inside the tube only if we have one anti-node contained inside the tube and one on each end. This means we have anti-nodes in the tube. The Distance between any two anti-nodes is half Wavelength This means there is half Wavelength between the left side and the middle and another half Wavelength between the middle and the right side so there must be one Wavelength inside the tube. The safest thing to do is Work (physics) out how many half Wavelength there are and equate this to the Length of the tube and then solve for Case 2: We want to have two nodes inside the tube. The left end must be node and the right end must be an anti-node. We can have one node inside the tube as drawn above. Again we can count the number of Distance between adjacent nodes or anti-nodes. If we start from the left end we have one half Wavelength between the end and the node inside the tube. So it is half of half Wavelength Together these add up to the Length of the tube: Case 3: In this case both ends have to be nodes. The Distance between any two anti-nodes is half Wavelength This means there is half Wavelength between every adjacent pair of anti-nodes. We count how many gaps there are between adjacent anti-nodes to determine how many half Wavelength there are and equate this to the Length of the tube and then solve for we can count the number of Distance between adjacent nodes or anti-nodes together these add up to the Length of the tube. 6.4.6|Superposition and Interference|125
If two Wave meet interesting things can happen. Wave are basically collective Motion of particles. So when two Wave meet they both try to impose their collective Motion on the particles. This can have quite different results. If two identical (same Wavelength Amplitude and Frequency Wave are both trying to form peak then they are able to achieve the sum of their efforts. The resulting Motion will be peak which has height which is the sum of the heights of the two Wave If two Wave are both trying to form Crest (physics) in the same place then deeper Crest (physics) is formed the depth of which is the sum of the depths of the two Wave Now in this case the two Wave have been trying to do the same thing and so add together constructively. This is called constructive interference. If one Wave is trying to form peak and the other is trying to form Crest (physics) then they are competing to do different things. In this case they can cancel out. The Amplitude of the resulting Wave will depend on the Amplitude of the two Wave that are Interference (wave propagation) If the depth of the Crest (physics) is the same as the height of the peak nothing will happen. If the height of the peak is bigger than the depth of the Crest (physics) smaller peak will appear. And if the Crest (physics) is deeper then less deep Crest (physics) will appear. This is destructive interference. 
---------------------------------------------------------

7.1|Introduction|129
You are indoors on sunny day. beam of sunlight through window Light up section of the floor. How would you draw this sunbeam? You might draw series of parallel lines showing the path of the sunlight from the window to the floor. This is not exactly accurate no matter how Hardness you look you will not find unique lines of Light in the sunbeam However this is good way to draw Light It is also good way to model Light geometrically as we will see in this chapter. We will call these narrow imaginary lines of Light Light Ray (optics) Since Light is an electromagnetic Wave you could think of Light Ray (optics) as the path of point on the Crest (physics) of Wave Or you could think of Light Ray (optics) as the path taken by miniscule particle that carries Light We will always draw them the same way: as straight lines between objects images and Geometrical optics devices. We can use Light Ray (optics) to model mirrors lenses Telescope Optical microscope and prisms. The study of how Light interacts with materials is Geometrical optics When dealing with Light Ray (optics) we are usually interested in the shape of material and the angles at which Light Ray (optics) hit it. From these angles we can Work (physics) out for example the Distance between an object and its Reflection (physics) We therefore refer to this kind of Geometrical optics as Geometrical optics

7.2|Light Rays|129

The most important thing to remember is that we can only see an object when Light from the object enters our eyes. The object must be source of Light (for example Light bulb) or else it must Reflection (physics) Light from source (for example the moon) and the Reflection (physics) Light enters our eyes. Important: We cannot see an object unless Light from that object enters our eyes. Definition: Light Ray (optics) Light Ray (optics) are straight lines with arrows to show the path of Light Important: Light Ray (optics) are not real. They are merely used to show the path that Light travels.

7.3|Reflection|132
When you smile into mirror you see your own face smiling back at you. This is caused by the Reflection (physics) of Light Ray (optics) on the Mirror image occurs when Light Ray (optics) bounces off surface.

7.3.1|Terminology|133
In Chapters and we saw that when pulse or Wave strikes surface it is Reflection (physics) This means that Wave bounce off things. Sound Wave bounce off walls Light Wave bounce off mirrors radar Wave bounce off aeroplanes and it can explain how bats can fly at night and avoid things as thin as telephone wires. The phenomenon of Reflection (physics) is very important and useful one. We will use the following terminology. The incoming Light Ray (optics) is called the incident Ray (optics) The Light Ray (optics) moving away from the surface is the Reflection (physics) Ray (optics) The most important characteristic of these Ray (optics) is their angles in relation to the Reflection (physics) surface. These angles are measured with respect to the normal of the surface. The normal is an imaginary line perpendicular to the surface. The Angle of incidence is measured between the incident Ray (optics) and the surface normal. The angle of Reflection (physics) is measured between the Reflection (physics) Ray (optics) and the surface normal. This is shown in Figure 7.4. When Ray (optics) of Light is Reflection (physics) the Reflection (physics) Ray (optics) lies in the same plane as the incident Ray (optics) and the normal. This plane is called the plane of incidence and is shown in Figure 7.5. 
---------------------------------------------------------

7.3.2|Law of Reflection|133
Specular reflection states that the Angle of incidence and Reflection (physics) are always equal and that the Reflection (physics) Ray (optics) always lies in the plane of incidence. Definition: Specular reflection Specular reflection states that the Angle of incidence is equal to the angle of Reflection (physics) The simplest example of the law of incidence is if the Angle of incidence is 0. In this case the angle of Reflection (physics) is also 0. You see this when you look straight into mirror. Figure 7.6: When Wave strikes surface at right angles to the surface then the Wave is Reflection (physics) directly back. If the Angle of incidence is not then the angle of Reflection (physics) is also not 0. For example if Light strikes surface at 60 to the surface normal then the angle that the Reflection (physics) Ray (optics) makes with the surface normal is also 60 as shown in Figure 7.7. Figure 7.7: Ray (optics) diagram showing Angle of incidence and angle of Reflection (physics) Specular reflection states that when Light Ray (optics) Reflection (physics) off surface the angle of Reflection (physics) is the same as the Angle of incidence i. 
---------------------------------------------------------

7.3.3|Types of Reflection|135
Specular reflection is true for any surface. Does this mean that when parallel Ray (optics) approach surface the Reflection (physics) Ray (optics) will also be parallel? This depends on the texture of the Reflection (physics) surface. Specular reflection Reflection (physics) Figure 7.8(a) shows surface that is flat and even. Parallel incident Light Ray (optics) hit the smooth surface and parallel Reflection (physics) Light Ray (optics) leave the surface. This type of Reflection (physics) is called Specular reflection Reflection (physics) Specular reflection Reflection (physics) occurs when Ray (optics) are Reflection (physics) from smooth shiny surface. The normal to the surface is the same at every point on the surface. Parallel incident Ray (optics) become parallel Reflection (physics) Ray (optics) When you look in mirror the image you see is formed by Specular reflection Reflection (physics) Diffuse Reflection (physics) Figure 7.8(b) shows surface with bumps and curves. When multiple Ray (optics) hit this uneven surface diffuse Reflection (physics) occurs. The incident Ray (optics) are parallel but the Reflection (physics) Ray (optics) are not. Each point on the surface has different normal. This means the Angle of incidence is different at each point. Then according to Specular reflection each angle of Reflection (physics) is different. Diffuse Reflection (physics) occurs when Light Ray (optics) are Reflection (physics) from bumpy surfaces. You can still see Reflection (physics) as long as the surface is not too bumpy. Diffuse Reflection (physics) enables us to see all objects that are not sources of Light
