11|Vectors|211

---------------------------------------------------------

11.1|Introduction|211
This chapter focuses on Euclidean vector We will learn what is Euclidean vector how it differs from everyday numbers how to add subtract and multiply them and where they appear in Physics Are Euclidean vector Physics No Euclidean vector themselves are not Physics Physics is just description of the world around us. To describe something we need to use language. The most common language used to describe Physics is Mathematics. Euclidean vector form very important part of the mathematical description of Physics so much so that it is absolutely essential to master the use of Euclidean vector 
---------------------------------------------------------

11.2|Scalars and Vectors|211
In Mathematics you learned that number is something that represents quantity. For example if you have books apples and bicycle the and represent how many of each item you have. These kinds of numbers are known as Scalar (mathematics) Definition: Scalar (mathematics) Scalar (mathematics) is quantity that has only magnitude (size). An extension to Scalar (mathematics) is Euclidean vector which is Scalar (mathematics) with Direction (geometry) For example if you travel km down Main Road to school the quantity km down Main Road is Euclidean vector The km is the quantity (or Scalar (mathematics) and the down Main Road gives Direction (geometry) In Physics we use the word magnitude to refer to the Scalar (mathematics) part of the Euclidean vector Definition: Euclidean vector Euclidean vector is quantity that has both magnitude and Direction (geometry) Euclidean vector should tell you how much and which way. For example man is driving his car east along freeway at 100 kmhr1. What we have given here is Euclidean vector the Velocity The car is moving at 100 kmhr1(this is the magnitude) and we know where it is going east (this is the Direction (geometry) Thus we know the Speed and Direction (geometry) of the car. These two quantities magnitude and Direction (geometry) form Euclidean vector we call Velocity 
---------------------------------------------------------

11.3|Notation|211
Euclidean vector are different to Scalar (mathematics) and therefore has its own notation. 
---------------------------------------------------------

11.3.1|Mathematical Representation|212
There are many ways of writing the symbol for Euclidean vector Euclidean vector are denoted by symbols with an arrow pointing to the right above it. For example and represent the Euclidean vector acceleration Velocity and Force meaning they have both magnitude and Direction (geometry) Sometimes just the magnitude of Euclidean vector is needed. In this case the arrow is omitted. In other words denotes the magnitude of Euclidean vector F. F| is another way of representing the magnitude of Euclidean vector 
---------------------------------------------------------

11.3.2|Graphical Representation|212
Euclidean vector are drawn as arrows. An arrow has both magnitude (how long it is) and Direction (geometry) (the Direction (geometry) in which it points). The starting point of Euclidean vector is known as the tail and the end point is known as the head. 
---------------------------------------------------------

11.4|Directions|212
There are many acceptable methods of writing Euclidean vector As long as the Euclidean vector has magnitude and Direction (geometry) it is most likely acceptable. These different methods come from the different methods of expressing Direction (geometry) for Euclidean vector 
---------------------------------------------------------

11.4.1|Relative Directions|212
The simplest method of expressing Direction (geometry) is relative Direction (geometry) to the left to the right forward backward up and down. 
---------------------------------------------------------

11.4.2|Compass Directions|213
Another common method of expressing direc- tions is to use the points of Compass North South East and West. If Euclidean vector does not point exactly in one of the Compass Direction (geometry) then we use an angle. For example we can have Euclidean vector pointing 40 North of West. Start with the Euclidean vector pointing along the West Direction (geometry) Then rotate the Euclidean vector towards the north until there is 40 angle between the Euclidean vector and the West. The Direction (geometry) of this Euclidean vector can also be described as: 40 (West 40 North) or 50 (North 50 West) 
---------------------------------------------------------

11.4.3|Bearing|213
The final method of expressing Direction (geometry) is to use bearing. bearing is Direction (geometry) relative to fixed point. Given just an angle the convention is to define the angle with respect to the North. So Euclidean vector with Direction (geometry) of 110 has been rotated clockwise 110 relative to the North. bearing is always written as three digit number for example 275 or 080 (for 80). 
---------------------------------------------------------

11.5|Drawing Vectors|214
In order to draw Euclidean vector accurately we must specify scale and include reference Direction (geometry) in the diagram. scale allows us to translate the Length of the arrow into the Euclidean vector magnitude. For instance if one chose scale of cm (1 cm represents N) Force of 20 towards the East would be represented as an arrow 10 cm long. reference Direction (geometry) may be line representing horizontal surface or the points of Compass Method: Drawing Euclidean vector 1. Decide upon scale and write it down. 2. Determine the Length of the arrow representing the Euclidean vector by using the scale. 3. Draw the Euclidean vector as an arrow. Make sure that you fill in the arrow head. 4. Fill in the magnitude of the Euclidean vector 
---------------------------------------------------------

11.6|Mathematical Properties of Vectors|215
Euclidean vector are mathematical objects and we need to understand the mathematical properties of Euclidean vector like adding and subtracting. For all the examples in this section we will use Displacement (vector) as our Euclidean vector quantity. Displacement (vector) was discussed in Chapter 3. Displacement (vector) is defined as the Distance together with Direction (geometry) of the straight line joining final point to an initial point. Remember that Displacement (vector) is just one example of Euclidean vector We could just as well have decided to use Force or Velocity to illustrate the properties of Euclidean vector 
---------------------------------------------------------

11.6.1|Adding Vectors|215
When Euclidean vector are added we need to add both magnitude and Direction (geometry) For example take steps in the forward Direction (geometry) stop and then take another steps in the forward Direction (geometry) The first steps is Displacement (vector) Euclidean vector and the second steps is also Displacement (vector) Euclidean vector If we did not stop after the first steps we would have taken steps in the forward Direction (geometry) in total. Therefore if we add the Displacement (vector) Euclidean vector for steps and steps we should get total of steps in the forward Direction (geometry) Graphically this can be seen by first following the first Euclidean vector two steps forward and then following the second one three steps forward: We add the second Euclidean vector at the end of the first Euclidean vector since this is where we now are after the first Euclidean vector has acted. The Euclidean vector from the tail of the first Euclidean vector (the starting point) to the head of the last (the end point) is then the sum of the Euclidean vector This is the head-to-tail method of Euclidean vector addition. As you can convince yourself the order in which you add Euclidean vector does not matter. In the example above if you decided to first go steps forward and then another steps forward the end result would still be steps forward. The final answer when adding Euclidean vector is called the resultant. The resultant Displacement (vector) in this case will be steps forward. Definition: Resultant of Euclidean vector The resultant of number of Euclidean vector is the single Euclidean vector whose effect is the same as the individual Euclidean vector acting together. In other words the individual Euclidean vector can be replaced by the resultant the overall effect is the same. If Euclidean vector and have resultant this can be represented mathematically as Let us consider some more examples of Euclidean vector addition using Displacement (vector) The arrows tell you how far to move and in what Direction (geometry) Arrows to the right correspond to steps forward while arrows to the left correspond to steps backward. Look at all of the examples below and check them. This example says step forward and then another step forward is the same as an arrow twice as long two steps forward. This examples says step backward and then another step backward is the same as an arrow twice as long two steps backward. It is sometimes possible that you end up back where you started. In this case the net result of what you have done is that you have gone nowhere (your start and end points are at the same place). In this case your resultant Displacement (vector) is Euclidean vector with Length zero units. We use the symbol to denote such Euclidean vector It is important to realise that the Direction (geometry) are not special forward and backwards or left and right are treated in the same way. The same is true of any set of parallel Direction (geometry) In the above examples the separate Displacement (vector) were parallel to one another. However the same head-to-tail technique of Euclidean vector addition can be applied to Euclidean vector in any Direction (geometry) Now you have discovered one use for Euclidean vector describing resultant Displacement (vector) how far and in what Direction (geometry) you have travelled after series of movements. Although Euclidean vector addition here has been demonstrated with Displacement (vector) all Euclidean vector behave in exactly the same way. Thus if given number of Force acting on body you can use the same method to determine the resultant Force acting on the body. We will return to Euclidean vector addition in more detail later. 
---------------------------------------------------------

11.6.2|Subtracting Vectors|217
What does it mean to subtract Euclidean vector Well this is really simple if we have apples and we subtract apples we have only apples left. Now lets Work (physics) in steps if we take steps forward and then subtract steps forward we are left with only two steps forward: What have we done? You originally took steps forward but then you took steps back. That backward Displacement (vector) would be represented by an arrow pointing to the left (backwards) with Length 3. The net result of adding these two Euclidean vector is steps forward: Thus subtracting Euclidean vector from another is the same as adding Euclidean vector in the opposite Direction (geometry) (i.e. subtracting steps forwards is the same as adding steps backwards). Important: Subtracting Euclidean vector from another is the same as adding Euclidean vector in the opposite Direction (geometry) This suggests that in this problem to the right was chosen as the Position (vector) Direction (geometry) Arrows to the right are Position (vector) and arrows to the left are negative. More generally Euclidean vector in opposite Direction (geometry) differ in sign (i.e. if we define up as Position (vector) then Euclidean vector acting down are negative). Thus changing the sign of Euclidean vector simply reverses its Direction (geometry) 
---------------------------------------------------------

11.6.3|Scalar Multiplication|218
What happens when you multiply Euclidean vector by Scalar (mathematics) (an ordinary number)? Going back to normal multiplication we know that is just groups of added together to give 4. We can adopt similar approach to understand how Euclidean vector multiplication Work (physics) 
---------------------------------------------------------

11.7|Techniques of Vector Addition|218
Now that you have learned about the mathematical properties of Euclidean vector we return to Euclidean vector addition in more detail. There are number of techniques of Euclidean vector addition. These techniques fall into two main categories graphical and algebraic techniques. 
---------------------------------------------------------

11.7.1|Graphical Techniques|218
Graphical techniques involve drawing accurate scale diagrams to denote individual Euclidean vector and their resultants. We next discuss the two primary graphical techniques the head-to-tail technique and the parallelogram method. The Head-to-Tail Method In describing the mathematical properties of Euclidean vector we used Displacement (vector) and the head-to-tail graphical method of Euclidean vector addition as an illustration. The head-to-tail method of graphically adding Euclidean vector is standard method that must be understood. Method: Head-to-Tail Method of Euclidean vector Addition 1. Choose scale and include reference Direction (geometry) 2. Choose any of the Euclidean vector and draw it as an arrow in the correct Direction (geometry) and of the correct Length remember to put an arrowhead on the end to denote its Direction (geometry) 3. Take the next Euclidean vector and draw it as an arrow starting from the arrowhead of the first Euclidean vector in the correct Direction (geometry) and of the correct Length 4. Continue until you have drawn each Euclidean vector each time starting from the head of the previous Euclidean vector In this way the Euclidean vector to be added are drawn one after the other head- to-tail. 5. The resultant is then the Euclidean vector drawn from the tail of the first Euclidean vector to the head of the last. Its magnitude can be determined from the Length of its arrow using the scale. Its Direction (geometry) too can be determined from the scale diagram. The Parallelogram Method The parallelogram method is another graphical technique of finding the resultant of two Euclidean vector Method: The Parallelogram Method 1. Choose scale and reference Direction (geometry) 2. Choose either of the Euclidean vector to be added and draw it as an arrow of the correct Length in the correct Direction (geometry) 3. Draw the second Euclidean vector as an arrow of the correct Length in the correct Direction (geometry) from the tail of the first Euclidean vector 4. Complete the parallelogram formed by these two Euclidean vector 5. The resultant is then the diagonal of the parallelogram. The magnitude can be determined from the Length of its arrow using the scale. The Direction (geometry) too can be determined from the scale diagram. The parallelogram method is restricted to the addition of just two Euclidean vector However it is arguably the most intuitive way of adding two Force acting at point. 
---------------------------------------------------------

11.7.2|Algebraic Addition and Subtraction of Vectors|223
Euclidean vector in Straight Line Whenever you are faced with adding Euclidean vector acting in straight line (i.e. some Direction (geometry) left and some right or some acting up and others down) you can use very simple algebraic technique: Method: AdditionSubtraction of Euclidean vector in Straight Line 1. Choose Position (vector) Direction (geometry) As an example for situations involving Displacement (vector) in the Direction (geometry) west and east you might choose west as your Position (vector) Direction (geometry) In that case Displacement (vector) east are negative. 2. Next simply add (or subtract) the Euclidean vector using the appropriate signs. 3. As final step the Direction (geometry) of the resultant should be included in words Position (vector) answers are in the Position (vector) Direction (geometry) while negative resultants are in the negative Direction (geometry) In the previous example we were able to use simple trigonometry to calculate the resultant Displacement (vector) This was possible since the Direction (geometry) of Motion were perpendicular (north and east). Algebraic techniques however are not limited to cases where the Euclidean vector to be combined are along the same straight line or at right angles to one another. The following example illustrates this. 
---------------------------------------------------------

11.8|Components of Vectors|228
In the discussion of Euclidean vector addition we saw that number of Euclidean vector acting together can be combined to give single Euclidean vector (the resultant). In much the same way single Euclidean vector can be broken down into number of Euclidean vector which when added give that original Euclidean vector These Euclidean vector which sum to the original are called components of the original Euclidean vector The process of breaking Euclidean vector into its components is called resolving into components. While summing given set of Euclidean vector gives just one answer (the resultant) single Euclidean vector can be resolved into infinitely many sets of components. In the diagrams below the same black Euclidean vector is resolved into different pairs of components. These components are shown as dashed lines. When added together the dashed Euclidean vector give the original black Euclidean vector (i.e. the original Euclidean vector is the resultant of its components). In practice it is most useful to resolve Euclidean vector into components which are at right angles to one another usually horizontal and vertical. Any Euclidean vector can be resolved into horizontal and vertical component. If is Euclidean vector then the horizontal component of is Ax and the vertical component is Ay. 
---------------------------------------------------------

11.8.1|Vector addition using components|231
Components can also be used to find the resultant of Euclidean vector This technique can be applied to both graphical and algebraic methods of finding the resultant. The method is simple: make rough sketch of the problem find the horizontal and vertical components of each Euclidean vector find the sum of all horizontal components and the sum of all the vertical components and then use them to find the resultant. Consider the two Euclidean vector and in Figure 11.3 together with their resultant R. Each Euclidean vector in Figure 11.3 can be broken down into component in the x-direction and one in the y-direction. These components are two Euclidean vector which when added give you the original Euclidean vector as the resultant. This is shown in Figure 11.4 where we can see that: In summary addition of the components of the two original Euclidean vector gives the component of the resultant. The same applies to the components. So if we just added all the components together we would get the same answer This is another important property of Euclidean vector

12|Force, Momentum and Impulse|239

---------------------------------------------------------

12.1|Introduction|239
In Grade 10 we studied Motion but not what caused the Motion In this chapter we will learn that net Force is needed to cause Motion We recall what Force is and learn about how Force and Motion are related. We are introduced to two new concepts momentum and impulse and we learn more about turning Force and the Force of Gravity 
---------------------------------------------------------

12.2|Force|239

---------------------------------------------------------

12.2.1|What is a force?|239
Force is anything that can cause change to objects. Force can: change the shape of an object move or stop an object change the Direction (geometry) of moving object. Force can be classified as either contact Force or non-contact Force contact Force must touch or be in contact with an object to cause change. Examples of contact Force are: the Force that is used to push or pull things like on door to open or close it the Force that sculptor uses to turn clay into pot the Force of the wind to turn windmill non-contact Force does not have to touch an object to cause change. Examples of non- contact Force are: the Force due to Gravity like the Earth pulling the Moon towards itself the Force due to electricity like proton and an Electron attracting each other the Force due to Magnet like Magnet pulling paper clip towards itself The unit of Force is the newton (symbol N). This unit is named after Sir Isaac Newton who first defined Force Force is Euclidean vector quantity and has magnitude and Direction (geometry) We use the abbreviation for Force There is popular story that while Sir Isaac Newton was sitting under an apple tree an apple fell on his head and he suddenly thought of the Universal Newton's law of universal gravitation Coincidently the weight of small apple is approximately N. Force was first described by Archimedes of Syracuse (circa 287 BC 212 BC). Archimedes was Greek mathematician astronomer philosopher physicist and engineer. He was killed by Roman soldier during the sack of the city despite orders from the Roman general Marcellus that he was not to be harmed. This chapter will often refer to the resultant Force acting on an object. The resultant Force is simply the Euclidean vector sum of all the Force acting on the object. It is very important to remember that all the Force must be acting on the same object. The resultant Force is the Force that has the same effect as all the other Force added together. 
---------------------------------------------------------

12.2.2|Examples of Forces in Physics|240
Most of Physics revolves around Force Although there are many different Force all are handled in the same way. All Force in Physics can be put into one of four groups. These are gravitational Force electromagnetic Force strong nuclear Force and weak nuclear Force You will mostly come across gravitational or electromagnetic Force at school. Gravitational Force Gravity is the attractive Force between two objects due to the Mass of the objects. When you throw ball in the air its Mass and the Earths Mass attract each other which leads to Force between them. The ball falls back towards the Earth and the Earth accelerates towards the ball. The movement of the Earth towards the ball is however so small that you couldnt possibly measure it. Electromagnetic Force Almost all of the Force that we experience in everyday life are electromagnetic in origin. They have this unusual name because long ago people thought that electric Force and Magnet Force were different things. After much Work (physics) and experimentation it has been realised that they are actually different manifestations of the same underlying theory. Electric or Electrostatics Force If we have objects carrying Electric charge which are not moving then we are dealing with Electrostatics Force Coulomb Law). This Force is actually much stronger than Gravity This may seem strange since Gravity is obviously very Power (physics) and holding balloon to the wall seems to be the most impressive thing Electrostatics Force have done but if we think about it: for Gravity to be detectable we need to have very large Mass nearby. But balloon rubbed in someones hair can stick to wall with Force so strong that it overcomes the Force of gravitywith just the Electric charge in the balloon and the wall Magnet Force The Magnet Force is different manifestation of the electromagnetic Force It stems from the interaction between moving Electric charge as opposed to the fixed Electric charge involved in Coulomb Law. Examples of the Magnet Force in action include Magnet compassescar engines and computer data storage. Magnet are also used in the wrecking industry to pick up cars and move them around sites. Friction According to Newtons First Law (we will discuss this later in the chapter) an object moving without Force acting on it will keep on moving. Then why does box sliding on table stop? The answer is Friction Friction arises from the interaction between the molecules on the bottom of box with the molecules on table. This interaction is electromagnetic in origin hence Friction is just another view of the electromagnetic Force Later in this chapter we will discuss Friction Force little more. Drag Force This is the Force an object experiences while travelling through medium like an aeroplane flying through air. When something travels through the air it needs to Displacement (vector) air as it travels and because of this the air exerts Force on the object. This becomes an important Force when you move fast and lot of thought is taken to try and reduce the amount of drag Force sports car or an aeroplane experiences. The drag Force is very useful for parachutists. They jump from high altitudes and if there was no drag Force then they would continue accelerating all the way to the ground. Parachutes are wide because the more surface area you show the greater the drag Force and hence the slower you hit the ground. 
---------------------------------------------------------

12.2.3|Systems and External Forces|241
The concepts of system and an external Force are very important in Physics system is any collection of objects. If one draws an imaginary box around such system then an external Force is one that is applied by an object or person outside the box. Imagine for example car pulling two trailers. If we draw box around the two trailers they can be considered closed system or unit. When we look at the Force on this closed system the following Force will apply: The Force of the car pulling the unit (trailer and B) The Force of Friction between the wheels of the trailers and the road (opposite to the Direction (geometry) of Motion The Force of the Earth pulling downwards on the system Gravity The Force of the road pushing upwards on the system These Force are called external Force to the system. The following Force will not apply: The Force of pulling The Force of pulling The Force of Friction between the wheels of the car and the road (opposite to the Direction (geometry) of Motion We can also draw box around trailer or in which case the Force will be different. If we consider trailer as system the following external Force will apply: The Force of the car pulling on (towards the right) The Force of pulling on (towards the left) The Force of the Earth pulling downwards on the trailer Gravity The Force of the road pushing upwards on the trailer 
---------------------------------------------------------

12.2.4|Force Diagrams|242
If we look at the example above and draw Force diagram of all the Force acting on the two-trailer-unit the diagram would look like this: It is important to keep the following in mind when you draw Force diagrams: Make your drawing large and clear. You must use arrows and the Direction (geometry) of the arrow will show the Direction (geometry) of the Force The Length of the arrow will indicate the size of the Force in other words the longer arrows in the diagram (F1 for example) indicates bigger Force than shorter arrow (Ff ). Arrows of the same Length indicate Force of equal size (FN and Fg). Use ?little lines? like in maths to show this. Draw neat lines using ruler. The arrows must touch the system or object. All arrows must have labels. Use letters with key on the side if you do not have enough space on your drawing. The labels must indicate what is applying the Force (the Force of the car?) on what the Force is applied (?on the trailer?) and in which Direction (geometry) (to the right) If the values of the Force are known these values can be added to the diagram or key. 
---------------------------------------------------------

12.2.5|Free Body Diagrams|243
In free-body diagram the object of interest is drawn as dot and all the Force acting on it are drawn as arrows pointing away from the dot. Free body diagram for the two-trailer-system will therefore look like this: 
---------------------------------------------------------

12.2.6|Finding the Resultant Force|244
The easiest way to determine resultant Force is to draw Free body diagram Remember from Chapter ?? that we use the Length of the arrow to indicate the Euclidean vector magnitude and the Direction (geometry) of the arrow to show which Direction (geometry) it acts in. After we have done this we have diagram of Euclidean vector and we simply find the sum of the Euclidean vector to get the resultant Force Figure 12.1: (a) Force diagram of Force acting on box. (b) Free body diagram of the box. For example two people push on box from opposite sides with Force of and respectively as shown in Figure 12.1(a). The Free body diagram in Figure 12.1(b) shows the object represented by dot and the two Force are represented by arrows with their tails on the dot. As you can see the arrows point in opposite Direction (geometry) and have different Length The resultant Force is to the left. This result can be obtained algebraically too since the two Force act along the same line. First as in Motion in one Direction (geometry) choose Frame of reference Secondly add the two Euclidean vector taking their Direction (geometry) into account. For the example assume that the Position (vector) Direction (geometry) is to the right then: FR (+4N) (6N) 2N 2N to the left Remember that negative answer means that the Force acts in the opposite Direction (geometry) to the one that you chose to be Position (vector) You can choose the Position (vector) Direction (geometry) to be any way you want but once you have chosen it you must keep it. As you Work (physics) with more Force diagrams in which the Force exactly balance you may notice that you get zero answer (e.g. N). This simply means that the Force are balanced and that the object will not move. Once Force diagram has been drawn the techniques of Euclidean vector addition introduced in Chapter ?? can be used. Depending on the situation you might choose to use graphical technique such as the tail-to-head method or the parallelogram method or else an algebraic approach to determine the resultant. Since Force is Euclidean vector all of these methods apply.

12.3|Newton's Laws|246
In grade 10 you learned about Motion but did not look at how things start to move. You have also learned about Force In this section we will look at the effect of Force on objects and how we can make things move. 
---------------------------------------------------------

12.3.1|Newton's First Law|247
Sir Isaac Newton was scientist who lived in England (1642-1727). He was interested in the reason why objects move. He suggested that objects that are stationary will remain stationary unless Force acts on them and objects that are moving will keep on moving unless Force slows them down Speed them up or let them change Direction (geometry) From this he formulated what is known as Newtons First Law of Motion Definition: Newtons First Law of Motion An object will remain in state of rest or continue traveling at constant Velocity unless acted upon by an unbalanced (net) Force Let us consider the following situations: An ice skater pushes herself away from the side of the ice rink and skates across the ice. She will continue to move in straight line across the ice unless something stops her. Objects are also like that. If we kick soccer ball across soccer Field (physics) according to Newtons First Law the soccer ball should keep on moving forever However in real life this does not happen. Is Newton's laws of motion wrong? Not really. Newtons First Law applies to situations where there arent any external Force present. This means that Friction is not present. In the case of the ice skater the Friction between the skates and the ice is very little and she will continue moving for quite Distance In the case of the soccer ball air Electrical Electrical resistance and conductance and conductance Friction between the air and the ball) and Friction between the grass and the ball is present and this will slow the ball down. Newtons First Law in action We experience Newtons First Law in every day life. Let us look at the following examples: Rockets: spaceship is launched into space. The Force of the exploding gases pushes the rocket through the air into space. Once it is in space the engines are switched off and it will keep on moving at constant Velocity If the astronauts want to change the Direction (geometry) of the spaceship they need to fire an engine. This will then apply Force on the rocket and it will change its Direction (geometry) Seat belts: We wear seat belts in cars. This is to protect us when the car is involved in an accident. If car is traveling at 120 kmhr1 the passengers in the car is also traveling at 120 kmhr1. When the car suddenly stops Force is exerted on the car (making it slow down) but not on the passengers. The passengers will carry on moving forward at 120 kmhr1according to Newton I. If they are wearing seat belts the seat belts will stop them and therefore prevent them from getting hurt. 
---------------------------------------------------------

12.3.2|Newton's Second Law of Motion|249
According to Newton things like to keep on doing what they are doing. In other words if an object is moving it likes to keep on moving and if an object is stationary it likes to stay stationary. So how do objects start to move then? Let us look at the example of 10 Kilogram box on rough table. If we push lightly on the box as indicated in the diagram the box wont move. Lets say we applied Force of 100 yet the box remains stationary. At this point Friction Force of 100 is acting on the box preventing the box from moving. If we increase the Force lets say to 150 and the box just about starts to move the Friction Force is 150 N. To be able to move the box we need to push Hardness enough to overcome the Friction and then move the box. If we therefore apply Force of 200 remembering that Friction Force of 150 is present the first 150 will be used to overcome or cancel the Friction and the other 50 will be used to move (accelerate) the block. In order to accelerate an object we must have resultant Force acting on the block. Now what do you think will happen if we pushed harder lets say 300 N? Or what do you think will happen if the Mass of the block was more say 20 Kilogram or what if it was less? Let us investigate how the Motion of an object is affected by Mass and Force You will have noted in the investigation above that the heavier the trolley is the slower it moved. The acceleration is indirectly proportional to the Mass In mathematical terms: In similar investigation where the Mass is kept constant but the applied Force is varied you will find that the bigger the Force is the faster the object will move. The acceleration of the trolley is therefore directly proportional to the resultant Force In mathematical terms: F. If we rearrange the above equations we get OR ma Newton formulated his second law as follows: Definition: Newtons Second Law of Motion If resultant Force acts on body it will cause the body to accelerate in the Direction (geometry) of the resultant Force The acceleration of the body will be directly proportional to the resultant Force and indirectly proportional to the Mass of the body. The mathematical representation is m. Applying Newtons Second Law Newtons Second Law can be applied to variety of situations. We will look at the main types of examples that you need to study. Object on an inclined plane When we place an object on slope the Force of Gravity (Fg) acts straight down and not perpendicular to the slope. Due to Gravity pulling straight down the object will tend to slide down the slope with Force equal to the horizontal component of the Force of Gravity (Fg sin ). The object will stick to the slope due to the Friction Force between the object and the surface. As you increase the angle of the slope the horizontal component will also increase until the Friction Force is overcome and the object starts to slide down the slope. The Force of Gravity will also tend to push an object into the slope. This Force is equal to the vertical component of the Force of Gravity (Fg cos ). There is no movement in this Direction (geometry) as this Force is balanced by the slope pushing up against the object. This ?pushing Force is called the normal Force (N) and is equal to the resultant Force in the vertical Direction (geometry) Fg sin in this case but opposite in Direction (geometry) Important: Do not use the abbreviation for weight as it is used to abbreviate Work (physics) Rather use the Force of Gravity Fg for weight. Lifts and rockets So far we have looked at objects being pulled or pushed across surface in other words hori- zontal Motion Here we only considered horizontal Force but we can also lift objects up or let them fall. This is vertical Motion where only vertical Force are being considered. Let us consider 500 Kilogram lift with no passengers hanging on cable. The purpose of the cable is to pull the lift upwards so that it can reach the next floor or to let go little so that it can move downwards to the floor below. We will look at five possible stages during the Motion of the lift. Stage 1: The 500 Kilogram lift is stationary at the second floor of tall building. Because the lift is stationary (not moving) there is no resultant Force acting on the lift. This means that the upward Force must be balanced by the downward Force The only Force acting down is the Force of Gravity which is equal to (500 98 4900 N) in this case. The cable must therefore pull upwards with Force of 4900 to keep the lift stationary at this point. Stage 2: The lift moves upwards at an acceleration of ms2. If the lift is accelerating it means that there is resultant Force in the Direction (geometry) of the Motion This means that the Force acting upwards is now bigger than the Force of Gravity Fg (down). To find the magnitude of the Force applied by the cable (Fc) we can do the following calculation: (Remember to choose Direction (geometry) as Position (vector) We have chosen upwards as Position (vector) FR ma Fc Fg ma Fc (4900) (500)(1) Fc 5400 upwards The answer makes sense as we need bigger Force upwards to cancel the effect of Gravity as well as make the lift go faster. Stage 3: The lift moves at constant Velocity When the lift moves at constant Velocity it means that all the Force are balanced and that there is no resultant Force The acceleration is zero therefore FR 0. The Force acting upwards is equal to the Force acting downwards therefore Fc 4900 N. Stage 4: The lift slow down at rate of 2ms2. As the lift is now slowing down there is resultant Force downwards. This means that the Force acting downwards is bigger than the Force acting upwards. To find the magnitude of the Force applied by the cable (Fc) we can do the following calculation: Again we have chosen upwards as Position (vector) which means that the acceleration will be negative number. This makes sense as we need smaller Force upwards to ensure resultant Force down. The Force of Gravity is now bigger than the upward pull of the cable and the lift will slow down. Stage 5: The cable snaps. When the cable snaps the Force that used to be acting upwards is no longer present. The only Force that is present would be the Force of Gravity The lift will Free fall and its acceleration can be calculated as follows: Rockets Like with lifts rockets are also examples of objects in vertical Motion The Force of Gravity pulls the rocket down while the thrust of the engine pushes the rocket upwards. The Force that the engine exerts must overcome the Force of Gravity so that the rocket can accelerate upwards. The Work (physics) example below looks at the application of Newtons Second Law in launching rocket.

12.3.4|Newton's Third Law of Motion|263
Newtons Third Law of Motion deals with the interaction between pairs of objects. For example if you hold book up against wall you are exerting Force on the book (to keep it there) and the book is exerting Force back at you (to keep you from falling through the book). This may Sound strange but if the book was not pushing back at you your hand will push through the book These two Force (the Force of the hand on the book (F1) and the Force of the book on the hand (F2)) are called an action-reaction pair of Force They have the same magnitude but act in opposite Direction (geometry) and act on different objects (the one Force is onto the book and the other is onto your hand). There is another action-reaction pair of Force present in this situation. The book is pushing against the wall (action Force and the wall is pushing back at the book (reaction). The Force of the book on the wall (F3) and the Force of the wall on the book (F4) are shown in the diagram. Definition: Newtons Third Law of Motion If body exerts Force on body then body exerts Force of equal magnitude on body but in the opposite Direction (geometry) Newtons action-reaction pairs can be found everywhere in life where two objects interact with one another. The following Work (physics) examples will illustrate this:

12.3.6|Different types of forces|268
Tension (physics) Tension (physics) is the magnitude of the Force that exists in objects like ropes chains and struts that are providing support. For example there are Tension (physics) Force in the ropes supporting childs swing hanging from tree. Contact and non-contact Force In this chapter we have come across number of different types of Force for example push or pull Tension (physics) in string Friction Force and the normal. These are all examples of contact Force where there is Physics point of contact between applying the Force and the object. Non-contact Force are Force that act over Distance for example Magnet Force Electrostatics Force and gravitational Force When an object is placed on surface two types of surface Force can be identified. Fric- tion is Force that acts between the surface and the object and parallel to the surface. The normal Force is Force that acts between the object and the surface and parallel to the surface. The normal Force Kilogram box is placed on rough surface and 10 Force is applied at an angle of 369 to the horizontal. The box does not move. The normal Force (N or FN) is the Force between the box and the surface acting in the vertical Direction (geometry) If this Force is not present the box would fall through the surface because the Force of Gravity pulls it downwards. The normal Force therefore acts upwards. We can calculate the normal Force by considering all the Force in the vertical Direction (geometry) All the Force in the vertical Direction (geometry) must add up to zero because there is no movement in the vertical Direction (geometry) The most interesting and illustrative normal Force question that is often asked has to do with scale in lift. Using Newtons third law we can solve these problems quite easily. When you stand on scale to measure your weight you are pulled down by Gravity There is no acceleration downwards because there is reaction Force we call the normal Force acting upwards on you. This is the Force that the scale would measure. If the gravitational Force were less then the reading on the scale would be less. Now we are going to add things to exactly the same problem to show how things change slightly. We will now move to lift moving at constant Velocity Remember if Velocity is constant then acceleration is zero. In the previous two examples we got exactly the same result because the net acceleration on the man was zero If the lift is accelerating downwards things are slightly different and now we will get more interesting answer Friction Force When the surface of one object slides over the surface of another each body exerts Friction Force on the other. For example if book slides across table the table exerts Friction Force onto the book and the book exerts Friction Force onto the table (Newtons Third Law). Friction Force act parallel to the surfaces. Force is not always big enough to make an object move for example small applied Force might not be able to move heavy crate. The Friction Force opposing the Motion of the crate is equal to the applied Force but acting in the opposite Direction (geometry) This Friction Force is called static Friction When we increase the applied Force (push harder) the Friction Force will also increase until the applied Force overcomes it. This Friction Force can vary from zero (when no other Force are present and the object is stationary) to maximum that depends on the surfaces. When the applied Force is greater than the Friction Force and the crate will move. The Friction Force will now decrease to new constant value which is also dependent on the surfaces. This is called kinetic Friction In both cases the maximum Friction Force is related to the normal Force and can be calculated as follows: For static Friction Ff Where the coefficient of static Friction and normal Force For kinetic Friction Ff Where the coefficient of kinetic Friction and normal Force Remember that static Friction is present when the object is not moving and kinetic Friction while the object is moving. For example when you drive at constant Velocity in car on tar road you have to keep the accelerator pushed in slightly to overcome the kinetic Friction between the tar road and the wheels of the car. The higher the value for the coefficient of Friction the more sticky the surface is and the lower the value the more slippery the surface is. The Friction Force (Ff acts in the horizontal Direction (geometry) and can be calculated in similar way to the normal for as long as there is no movement. If we use the same example as in figure 12.12 and we choose to the right as Position (vector) We often think about Friction in negative way but very often Friction is useful without us realizing it. If there was no Friction and you tried to prop ladder up against wall it would simply slide to the ground. Rock climbers use Friction to maintain their grip on cliffs. The brakes of cars would be useless if it wasnt for Friction

12.3.8|Forces in equilibrium|276
At the beginning of this chapter it was mentioned that resultant Force cause objects to accelerate in straight line. If an object is stationary or moving at constant Velocity then either no Force are acting on the object or the Force acting on that object are exactly balanced. In other words for stationary objects or objects moving with constant Velocity the resultant Force acting on the object is zero. Additionally if there is perpendicular moment of Force then the object will rotate. You will learn more about moments of Force later in this chapter. Therefore in order for an object not to move or to be in equilibrium the sum of the Force (resultant Force must be zero and the sum of the moments of Force must be zero. Definition: Equilibrium An object in equilibrium has both the sum of the Force acting on it and the sum of the moments of the Force equal to zero. If resultant Force acts on an object then that object can be brought into equilibrium by applying an additional Force that exactly balances this resultant. Such Force is called the equilibrant and is equal in magnitude but opposite in Direction (geometry) to the original resultant Force acting on the object. Definition: Equilibrant The equilibrant of any number of Force is the single Force required to produce equilibrium and is equal in magnitude but opposite in Direction (geometry) to the resultant Force In the figure the resultant of F1 and F2 is shown. The equilibrant of F1 and F2 is then the Euclidean vector opposite in Direction (geometry) to this resultant with the same magnitude (i.e. F3). F1 F2 and F3 are in equilibrium F3 is the equilibrant of F1 and F2 F1 and F2 are kept in equilibrium by F3 As an example of an object in equilibrium consider an object held stationary by two ropes in the arrangement below: Let us draw Free body diagram for the object. In the Free body diagram the object is drawn as dot and all Force acting on the object are drawn in the correct Direction (geometry) starting from that dot. In this case three Force are acting on the object. Each rope exerts Force on the object in the Direction (geometry) of the rope away from the object. These Tension (physics) Force are represented by T1 and T2. Since the object has Mass it is attracted towards the centre of the Earth. This weight is represented in the Force diagram as Fg. Since the object is stationary the resultant Force acting on the object is zero. In other words the three Force Euclidean vector drawn tail-to-head form closed triangle:

12.5|Momentum and Impulse|287
Momentum is Physical quantity which is closely related to Force Momentum is property which applies to moving objects. Definition: Momentum Momentum is the tendency of an object to continue to move in its Direction (geometry) of travel. Momentum is calculated from the product of the Mass and Velocity of an object. The momentum (symbol p) of an object of Mass moving at Velocity is: According to this equation momentum is related to both the Mass and Velocity of an object. small car travelling at the same Velocity as big truck will have smaller momentum than the truck. The smaller the Mass the smaller the Velocity car travelling at 120 kmhr1will have bigger momentum than the same car travelling at 60 kmhr1. Momentum is also related to Velocity the smaller the Velocity the smaller the momentum. Different objects can also have the same momentum for example car travelling slowly can have the same momentum as motor cycle travelling relatively fast. We can easily demonstrate this. Consider car of Mass 000 Kilogram with Velocity of ms1(about 30 kmhr1). The momentum of the car is therefore Now consider motor cycle of Mass 250 Kilogram travelling at 32 ms1 (about 115 kmhr1). The momentum of the motor cycle is: Even though the motor cycle is considerably lighter than the car the fact that the motor cy- cle is travelling much faster than the car means that the momentum of both vehicles is the same. From the calculations above you are able to derive the unit for momentum as kgms1. Momentum is also Euclidean vector quantity because it is the product of Scalar (mathematics) (m) with Euclidean vector (v). This means that whenever we calculate the momentum of an object we need to include the Direction (geometry) of the momentum.

12.5.1|Vector Nature of Momentum|290

12.5.5|Newton's Second Law revisited|293
You have learned about Newtons Second Law of Motion earlier in this chapter. Newtons Second Law describes the relationship between the Motion of an object and the net Force on the object. We said that the Motion of an object and therefore its momentum can only change when resultant Force is acting on it. We can therefore say that because net Force causes an object to move it also causes its momentum to change. We can now define Newtons Second Law of Motion in terms of momentum. Definition: Newtons Second Law of Motion (N2) The net or resultant Force acting on an object is equal to the rate of change of momentum. 
---------------------------------------------------------

12.5.6|Impulse|294
Impulse is the product of the net Force and the time interval for which the Force acts. Impulse is defined as: However from Newtons Second Law we know that Impulse is equal to the change in momentum of an object. From this equation we see that for given change in momentum Fnett is fixed. Thus if Fnet is reduced must be in- creased (i.e. smaller resultant Force must be applied for longer to bring about the same change in momentum). Alternatively if is reduced (i.e. the resultant Force is applied for shorter period) then the resultant Force must be increased to bring about the same change in momentum. 
---------------------------------------------------------

12.5.8|Conservation of Momentum|297
In the absence of an external Force acting on system momentum is conserved. Definition: Conservation of Linear Momentum The total linear momentum of an isolated system is constant. An isolated system has no Force acting on it from the outside. This means that in an isolated system the total momentum before collision or explosion is equal to the total momentum after the collision or explosion. Consider simple collision of two billiard balls. The balls are rolling on frictionless surface and the system is isolated. So we can apply conservation of momentum. The first ball has Mass m1 and an initial Velocity vi1. The second ball has Mass m2 and moves towards the first ball with an initial Velocity vi2. This situation is shown in Figure 12.14. The total momentum of the system before the collision pi is: After the two balls Collision and move away they each have different momentum. If the first ball has final Velocity of vf1 and the second ball has final Velocity of vf2 then we have the situation shown in Figure 12.15. This system of two balls is isolated since there are no external Force acting on the balls. There- fore by the principle of conservation of linear momentum the total momentum before the collision is equal to the total momentum after the collision. This gives the equation for the conservation of momentum in collision of two objects 
---------------------------------------------------------

12.5.9|Physics in Action: Impulse|300
very important application of impulse is improving safety and reducing injuries. In many cases an object needs to be brought to rest from certain initial Velocity This means there is certain specified change in momentum. If the time during which the momentum changes can be increased then the Force that must be applied will be less and so it will cause less damage. This is the principle behind arrestor beds for trucks airbags and bending your knees when you jump off chair and land on the ground. Air-Bags in Motor Vehicles Air bags are used in motor vehicles because they are able to reduce the effect of the Force experienced by person during an accident. Air bags extend the time required to stop the momentum of the driver and passenger. During collision the Motion of the driver and passenger carries them towards the windshield which results in large Force exerted over short time in order to stop their momentum. If instead of hitting the windshield the driver and passenger hit an air bag then the time of the impact is increased. Increasing the time of the impact results in decrease in the Force Padding as Protection During Sports The same principle explains why wicket keepers in cricket use padded gloves or why there are padded mats in gymnastics. In cricket when the wicket keeper catches the ball the padding is slightly Compression (physics) thus reducing the effect of the Force on the wicket keepers hands. Similarly if gymnast falls the padding Compression (physics) and reduces the effect of the Force on the gymnasts body. Arrestor Beds for Trucks An arrestor bed is patch of ground that is softer than the road. Trucks use these when they have to make an emergency stop. When trucks reaches an arrestor bed the time interval over which the momentum is changed is increased. This decreases the Force and causes the truck to slow down. Follow-Through in Sports In sports where rackets and bats are used like tennis cricket squash badminton and baseball the hitter is often encouraged to follow-through when striking the ball. High Speed films of the collisions between batsrackets and balls have shown that following through increases the time over which the collision between the racketbat and ball occurs. This increase in the time of the collision causes an increase in the Velocity change of the ball. This means that hitter can cause the ball to leave the racketbat faster by following through. In these sports returning the ball with higher Velocity often increases the chances of success. Crumple Zones in Cars Another safety application of trying to reduce the Force experienced is in crumple zones in cars. When two cars have collision two things can happen: 1. the cars bounce off each other or 2. the cars crumple together. Which situation is more dangerous for the occupants of the cars? When cars bounce off each other or rebound there is larger change in momentum and therefore larger impulse. larger impulse means that greater Force is experienced by the occupants of the cars. When cars crumple together there is smaller change in momentum and therefore smaller impulse. The smaller impulse means that the occupants of the cars experience smaller Force Car manufacturers use this idea and design crumple zones into cars such that the car has greater chance of crumpling than rebounding in collision. Also when the car crumples the change in the cars momentum happens over longer time. Both these effects result in smaller Force on the occupants of the car thereby increasing their chances of survival. 
---------------------------------------------------------

12.6|Torque and Levers|302

---------------------------------------------------------

12.6.1|Torque|302
This chapter has dealt with Force and how they lead to Motion in straight line. In this section we examine how Force lead to rotational Motion When an object is fixed or supported at one point and Force acts on it Distance away from the support it tends to make the object turn. The moment of Force or Torque (symbol read tau) is defined as the product of the Distance from the support or pivot (r) and the component of Force perpendicular to the object Torque can be seen as rotational Force The unit of Torque is Nm and Torque is Euclidean vector quantity. Some examples of where Torque arises are shown in Figures 12.17 12.18 and 12.19. This shows that there is less Torque when the Force is applied closer to the bolt than further away. Important: Loosening bolt If you are trying to loosen (or tighten) bolt apply the Force on the spanner further away from the bolt as this results in greater Torque to the bolt making it easier to loosen. Important: Any component of Force exerted parallel to an object will not cause the object to turn. Only Tangential and normal components cause turning. Important: Torque The Direction (geometry) of Torque is either clockwise or anticlockwise. When Torque are added choose one Direction (geometry) as Position (vector) and the opposite Direction (geometry) as negative. If equal clockwise and anti- clockwise Torque are applied to an object they will cancel out and there will be no net turning effect. 
---------------------------------------------------------

12.6.2|Mechanical Advantage and Levers|305
We can use our knowlegde about the moments of Force Torque to determine whether situations are balanced. For example two Mass pieces are placed on seesaw as shown in Figure 12.20. The one Mass is Kilogram and the other is Kilogram The Mass are placed Distance and respectively from the pivot. By looking at the clockwise and anti-clockwise moments we can determine whether the seesaw will pivot (move) or not. If the sum of the clockwise and anti-clockwise moments is zero the seesaw is in equilibrium (i.e. balanced). The resultant moment is zero as the clockwise and anti-clockwise moments of Force are in op- Position (vector) Direction (geometry) and therefore cancel each other. As we see in Figure 12.20 we can use different Distance away from pivot to balance two different Force This principle is applied to Lever to make lifting heavy object much easier. Definition: Lever Lever is rigid object that is used with an appropriate Lever or pivot point to multiply the mechanical Force that can be applied to another object. Archimedes reputedly said: Give me Lever long enough and Lever on which to place it and shall move the world. The concept of getting out more than the effort is termed mechanical advantage and is one example of the principle of moments. The Lever allows to do less effort but for greater Distance For instance to lift certain unit of weight with Lever with an effort of half unit we need Distance from the Lever in the efforts side to be twice the Distance of the weights side. It also means that to lift the weight Metre we need to push the Lever for Metre The amount of Work (physics) done is always the same and independent of the dimensions of the Lever (in an ideal Lever The Lever only allows to trade effort for Distance Ideally this means that the mechanical advantage of system is the ratio of the Force that performs the Work (physics) (output or load) to the applied Force (input or effort) assuming there is no Friction in the system. In reality the mechanical advantage will be less than the ideal value by an amount determined by the amount of Friction For example you want to raise an object of Mass 100 Kilogram If the pivot is placed as shown in Figure 12.22 what is the mechanical advantage of the Lever In order to calculate mechanical advantage we need to determine the load and effort. Important: Effort is the input Force and load is the output Force 
---------------------------------------------------------

12.6.3|Classes of levers|307
Class Lever In class Lever the Lever is between the effort and the load. Examples of class Lever are the seesaw crowbar and equal-arm balance. The mechanical advantage of class Lever can be increased by moving the Lever closer to the load. Class Lever In class Lever the Lever is at the one end of the bar with the load closer to the Lever and the effort on the other end of bar. The mechanical advantage of this type of Lever can be increased by increasing the Length of the bar. bottle opener or wheel barrow are examples of class Lever Class Lever In class Lever the Lever is also at the end of the bar but the effort is between the Lever and the load. An example of this type of Lever is the human arm.

13.1|Introduction|327
