13.2|Lenses|327
In this section we will discuss properties of thin lenses. In Grade 10 you learnt about two kinds of mirrors: Curved mirror which were also known as converging mirrors and Curved mirror which were also known as diverging mirrors. Similarly there are two types of lenses: converging and diverging lenses. We have learnt how Light travels in different materials and we are now ready to learn how we can control the Direction (geometry) of Light Ray (optics) We use lenses to control the Direction (geometry) of Light When Light enters lens the Light Ray (optics) bend or change Direction (geometry) as shown in Figure 13.1. Definition: Lens lens is any transparent material (e.g. glass) of an appropriate shape that can take parallel Ray (optics) of incident Light and either converge the Ray (optics) to point or diverge the Ray (optics) from point. Some lenses will focus Light Ray (optics) to single point. These lenses are called converging or concave lenses. Other lenses spread out the Light Ray (optics) so that it looks like they all come from the same point. These lenses are called diverging or convex lenses. Lenses change the Direction (geometry) of Light Ray (optics) by Refraction They are designed so that the image appears in certain place or as certain size. Lenses are used in eyeglasses cameras Optical microscope and Telescope You also have lenses in your eyes Definition: Converging Lenses Converging lenses converge parallel Ray (optics) of Light and are thicker in the middle than at the edges. Definition: Diverging Lenses Diverging lenses diverge parallel Ray (optics) of Light and are thicker at the edges than in the middle. parallel Ray (optics) of Light enter the lens Ray (optics) are focused at the same point (a) converging lens will focus the Ray (optics) that enter the lens parallel Ray (optics) of Light enter the lens Ray (optics) are spread out as if they are coming from the same point (b) diverging lens will spread out the Ray (optics) that enter the lens Figure 13.1: The behaviour of parallel Light Ray (optics) entering either converging or diverging lens. Examples of converging and diverging lenses are shown in Figure 13.2. converging lenses diverging lenses Figure 13.2: Types of lenses Before we study lenses in detail there are few important terms that must be defined. Figure 13.3 shows important lens properties: The principal axis is the line which runs horizontally straight through the Geometrical optics centre of the lens. It is also sometimes called the Geometrical optics axis. The Geometrical optics centre (O) of convex lens is usually the centre point of the lens. The Direction (geometry) of all Light Ray (optics) which pass through the Geometrical optics centre remains unchanged. The focus or focal point of the lens is the Position (vector) on the principal axis where all Light Ray (optics) which run parallel to the principal axis through the lens converge (come together) at point. Since Light can pass through the lens either from right to left or left to right there is focal point on each side of the lens (F1 and F2) at the same Distance from the Geometrical optics centre in each Direction (geometry) (Note: the plural form of the word focus is foci.) The focal Length (f) is the Distance between the Geometrical optics centre and the focal point. 
---------------------------------------------------------

13.2.1|Converging Lenses|329
We will only discuss double convex converging lenses as shown in Figure 13.4. Converging lenses are thinner on the outside and thicker on the inside. Figure 13.5 shows convex lens. Light Ray (optics) traveling through convex lens are bent towards the principal axis. For this reason convex lenses are called converging lenses. Figure 13.5: Light Ray (optics) bend towards each other or converge when they travel through convex lens. F1 and F2 are the foci of the lens. The type of images created by convex lens is dependent on the Position (vector) of the object. We will examine the following cases: 1. the object is placed at Distance greater than 2f from the lens 2. the object is placed at Distance equal to 2f from the lens 3. the object is placed at Distance between 2f and from the lens 4. the object is placed at Distance less than from the lens We examine the properties of the image in each of these cases by drawing Ray (optics) diagrams. We can find the image by tracing the path of three Light Ray (optics) through the lens. Any two of these Ray (optics) will show us the location of the image. You can use the third Ray (optics) to check the location. Drawing Ray (optics) Diagrams for Converging Lenses The three Ray (optics) are labelled R1 R2 and R3. The Ray (optics) diagrams that follow will use this naming convention. 1. The first Ray (optics) (R1) travels from the object to the lens parallel to the principal axis. This Ray (optics) is bent by the lens and travels through the focal point. 2. Any Ray (optics) travelling parallel to the principal axis is bent through the focal point. 3. If Light Ray (optics) passes through focal point before it enters the lens then it will leave the lens parallel to the principal axis. The second Ray (optics) (R2) is therefore drawn to pass through the focal point before it enters the lens. 4. Ray (optics) that travels through the centre of the lens does not change Direction (geometry) The third Ray (optics) (R3) is drawn through the centre of the lens. 5. The point where all three of the Ray (optics) (R1 R2 and R3) intersect is the image of the point where they all started. The image will form at this point. CASE 1: Object placed at Distance greater than 2f from the lens Figure 13.7: An object is placed at Distance greater than 2f away from the converging lens. Three Ray (optics) are drawn to locate the image which is real smaller than the object and inverted. We can locate the Position (vector) of the image by drawing our three Ray (optics) R1 travels from the object to the lens parallel to the principal axis and is bent by the lens and then travels through the focal point. R2 passes through the focal point before it enters the lens and therefore must leave the lens parallel to the principal axis. R3 travels through the center of the lens and does not change Direction (geometry) The point where R1 R2 and R3 intersect is the image of the point where they all started. The image of an object placed at Distance greater than 2f from the lens is upside down or inverted. This is because the Ray (optics) which began at the top of the object above the principal axis after passing through the lens end up below the principal axis. The image is called Real image because it is on the opposite side of the lens to the object and you can trace all the Light Ray (optics) directly from the image back to the object. The image is also smaller than the object and is located closer to the lens than the object. Important: In reality Light Ray (optics) come from all points along the Length of the object. In Ray (optics) diagrams we only draw three Ray (optics) (all starting at the top of the object) to keep the diagram clear and simple. CASE 2: Object placed at Distance equal to 2f from the lens Figure 13.8: An object is placed at Distance equal to 2f away from the converging lens. Three Ray (optics) are drawn to locate the image which is real the same size as the object and inverted. We can locate the Position (vector) of the image by drawing our three Ray (optics) R1 travels from the object to the lens parallel to the principal axis and is bent by the lens and then travels through the focal point. R2 passes through the focal point before it enters the lens and therefore must leave the lens parallel to the principal axis. R3 travels through the center of the lens and does not change Direction (geometry) The point where R1 R2 and R3 intersect is the image of the point where they all started. The image of an object placed at Distance equal to 2f from the lens is upside down or inverted. This is because the Ray (optics) which began at the top of the object above the principal axis after passing through the lens end up below the principal axis. The image is called Real image because it is on the opposite side of the lens to the object and you can trace all the Light Ray (optics) directly from the image back to the object. The image is the same size as the object and is located at Distance 2f away from the lens. CASE 3: Object placed at Distance between 2f and from the lens Figure 13.9: An object is placed at Distance between 2f and away from the converging lens. Three Ray (optics) are drawn to locate the image which is real larger than the object and inverted. We can locate the Position (vector) of the image by drawing our three Ray (optics) R1 travels from the object to the lens parallel to the principal axis and is bent by the lens and then travels through the focal point. R2 passes through the focal point before it enters the lens and therefore must leave the lens parallel to the principal axis. R3 travels through the center of the lens and does not change Direction (geometry) The point where R1 R2 and R3 intersect is the image of the point where they all started. The image of an object placed at Distance between 2f and from the lens is upside down or inverted. This is because the Ray (optics) which began at the top of the object above the principal axis after passing through the lens end up below the principal axis. The image is called Real image because it is on the opposite side of the lens to the object and you can trace all the Light Ray (optics) directly from the image back to the object. The image is larger than the object and is located at Distance greater than 2f away from the lens. CASE 4: Object placed at Distance less than from the lens Figure 13.10: An object is placed at Distance less than away from the converging lens. Three Ray (optics) are drawn to locate the image which is virtual larger than the object and upright. We can locate the Position (vector) of the image by drawing our three Ray (optics) R1 travels from the object to the lens parallel to the principal axis and is bent by the lens and then travels through the focal point. R2 passes through the focal point before it enters the lens and therefore must leave the lens parallel to the principal axis. R3 travels through the center of the lens and does not change Direction (geometry) The point where R1 R2 and R3 intersect is the image of the point where they all started. The image of an object placed at Distance less than from the lens is upright. The image is called Virtual image because it is on the same side of the lens as the object and you cannot trace all the Light Ray (optics) directly from the image back to the object. The image is larger than the object and is located further away from the lens than the object. Extension: The thin lens equation and Magnification The Thin Lens Equation We can find the Position (vector) of the image of lens mathematically as there is mathematical relation between the object Distance image Distance and focal Length The equation is: where is the focal Length do is the object Distance and di is the image Distance The object Distance do is the Distance from the object to the lens. do is Position (vector) if the object is on the same side of the lens as the Light Ray (optics) enter the lens. This should make sense: we expect the Light Ray (optics) to travel from the object to the lens. The image Distance di is the Distance from the lens to the image. Unlike mirrors which Reflection (physics) Light back lenses Refraction Light through them. We expect to find the image on the same side of the lens as the Light leaves the lens. If this is the case then di is Position (vector) and the image is real (see Figure 13.9). Sometimes the image will be on the same side of the lens as the Light Ray (optics) enter the lens. Then di is negative and the image is virtual (Figure 13.10). If we know any two of the three quantities above then we can use the Thin Lens Equation to solve for the third quantity. Magnification It is possible to calculate the Magnification of an image. The Magnification is how much bigger or smaller the image is than the object. where is the Magnification do is the object Distance and di is the image Distance If di and do are both Position (vector) the Magnification is negative. This means the image is inverted or upside down. If di is negative and do is Position (vector) then the image is not inverted or right side up. If the absolute value of the Magnification is greater than one the image is larger than the object. For example Magnification of -2 means the image is inverted and twice as big as the object. 
---------------------------------------------------------

13.2.2|Diverging Lenses|340
We will only discuss double concave diverging lenses as shown in Figure 13.11. Concave lenses are thicker on the outside and thinner on the inside. Figure 13.12 shows concave lens with Light Ray (optics) travelling through it. You can see that concave lenses have the opposite curvature to convex lenses. This causes Light Ray (optics) passing through concave lens to diverge or be spread out away from the principal axis. For this reason concave lenses are called diverging lenses. Images formed by concave lenses are always virtual. Unlike converging lenses the type of images created by concave lens is not dependent on the Position (vector) of the object. The image is always upright smaller than the object and located closer to the lens than the object. We examine the properties of the image by drawing Ray (optics) diagrams. We can find the image by tracing the path of three Light Ray (optics) through the lens. Any two of these Ray (optics) will show us the location of the image. You can use the third Ray (optics) to check the location but it is not necessary to show it on your diagram. Figure 13.12: Light Ray (optics) bend away from each other or diverge when they travel through concave lens. F1 and F2 are the foci of the lens. Drawing Ray (optics) Diagrams for Diverging Lenses Draw the three Ray (optics) starting at the top of the object. 1. Ray (optics) R1 travels parallel to the principal axis. The Ray (optics) bends and lines up with focal point. However the concave lens is diverging lens so the Ray (optics) must line up with the focal point on the same side of the lens where Light Ray (optics) enter it. This means that we must project an imaginary line backwards through that focal point (F1) (shown by the dashed line extending from R1). 2. Ray (optics) R2 points towards the focal point F2 on the opposite side of the lens. When it hits the lens it is bent parallel to the principal axis. 3. Ray (optics) R3 passes through the Geometrical optics center of the lens. Like for the convex lens this Ray (optics) passes through with its Direction (geometry) unchanged. 4. We find the image by locating the point where the Ray (optics) meet. Since the Ray (optics) diverge they will only meet if projected backward to point on the same side of the lens as the object. This is why concave lenses always have Virtual image (Since the Light Ray (optics) do not actually meet at the image the image cannot be real.) Figure 13.13 shows an object placed at an arbitrary Distance from the diverging lens. We can locate the Position (vector) of the image by drawing our three Ray (optics) for diverging lens. Figure 13.13 shows that the image of an object is upright. The image is called Virtual image because it is on the same side of the lens as the object. The image is smaller than the object and is closer to the lens than the object. Figure 13.13: Three Ray (optics) are drawn to locate the image which is virtual smaller than the object and upright. 
---------------------------------------------------------

13.3|The Human Eye|344

---------------------------------------------------------

13.3.1|Structure of the Eye|345
Eyesight begins with lenses. As Light Ray (optics) enter your eye they pass first through the cornea and then through the Crystallinity lens. These form double lens system and focus Light Ray (optics) onto the back wall of the eye called the retina. Rods and cones are nerve cells on the retina that transform Light into electrical signals. These signals are sent to the brain via the Geometrical optics nerve. cross-section of the eye is shown in Figure 13.14. For clear vision the image must be formed right on the retina not in front of or behind it. To accomplish this you may need long or short focal Length depending on the object Distance How do we get the exact right focal Length we need? Remember that the lens system has two parts. The cornea is fixed in place but the Crystallinity lens is Stiffness it can change shape. When the shape of the lens changes its focal Length also changes. You have muscles in your eye called ciliary muscles that control the shape of the Crystallinity lens. When you focus your gaze on something you are squeezing (or relaxing) these muscles. This process of accommodation changes the focal Length of the lens and allows you to see an image clearly. The lens in the eye creates Real image that is smaller than the object and is inverted 
---------------------------------------------------------

13.3.2|Defects of Vision|346
In normal eye the image is focused on the retina. If the muscles in the eye are unable to accommodate adequately the image will not be in focus. This leads to problems with vision. There are three basic conditions that arise: 1. short-sightedness 2. long-sightedness 3. astigmatism Short-sightedness Short-sightedness or myopia is defect of vision which means that the image is focused in front of the retina. Close objects are seen clearly but distant objects appear blurry. This condition can be corrected by placing diverging lens in front of the eye. The diverging lens spreads out Light Ray (optics) before they enter the eye. The situation for short-sightedness and how to correct it is shown in Figure 13.17. Long-sightedness Long-sightedness or hyperopia is defect of vision which means that the image is focused in behind the retina. People with this condition can see distant objects clearly but not close ones. converging lens in front of the eye corrects long-sightedness by converging the Light Ray (optics) slightly before they enter the eye. Reading glasses are an example of converging lens used to correct long-sightedness. Astigmatism Astigmatism is characterised by cornea or lens that is not spherical but is more curved in one plane compared to another. This means that horizontal lines may be focused at different point to vertical lines. Astigmatism is corrected by special lens which has different focal Length in the vertical and horizontal planes. 
---------------------------------------------------------

13.4|Gravitational Lenses|347
Einsteins Theory of General Relativity predicts that Light that passes close to very heavy objects like galaxies black holes and massive stars will be bent. These massive objects therefore act as kind of lens that is known as gravitational lens. Gravitational lenses distort and change the apparent Position (vector) of the image of stars. If heavy object is acting as gravitational lens then an observer from Earth will see many images of distant star (Figure 13.19). 
---------------------------------------------------------

13.5|Telescopes|347
We have seen how simple lens can be used to correct eyesight. Lenses and mirrors are also combined to magnify (or make bigger) objects that are far away. Telescope use combinations of lenses to gather and focus Light However Telescope collect Light from objects that are large but far away like Planet and galaxies. For this reason Telescope are the tools of astronomers. Astronomy is the study of objects outside the Earth like stars Planet galaxies comets and asteroids. Usually the object viewed with Telescope is very far away. There are two types of objects: those with detectable diameter such as the moon and objects that appear as points of Light like stars. There are many kinds of Telescope but we will look at two basic types: Reflection (physics) and Refraction 
---------------------------------------------------------

13.5.1|Refracting Telescopes|347
Refraction Telescope like the one pictured in Figure 13.20 uses two convex lenses to enlarge an image. The Refraction Telescope has large primary lens with long focal Length to gather lot of Light The lenses of Refraction Telescope share focal point. This ensures that parallel Ray (optics) entering the Telescope are again parallel when they reach your eye. 
---------------------------------------------------------

13.5.2|Reflecting Telescopes|348
Some Telescope use mirrors as well as lenses and are called Reflection (physics) Telescope Specifically Reflection (physics) Telescope uses convex lens and two mirrors to make an object appear larger. (Figure 13.21.) Light is collected by the primary mirror which is large and concave. Parallel Ray (optics) traveling toward this mirror are Reflection (physics) and focused to point. The secondary Plane mirror is placed within the focal Length of the primary mirror. This changes the Direction (geometry) of the Light final eyepiece lens diverges the Ray (optics) so that they are parallel when they reach your eye. 
---------------------------------------------------------

13.5.3|Southern African Large Telescope|348
The Southern African Large Telescope (SALT) is the largest single Geometrical optics Telescope in the southern hemisphere with hexagonal mirror array 11 Metre across. SALT is located in Sutherland in the Northern Cape. SALT is able to record distant stars galaxies and quasars billion times too faint to be seen with the unaided eye. This is equivalent to person being able to see candle flame at on the moon. SALT was completed in 2005 and is truly international initiative because the money to build it came from South Africa the United States Germany Poland the United Kingdom and New Zealand. 
---------------------------------------------------------

13.6|Microscopes|349
We have seen how lenses and mirrors are combined to magnify objects that are far away in Telescope Lenses can also be used to make very small objects bigger. Figure 13.10 shows that when an object is placed at Distance less than from the lens the image formed is virtual upright and is larger than the object. This set-up is simple magnifier. If you want to look at something very small two lenses may Work (physics) better than one. Optical microscope and Telescope often use two lenses to make an image large enough to see. Optical microscope uses two lenses to achieve high Magnification (Figure 13.22). Both lenses are convex or converging. Light from the object first passes through the objective lens. The lens that you look through is called the eyepiece. The focus of the system can be adjusted by changing the Length of the tube between the lenses. Drawing Ray (optics) Diagram for Two-Lens System You already have all the tools to analyze two-lens system. Just consider one lens at time. 1. Use Ray (optics) tracing or the lens equation to find the image for the first lens. 2. Use the image of the first lens as the object of the second lens. 3. To find the Magnification multiply:

14|Longitudinal Waves|355

---------------------------------------------------------

14.1|Introduction|355
In Grade 10 we studied pulses and Wave We looked at transverse Wave more closely. In this chapter we look at another type of Wave called longitudinal Wave In transverse Wave the Motion of the particles in the medium were perpendicular to the Direction (geometry) of the Wave In longitudinal Wave the particles in the medium move parallel (in the same Direction (geometry) as) to the Motion of the Wave Examples of transverse Wave are water Wave or Light Wave An example of longitudinal Wave is Sound Wave 
---------------------------------------------------------

14.2|What is a longitudinal wave?|355
Definition: Longitudinal Wave longitudinal Wave is Wave where the particles in the medium move parallel to the Direction (geometry) of propagation of the Wave When we studied transverse Wave we looked at two different Motion the Motion of the particles of the medium and the Motion of the save itself. We will do the same for longitudinal Wave The question is how do we construct such Wave To create transverse Wave we flick the end of for example rope up and down. The particles move up and down and return to their equilibrium Position (vector) The Wave moves from left to right and will be Displacement (vector) longitudinal Wave is seen best in spring that is hung from ceiling. Do the following investigation to find out more about longitudinal Wave From the investigation you will have noticed that the disturbance moves in the same Direction (geometry) as the Direction (geometry) in which the spring was pulled. The spring was pulled up and down and the Wave also moved up and down. The ribbon in the investigation represents one particle in the medium. The particles in the medium move in the same Direction (geometry) as the Wave The ribbon moves from rest upwards then back to its original Position (vector) then down and then back to its original Position (vector) 
---------------------------------------------------------

14.3|Characteristics of Longitudinal Waves|356
As for transverse Wave the following can be defined for longitudinal Wave Wavelength Amplitude period Frequency and Wave Speed However instead of peaks and Crest (physics) longitudinal Wave have Compression (physics) and Rarefaction Definition: Compression (physics) Compression (physics) is region in longitudinal Wave where the particles are closer together. Definition: Rarefaction Rarefaction is region in longitudinal Wave where the particles are further apart. 
---------------------------------------------------------

14.3.1|Compression and Rarefaction|356
As seen in Figure 14.2 there are regions where the medium is Compression (physics) and other regions where the medium is spread out in longitudinal Wave The region where the medium is Compression (physics) is known as Compression (physics) and the region where the medium is spread out is known as Rarefaction 
---------------------------------------------------------

14.3.2|Wavelength and Amplitude|357
Definition: Wavelength The Wavelength in longitudinal Wave is the Distance between two consecutive points that are in phase. The Wavelength in longitudinal Wave refers to the Distance between two consecutive Compression (physics) or between two consecutive Rarefaction Definition: Amplitude The Amplitude is the maximum Displacement (vector) from Position (vector) of rest. The Amplitude is the Distance from the equilibrium Position (vector) of the medium to Compression (physics) or Rarefaction 
---------------------------------------------------------

14.3.3|Period and Frequency|357
Definition: Period The period of Wave is the time taken by the Wave to move one Wavelength Definition: Frequency The Frequency of Wave is the number of Wavelength per second. The period of longitudinal Wave is the time taken by the Wave to move one Wavelength As for transverse Wave the symbol is used to represent period and period is measured in seconds (s). The Frequency of Wave is the number of Wavelength per second. Using this definition and the fact that the period is the time taken for Wavelength we can define:

The Speed of longitudinal Wave is defined as: where Speed in m.s1 Frequency in Hertz Wavelength in 
---------------------------------------------------------

14.4|Graphs of Particle Position, Displacement, Velocity and Acceleration|359
When longitudinal Wave moves through the medium the particles in the medium only move back and forth relative to the Direction (geometry) of Motion of the Wave We can see this in Figure 14.4 which shows the Motion of the particles in medium as longitudinal Wave moves through the medium. Figure 14.4: Position (vector) of particles in medium at different times as longitudinal Wave moves through it. The Wave moves to the right. The dashed line shows the equilibrium Position (vector) of particle 0. Important: particle in the medium only moves back and forth when longitudinal Wave moves through the medium. As in Chapter we can draw graph of the particles Position (vector) as function of time. For the Wave shown in Figure 14.4 we can draw the graph shown in Figure 14.5 for particle 0. The graph for each of the other particles will be identical. Figure 14.5: Graph of particle Displacement (vector) as function of time for the longitudinal Wave shown in Figure 14.4. The graph of the particles acceleration as function of time is obtained by taking the gradient of the Velocity vs. time graph. ---------------------------------------------------------

14.5|Sound Waves|360
Sound Wave coming from tuning fork cause the tuning fork to vibrate and push against the air particles in front of it. As the air particles are pushed together Compression (physics) is formed. The particles behind the Compression (physics) move further apart causing Rarefaction As the particles continue to push against each other the Sound Wave travels through the air. Due to this Motion of the particles there is constant variation in the pressure in the air. Sound Wave are therefore pressure Wave This means that in media where the particles are closer together Sound Wave will travel quicker. Sound Wave travel faster through liquids like water than through the air because water is denser than air (the particles are closer together). Sound Wave travel faster in solids than in liquids. Figure 14.8: Sound Wave are pressure Wave and need medium through which to travel. Important: Sound Wave is different from Light Wave Sound Wave is produced by an oscillating object while Light Wave is not. Sound Wave cannot be Diffraction while Light Wave can be Diffraction Also because Sound Wave is mechanical Wave (i.e. that it needs medium) it is not capable of traveling through vacuum whereas Light Wave can travel through vacuum. Important: Sound Wave is pressure Wave This means that regions of high pressure Compression (physics) and low pressure Rarefaction are created as the Sound source vibrates. These Compression (physics) and Rarefaction arise because Sound vibrates longitudinally and the longitudinal Motion of air produces pressure fluctuations. Sound will be studied in more detail in Chapter 15. 
---------------------------------------------------------

14.6|Seismic Waves|361
Seismic Wave are Wave from vibrations in the Earth (core mantle oceans). Seismic Wave also occur on other Planet for example the moon and can be natural (due to earthquakes volcanic eruptions or meteor strikes) or man-made (due to explosions or anything that hits the earth Hardness Seismic P-waves (P for pressure) are longitudinal Wave which can travel through solid and liquid. 
---------------------------------------------------------

15|Sound|363

---------------------------------------------------------

15.1|Introduction|363
Now that we have studied the basics of longitudinal Wave we are ready to study Sound Wave in detail. Have you ever thought about how amazing your sense of hearing is? It is actually pretty remarkable. There are many types of Sound car horn laughing baby barking dog and somehow your brain can sort it all out. Though it seems complicated it is rather simple to understand once you learn very simple fact. Sound is Wave So you can use everything you know about Wave to explain Sound 
---------------------------------------------------------

15.2|Characteristics of a Sound Wave|363
Since Sound is Wave we can relate the properties of Sound to the properties of Wave The basic properties of Sound are: Pitch (music) loudness and Musical tone Figure 15.1: Pitch (music) and loudness of Sound Sound has lower Pitch (music) (lower Frequency than Sound and is softer (smaller Amplitude than Sound C. 
---------------------------------------------------------

15.2.1|Pitch|364
The Frequency of Sound Wave is what your ear understands as Pitch (music) higher Frequency Sound has higher Pitch (music) and lower Frequency Sound has lower Pitch (music) In Figure 15.1 Sound has higher Pitch (music) than Sound B. For instance the chirp of bird would have high Pitch (music) but the roar of lion would have low Pitch (music) The human ear can detect wide range of Frequency Frequency from 20 to 20 000 Hertz are audible to the human ear. Any Sound with Frequency below 20 Hertz is known as an infrasound and any Sound with Frequency above 20 000 Hertz is known as an ultrasound. Table 15.1 lists the ranges of some common animals compared to humans. 
---------------------------------------------------------

15.2.2|Loudness|364
The Amplitude of Sound Wave determines its loudness or volume. larger Amplitude means louder Sound and smaller Amplitude means softer Sound In Figure 15.1 Sound is louder than Sound B. The vibration of source sets the Amplitude of Wave It transmits Energy into the medium through its vibration. More energetic vibration corresponds to larger Amplitude The molecules move back and forth more vigorously. The loudness of Sound is also determined by the sensitivity of the ear. The human ear is more sensitive to some Frequency than to others. Loudness thus depends on both the Amplitude of Sound Wave and its Frequency whether it lies in region where the ear is more or less sensitive. 
---------------------------------------------------------

15.2.3|Tone|364
Musical tone is measure of the quality of the Sound Wave For example the quality of the Sound produced in particular musical instruments depends on which harmonics are superposed and in which proportions. The harmonics are determined by the Standing wave that are produced in the instrument. Chapter 16 will explain the Physics of music in greater detail. The quality (timbre) of the Sound heard depends on the pattern of the incoming vibrations i.e. the shape of the Sound Wave The more irregular the vibrations the more jagged is the shape of the Sound Wave and the harsher is the Sound heard. 
---------------------------------------------------------

15.3|Speed of Sound|365
The Speed of Sound depends on the medium the Sound is travelling in. Sound travels faster in solids than in liquids and faster in liquids than in gases. This is because the density of solids is higher than that of liquids which means that the particles are closer together. Sound can be transmitted more easily. The Speed of Sound also depends on the Temperature of the medium. The hotter the medium is the faster its particles move and therefore the quicker the Sound will travel through the medium. When we heat substance the particles in that substance have more kinetic Energy and vibrate or move faster. Sound can therefore be transmitted more easily and quickly in hotter substances. Sound Wave are pressure Wave The Speed of Sound will therefore be influenced by the pressure of the medium through which it is travelling. At sea level the air pressure is higher than high up on mountain. Sound will travel faster at sea level where the air pressure is higher than it would at places high above sea level. Definition: Speed of Sound The Speed of Sound in air at sea level at Temperature of 21C and under normal atmo- spheric conditions is 344 ms1. 
---------------------------------------------------------

15.4|Physics of the Ear and Hearing|365
The human ear is divided into three main sections: the outer middle and inner ear. Lets follow the journey of Sound Wave from the pinna to the auditory nerve which transmits signal to the brain. The pinna is the part of the ear we typically think of when we refer to the ear. Its main function is to collect and focus an incident Sound Wave The Wave then travels through the ear canal until it meets the eardrum. The pressure fluctuations of the Sound Wave make the eardrum vibrate. The three very small bones of the middle ear the malleus (hammer) the incus (anvil) and the stapes (stirrup) transmit the signal through to the elliptical window. The elliptical window is the beginning of the inner ear. From the elliptical window the Sound Wave are transmitted through the liquid in the inner ear and interpreted as Sound by the brain. The inner ear made of the semicircular canals the cochlea and the auditory nerve is filled with fluid. The fluid allows the body to detect quick movements and maintain balance. The snail-shaped cochlea is covered in nerve cells. There are more than 25 000 hairlike nerve cells. Different nerve cells vibrate with different Frequency When nerve cell vibrates it releases electrical impulses to the auditory nerve. The impulses are sent to the brain through the auditory nerve and understood as Sound 
---------------------------------------------------------

15.4.1|Intensity of Sound|366
Intensity is one indicator of Amplitude Intensity is the Energy transmitted over unit of area each second. Extension: Intensity Intensity is defined as: Intensity Energy time area Power (physics) area By the definition of intensity we can see that the units of intensity are Joule m2 Watts m2 The unit of intensity is the decibel (symbol: dB). This reduces to an SI equivalent of m2. The threshold of hearing is 1012 m2. Below this intensity the Sound is too soft for the ear to hear. The threshold of pain is 1.0 m2. Above this intensity Sound is so loud it becomes uncomfortable for the ear. Notice that there is factor of 1012 between the thresholds of hearing and pain. This is one reason we define the decibel (dB) scale. Extension: dB Scale The intensity in dB of Sound of intensity is given by: 10 log Io Io 1012 m2 (15.1) In this way we can Compression (physics) the whole hearing intensity scale into range from dB to 120 dB. Notice that there are Sound which exceed the threshold of pain. Exposure to these Sound can cause immediate damage to hearing. In fact exposure to Sound from 80 dB and above can damage hearing over time. Measures can be taken to avoid damage such as wearing earplugs or ear muffs. Limiting exposure time and increasing Distance between you and the source are also important steps to protecting your hearing. 
---------------------------------------------------------

15.5|Ultrasound|367
Ultrasound is Sound with Frequency that is higher than 20 kHz. Some animals such as dogs dolphins and bats have an upper limit that is greater than that of the human ear and can hear ultrasound. The most common use of ultrasound is to create images and has industrial and medical applications. The use of ultrasound to create images is based on the Reflection (physics) and transmission of Wave at boundary. When an ultrasound Wave travels inside an object that is made up of different materials such as the human body each time it encounters boundary e.g. between bone and muscle or muscle and fat part of the Wave is Reflection (physics) and part of it is transmitted. The Reflection (physics) Ray (optics) are detected and used to construct an image of the object. Ultrasound in medicine can visualise muscle and soft tissue making them useful for scanning the organs and is commonly used during pregnancy. Ultrasound is safe non-invasive method of looking inside the human body. Ultrasound sources may be used to generate local heating in biological tissue with applications in Physics therapy and cancer treatment. Focussed ultrasound sources may be used to break up kidney stones. Ultrasonic cleaners sometimes called supersonic cleaners are used at Frequency from 20-40 kHz for jewellery lenses and other Geometrical optics parts watches dental instruments surgical instruments and industrial parts. These cleaners consist of containers with fluid in which the object to be cleaned is placed. Ultrasonic Wave are then sent into the fluid. The main mechanism for cleaning action in an ultrasonic cleaner is actually the Energy released from the collapse of millions of Optical microscope bubbles occurring in the liquid of the cleaner. Ultrasound generatorspeaker systems are sold with claims that they frighten away rodents and insects but there is no scientific evidence that the devices Work (physics) controlled tests have shown that rodents quickly learn that the speakers are harmless. In echo-sounding the Reflection (physics) from ultrasound pulses that are bounced off objects (for example the bottom of the sea fish etc.) are picked up. The Reflection (physics) are timed and since their Speed is known the Distance to the object can be found. This information can be built into picture of the object that Reflection (physics) the ultrasound pulses. 
---------------------------------------------------------

15.6|SONAR|368
Ships on the ocean make use of the Reflection (physics) properties of Sound Wave to determine the depth of the ocean. Sound Wave is transmitted and bounces off the seabed. Because the Speed of Sound is known and the time lapse between sending and receiving the Sound can be measured the Distance from the ship to the bottom of the ocean can be determined This is called sonar which stands from Sound Navigation And Ranging. 
---------------------------------------------------------

15.6.1|Echolocation|368
Animals like dolphins and bats make use of Sound Wave to find their way. Just like ships on the ocean bats use sonar to navigate. Ultrasound Wave that are sent out are Reflection (physics) off the objects around the animal. Bats or dolphins then use the Reflection (physics) Sound to form picture of their surroundings. This is called echolocation. 
---------------------------------------------------------

16|The Physics of Music|373

---------------------------------------------------------

16.1|Introduction|373
What is your favorite musical instrument? How do you play it? Do you pluck string like guitar? Do you blow through it like flute? Do you hit it like drum? All of these Work (physics) by making Standing wave Each instrument has unique Sound because of the special Wave made in it. These Wave could be in the strings of guitar or violin. They could also be in the skin of drum or tube of air in trumpet. These Wave are picked up by the air and later reach your ear as Sound In Grade 10 you learned about Standing wave and boundary conditions. We saw rope that was: fixed at both ends fixed at one end and free at the other We also saw pipe: closed at both ends open at both ends open at one end closed at the other String and wind instruments are good examples of Standing wave on strings and pipes. One way to describe Standing wave is to count nodes. Recall that node is point on string that does not move as the Wave changes. The anti-nodes are the highest and lowest points on the Wave There is node at each end of fixed string. There is also node at the closed end of pipe. But an open end of pipe has an anti-node. What causes Standing wave There are incident and Reflection (physics) Wave traveling back and forth on our string or pipe. For some Frequency these Wave combine in just the right way so that the whole Wave appears to be standing still. These special cases are called harmonic Frequency or harmonics. They depend on the Length and material of the medium. Definition: Harmonic harmonic Frequency is Frequency at which Standing wave can be made. 
---------------------------------------------------------

16.2|Standing Waves in String Instruments|373
Let us look at basic instrument: string pulled tight and fixed at both ends. When you pluck the string you hear certain Pitch (music) This Pitch (music) is made by certain Frequency What causes the string to emit Sound at this Pitch (music) You have learned that the Frequency of Standing wave depends on the Length of the Wave The Wavelength depends on the nodes and anti-nodes. The longest Wave that can fit on the string is shown in Figure 16.1. This is called the fundamental or natural Frequency of the string. The string has nodes at both ends. The Wavelength of the fundamental is twice the Length of the string. Now put your finger on the center of the string. Hold it down gently and pluck it. The Standing wave now has node in the middle of the string. There are three nodes. We can fit whole Wave between the ends of the string. This means the Wavelength is equal to the Length of the string. This Wave is called the first harmonic. As we add more nodes we find the second harmonic third harmonic and so on. We must keep the nodes equally spaced or we will lose our Standing wave You should have found this formula: 2L Here is the number of nodes. is the Length of the string. The Frequency is: Here is the Velocity of the Wave This may seem confusing. The Wave is Standing wave so how can it have Velocity But one Standing wave is made up of many Wave that travel back and forth on the string. Each of these Wave has the same Velocity This Speed depends on the Mass and Tension (physics) of the string. Extension: Guitar Guitars use strings with high Tension (physics) The Length Tension (physics) and Mass of the strings affect the Pitch (music) you hear. High Tension (physics) and short strings make high Frequency Low Tension (physics) and long strings make low Frequency When string is first plucked it vibrates at many Frequency All of these except the harmonics are quickly filtered out. The harmonics make up the Musical tone we hear. The body of guitar acts as large wooden soundboard. Here is how soundboard Work (physics) the body picks up the vibrations of the strings. It then passes these vibrations to the air. Sound hole allows the soundboard of the guitar to vibrate more freely. It also helps Sound Wave to get out of the body. The neck of the guitar has thin metal bumps on it called frets. Pressing string against fret shortens the Length of that string. This raises the natural Frequency and the Pitch (music) of that string. Most guitars use an equal tempered tuning of 12 notes per octave. string guitar has range of octaves with Pitch (music) from 82.407 Hertz (low E) to 2093 kHz (high C). Harmonics may reach over 20 kHz in the inaudible range. Extension: Piano Let us look at another stringed instrument: the piano. The piano has strings that you can not see. When key is pressed felt-tipped hammer hits string inside the piano. The Pitch (music) depends on the Length Tension (physics) and Mass of the string. But there are many more strings than keys on piano. This is because the short and thin strings are not as loud as the long and heavy strings. To make up for this the higher keys have groups of two to four strings each. The soundboard in piano is large cast iron plate. It picks up vibrations from the strings. This heavy plate can withstand over 200 tons of pressure from string Tension (physics) Its Mass also allows the piano to sustain notes for long periods of time. The piano has wide Frequency range from 275 Hertz (low A) to 41860 Hertz (upper C). But these are just the fundamental Frequency piano plays complex rich Musical tone with over 20 harmonics per note. Some of these are out of the range of human hearing. Very low piano notes can be heard mostly because of their higher harmonics. 
---------------------------------------------------------

16.3|Standing Waves in Wind Instruments|377
wind instrument is an instrument that is usually made with pipe or thin tube. Examples of wind instruments are recorders clarinets flutes organs etc. When one plays wind instrument the air that is pushed through the pipe vibrates and Standing wave are formed. Just like with strings the Wavelength of the Standing wave will depend on the Length of the pipe and whether it is open or closed at each end. Lets consider each of the following situations: pipe with both ends open like flute or organ pipe. pipe with one end open and one closed like clarinet. If you blow across small hole in pipe or reed it makes Sound If both ends are open Standing wave will form according to figure 16.2. You will notice that there is an anti-node at each end. In the next activity you will find how this affects the Wavelength The formula is different because there are more anti-nodes than nodes. The right formula is: Here is still the number of nodes. long Wavelength has low Frequency and low Pitch (music) If you took your pipe from the last example and covered one end you should hear much lower note Also the Wavelength of the harmonics for this tube are not integer multiples of each other. Extension: Musical Scale The 12 Musical tone scale popular in Western music took centuries to develop. This scale is also called the 12-note Equal Tempered scale. It has an octave divided into 12 steps. (An octave is the main interval of most scales. If you double Frequency you have raised the note one octave.) All steps have equal ratios of Frequency But this scale is not perfect. If the octaves are in tune all the other intervals are slightly mistuned. No interval is badly out of tune. But none is perfect. For example suppose the base note of scale is Frequency of 110 Hertz low A). The first harmonic is 220 Hertz This note is also an but is one octave higher. The second harmonic is at 330 Hertz (close to an E). The third is 440 Hertz (also an A). But not all the notes have such simple ratios. Middle has Frequency of about 262 Hertz This is not simple multiple of 110 Hertz So the interval between and is little out of tune. Many other types of tuning exist. Just Tempered scales are tuned so that all intervals are simple ratios of Frequency There are also equal tempered scales with more or less notes per octave. Some scales use as many as 31 or 53 notes. 
---------------------------------------------------------

16.4|Resonance|382
Resonance (particle physics) is the tendency of system to vibrate at maximum Amplitude at the natural Frequency of the system. Resonance (particle physics) takes place when system is made to vibrate at its natural Frequency as result of vibrations that are received from another source of the same Frequency In the following investigation you will measure the Speed of Sound using Resonance (particle physics) Interesting fact: Soldiers march out of time on bridges to avoid stimulating the bridge to vibrate at its natural Frequency From the investigation you will notice that the column of air will make Sound at certain Length This is where Resonance (particle physics) takes place. 
---------------------------------------------------------

16.5|Music and Sound Quality|384
In the Sound chapter we referred to the quality of Sound as its Musical tone What makes the Musical tone of note played on an instrument? When you pluck string or vibrate air in tube you hear mostly the fundamental Frequency Higher harmonics are present but are fainter. These are called overtones. The Musical tone of note depends on its mixture of overtones. Different instruments have different mixtures of overtones. This is why the same note Sound different on flute and piano. Let us see how overtones can change the shape of Wave The resultant waveform is very different from the fundamental Frequency Even though the two Wave have the same main Frequency they do not Sound the same

17|Electrostatics|387

---------------------------------------------------------

17.1|Introduction|387
In Grade 10 you learnt about the Force between Electric charge In this chapter you will learn exactly how to determine this Force and about basic law of Electrostatics 
---------------------------------------------------------

17.2|Forces between charges - Coulomb's Law|387
Like Electric charge repel each other while opposite Electric charge attract each other. If the Electric charge are at rest then the Force between them is known as the Electrostatics Force The Electrostatics Force between Electric charge increases when the magnitude of the Electric charge increases or the Distance between the Electric charge decreases. The Electrostatics Force was first studied in detail by Charles Coulomb around 1784. Through his observations he was able to show that the Electrostatics Force between two point-like Electric charge is inversely proportional to the square of the Distance between the objects. He also discovered that the Force is proportional to the product of the Electric charge on the two objects. Q1Q2 r2 where Electric charge is the Electric charge on the one point-like object Electric charge is the Electric charge on the second and is the Distance between the two. The magnitude of the Electrostatics Force between two point-like Electric charge is given by Coulomb Law. Definition: Coulomb Law Coulomb Law states that the magnitude of the Electrostatics Force between two point Electric charge is directly proportional to the magnitudes of each Electric charge and inversely proportional to the square of the Distance between the Electric charge Q1Q2 r2 and the proportionality constant is called the Electrostatics constant and has the value: 899 109N m2 C2. Extension: Similarity of Coulomb Law to the Newtons Universal Newton's law of universal gravitation Notice how similar Coulomb Law is to the form of Newtons Universal Newton's law of universal gravitation between two point-like particles: where m1 and m2 are the Mass of the two particles is the Distance between them and is the Gravitational constant Both laws represent the Force exerted by particles Mass or Electric charge on each other that interact by means of Field (physics) It is very interesting that Coulomb Law has been shown to be correct no matter how small the Distance nor how large the Electric charge For example it still applies inside the atom (over Distance smaller than 1010m). Next is another example that demonstrates the difference in magnitude between the gravitational Force and the Electrostatics Force Important: We can apply Newtons Third Law to Electric charge because two Electric charge exert Force of equal magnitude on one another in opposite Direction (geometry) Important: Coulomb Law When substituting into the Coulomb Law equation it is not necessary to include the signs of the Electric charge Instead select Position (vector) Direction (geometry) Then Force that tend to move the Electric charge in this Direction (geometry) are added while Force that act in the opposite Direction (geometry) are subtracted. We mentioned in Chapter that Electric charge placed on spherical Electrical conductor spreads evenly along the surface. As result if we are far enough from the Electric charge sphere Electrostatics it behaves as point-like Electric charge Thus we can treat spherical Electrical conductor (e.g. metallic balls) as point-like Electric charge with all the Electric charge acting at the centre. 
---------------------------------------------------------

17.3|Electric field around charges|392
We have learnt that objects that carry Electric charge feel Force from all other Electric charge objects. It is useful to determine what the effect of Electric charge would be at every point surrounding it. To do this we need some sort of reference. We know that the Force that one Electric charge feels due to another depends on both Electric charge Electric charge and Electric charge How then can we talk about Force if we only have one Electric charge The solution to this dilemma is to introduce test Electric charge We then determine the Force that would be exerted on it if we placed it at certain location. If we do this for every point surrounding Electric charge we know what would happen if we put test Electric charge at any location. This map of what would happen at any point we call an Electric field map. It is map of the Electric field due to Electric charge It tells us how large the Force on test Electric charge would be and in what Direction (geometry) the Force would be. Our map consists of the lines that tell us how the test Electric charge would move if it were placed there. Definition: Electric field An Electric field as region of space in which an Electric charge experiences Force The Direction (geometry) of the Electric field at point is the Direction (geometry) that Position (vector) test Electric charge would move if placed at that point. 
---------------------------------------------------------

17.3.1|Electric field lines|393
The maps depend very much on the Electric charge or Electric charge that the map is being made for. We will start off with the simplest possible case. Take single Position (vector) Electric charge with no other Electric charge around it. First we will look at what effects it would have on test Electric charge at number of points. Electric field lines like the Magnet Field (physics) lines that were studied in Grade 10 are way of representing the Electric field at point. Arrows on the Field (physics) lines indicate the Direction (geometry) of the Field (physics) i.e. the Direction (geometry) Position (vector) test Electric charge would move. Electric field lines therefore point away from Position (vector) Electric charge and towards Electric charge Field (physics) lines are drawn closer together where the Field (physics) is stronger. 
---------------------------------------------------------

17.3.2|Positive charge acting on a test charge|393
At each point we calculate the Force on test Electric charge and represent this Force by Euclidean vector We can see that at every point the Position (vector) test Electric charge would experience Force pushing it away from the Electric charge Electric charge This is because both Electric charge are Position (vector) and so they repel. Also notice that at points further away the Euclidean vector are shorter. That is because the Force is smaller if you are further away. Electric charge acting on test Electric charge If the Electric charge were negative we would have the following result. Notice that it is almost identical to the Position (vector) Electric charge case. This is important the arrows are the same Length because the magnitude of the Electric charge is the same and so is the magnitude of the test Electric charge Thus the magnitude (size) of the Force is the same. The arrows point in the opposite Direction (geometry) because the Electric charge now have opposite sign and so the test Electric charge is attracted to the Electric charge Now to make things simpler we draw continuous lines showing the path that the test Electric charge would travel. This means we dont have to Work (physics) out the magnitude of the Force at many different points. Some important points to remember about Electric field There is an Electric field at every point in space surrounding Electric charge Field (physics) lines are merely representation they are not real. When we draw them we just pick convenient places to indicate the Field (physics) in space. Field (physics) lines always start at right-angle (90o) to the Electric charge object causing the Field (physics) Field (physics) lines never cross. 
---------------------------------------------------------

17.3.3|Combined charge distributions|394
We will now look at the Field (physics) of Position (vector) Electric charge and Electric charge placed next to each other. The net resulting Field (physics) would be the addition of the Field (physics) from each of the Electric charge To start off with let us sketch the Field (physics) maps for each of the Electric charge separately. Notice that test Electric charge starting off directly between the two would be pushed away from the Position (vector) Electric charge and pulled towards the Electric charge in straight line. The path it would follow would be straight line between the Electric charge Now lets consider test Electric charge starting off bit higher than directly between the Electric charge If it starts closer to the Position (vector) Electric charge the Force it feels from the Position (vector) Electric charge is greater but the Electric charge also attracts it so it would move away from the Position (vector) Electric charge with tiny Force attracting it towards the Electric charge As it gets further from the Position (vector) Electric charge the Force from the negative and Position (vector) Electric charge change and they are equal in magnitude at equal Distance from the Electric charge After that point the Electric charge starts to exert stronger Force on the test Electric charge This means that the test Electric charge moves towards the Electric charge with only small Force away from the Position (vector) Electric charge Two like Electric charge both Position (vector) For the case of two Position (vector) Electric charge things look little different. We cant just turn the arrows around the way we did before. In this case the test Electric charge is repelled by both Electric charge This tells us that test Electric charge will never cross half way because the Force of repulsion from both Electric charge will be equal in magnitude. The Field (physics) directly between the Electric charge cancels out in the middle. The Force has equal magnitude and opposite Direction (geometry) Interesting things happen when we look at test Electric charge that are not on line directly between the two. We know that Electric charge the same Distance below the middle will experience Force along Reflection (physics) line because the problem is symmetric (i.e. if we flipped vertically it would look the same). This is also true in the horizontal Direction (geometry) So we use this fact to easily draw in the next four lines. Two like Electric charge both negative We can use the fact that the Direction (geometry) of the Force is reversed for test Electric charge if you change the sign of the Electric charge that is influencing it. If we change to the case where both Electric charge are negative we get the following result:

17.3.4|Parallel plates|397
One very important example of Electric field which is used extensively is the Electric field between two Electric charge parallel plates. In this situation the Electric field is constant. This is used for many practical purposes and later we will explain how Millikan used it to measure the Electric charge on the Electron Field (physics) map for oppositely Electric charge parallel plates This means that the Force that test Electric charge would feel at any point between the plates would be identical in magnitude and Direction (geometry) The Field (physics) on the edges exhibit fringe effects i.e. they bulge outwards. This is because test Electric charge placed here would feel the effects of Electric charge only on one side (either left or right depending on which side it is placed). Test Electric charge placed in the middle experience the effects of Electric charge on both sides so they balance the components in the horizontal Direction (geometry) This is clearly not the case on the edges. Strength of materials of an Electric field When we started making Field (physics) maps we drew arrows to indicate the Strength of materials of the Field (physics) and the Direction (geometry) When we moved to lines you might have asked Did we forget about the Field (physics) Strength of materials We did not. Consider the case for single Position (vector) Electric charge again: Notice that as you move further away from the Electric charge the Field (physics) lines become more spread out. In Field (physics) map diagrams the closer Field (physics) lines are together the stronger the Field (physics) Therefore the Electric field is stronger closer to the Electric charge (the Electric field lines are closer together) and weaker further from the Electric charge (the Electric field lines are further apart). The magnitude of the Electric field at point as the Force per unit Electric charge Therefore and are Euclidean vector From this we see that the Force on Electric charge is simply: The Force between two Electric charge is given by: Qq r2 (if we make the one Electric charge and the other Electric charge Therefore the Electric field can be written as: r2 The Electric field is the Force per unit of Electric charge and hence has units of newtons per Coulomb As with Coulomb law calculations do not substitute the sign of the Electric charge into the equation for Electric field Instead choose Position (vector) Direction (geometry) and then either add or subtract the contribution to the Electric field due to each Electric charge depending upon whether it points in the Position (vector) or negative Direction (geometry) respectively.

17.4|Electrical potential energy and potential|400
The Electric potential energy of Electric charge is the Energy it has because of its Position (vector) relative to other Electric charge that it interacts with. The Potential energy of Electric charge Electric charge relative to Electric charge Electric charge Distance away is calculated by:

17.4.1|Electrical potential|400
The Electric potential at point is the Electric potential energy per unit Electric charge i.e. the Potential energy Position (vector) test Electric charge would have if it were placed at that point. Consider Position (vector) test Electric charge Electric charge placed at in the Electric field of another Position (vector) point Electric charge The test Electric charge moves towards under the influence of the Electric field of the other Electric charge In the process the test Electric charge loses Electric potential energy and gains kinetic Energy Thus at the test Electric charge has more Potential energy than at is said to have higher Electric potential than B. The Potential energy of Electric charge at point in Field (physics) is defined as the Work (physics) required to move that Electric charge from infinity to that point. Definition: Voltage The Voltage between two points in an Electric field is defined as the Work (physics) required to move unit Position (vector) test Electric charge from the point of lower potential to that of higher potential. If an amount of Work (physics) is required to move Electric charge from one point to another then the Voltage between the two points is given by unit J.C1 or (the volt) From this equation we can define the volt. Definition: The Volt One volt is the Voltage between two points in an Electric field if one Joule of Work (physics) is done in moving one Coulomb of Electric charge from the one point to the other.

17.4.2|Real-world application: lightning|402
Lightning is an atmospheric discharge of electricity usually but not always during rain storm. An understanding of lightning is important for Power (physics) transmission lines as engineers who need to know about lightning in order to adequately protect lines and equipment. Extension: Formation of lightning 1. Electric charge separation The first process in the generation of lightning is Electric charge separation. The mechanism by which Electric charge separation happens is still the subject of research. One theory is that opposite Electric charge are driven apart and Energy is stored in the Electric field between them. Cloud electrification appears to require strong updrafts which carry water droplets upward supercooling them to 10 to 20 oC. These Collision with ice crystals to form soft ice-water mixture called graupel. The collisions result in slight Position (vector) Electric charge being transferred to ice crystals and slight Electric charge to the graupel. Updrafts drive lighter ice crystals upwards causing the cloud top to accumulate increasing Position (vector) Electric charge The heavier Electric charge graupel falls towards the middle and lower portions of the cloud building up an increasing Electric charge Electric charge separation and accumulation continue until the Electric potential becomes sufficient to initiate lightning discharges which occurs when the gathering of Position (vector) and Electric charge forms sufficiently strong Electric field 2. Leader formation As thundercloud moves over the Earths surface an equal but opposite Electric charge is induced in the Earth below and the induced ground Electric charge follows the movement of the cloud. An initial bipolar discharge or path of ionized air starts from Electric charge mixed water and ice region in the thundercloud. The discharge ionized channels are called leaders. The Electric charge leaders called stepped leader proceed generally downward in number of quick jumps each up to 50 Metre long. Along the way the stepped leader may branch into number of paths as it continues to descend. The progression of stepped leaders takes comparatively long time (hundreds of milliseconds) to approach the ground. This initial phase involves relatively small electric Electric current (tens or hundreds of amperes) and the leader is almost invisible compared to the subsequent lightning channel. When step leader approaches the ground the presence of opposite Electric charge on the ground enhances the Electric field The Electric field is highest on trees and tall buildings. If the Electric field is strong enough conductive discharge (called Position (vector) streamer) can develop from these points. As the Field (physics) increases the Position (vector) streamer may evolve into hotter higher Electric current leader which eventually connects to the descending stepped leader from the cloud. It is also possible for many streamers to develop from many different objects simultaneously with only one connecting with the leader and forming the main discharge path. Photographs have been taken on which non-connected streamers are clearly visible. When the two leaders meet the electric Electric current greatly increases. The region of high Electric current propagates back up the Position (vector) stepped leader into the cloud with return stroke that is the most luminous part of the lightning discharge. 3. Discharge When the Electric field becomes strong enough an electrical discharge (the bolt of lightning) occurs within clouds or between clouds and the ground. During the strike successive portions of air become conductive discharge channel as the Electron and Position (vector) ions of air molecules are pulled away from each other and Force to flow in opposite Direction (geometry) The electrical discharge rapidly superheats the discharge channel causing the air to expand rapidly and produce shock Wave heard as thunder. The rolling and gradually dissipating rumble of thunder is caused by the time delay of Sound coming from different portions of long stroke. Important: Estimating Distance of lightning strike The flash of lightning strike and resulting thunder occur at roughly the same time. But Light travels at 300 000 kilometres in second almost million times the Speed of Sound Sound travels at the slower Speed of 330 ms in the same time so the flash of lightning is seen before thunder is heard. By counting the seconds between the flash and the thunder and dividing by you can estimate your Distance from the strike and initially the actual storm cell (in kilometres). 
---------------------------------------------------------

17.5|Capacitance and the parallel plate capacitor|403

---------------------------------------------------------

17.5.1|Capacitors and capacitance|403
parallel plate Capacitor is device that consists of two oppositely Electric charge conducting plates separated by small Distance which stores Electric charge When Voltage is applied to the Capacitor Electric charge of equal magnitude but opposite polarity build up on each plate. Figure 17.1: Capacitor (C) connected in series with resistor (R) and an Energy source (E). Definition: Capacitance Capacitance is the Electric charge stored per volt and is measured in farad (F) Mathematically Capacitance is the ratio of the Electric charge on single plate to the Voltage across the plates of the Capacitor Capacitance is measured in farads (F). Since Capacitance is defined as the units are in terms of Electric charge over Voltage The unit of Electric charge is the Coulomb and the unit of the Voltage is the volt. One farad is therefore the Capacitance if one Coulomb of Electric charge was stored on Capacitor for every volt applied. of Electric charge is very large amount of Electric charge So for small amount of Voltage applied Capacitor can store enormous amount of Electric charge Therefore Capacitor are often denoted in terms of microfarads (1 106) nanofarads (1 109) or picofarads (1 1012). Important: is the magnitude of the Electric charge stored on either plate not on both plates added together. Since one plate stores Position (vector) Electric charge and the other stores Electric charge the total Electric charge on the two plates is zero. 
---------------------------------------------------------

17.5.2|Dielectrics|404
The Electric field between the plates of Capacitor is affected by the substance between them. The substance between the plates is called Dielectric Common substances used as Dielectric are mica perspex air paper and glass. When Dielectric is inserted between the plates of parallel plate Capacitor the Dielectric becomes polarised so an Electric field is induced in the Dielectric that opposes the Field (physics) between the plates. When the two Electric field are superposed the new Field (physics) between the plates becomes smaller. Thus the Voltage between the plates decreases so the Capacitance increases. In every Capacitor the Dielectric keeps the Electric charge on one plate from travelling to the other plate. However each Capacitor is different in how much Electric charge it allows to build up on the electrodes per Voltage applied. When scientists started studying Capacitor they discovered the property that the Voltage applied to the Capacitor was proportional to the maximum Electric charge that would accumulate on the electrodes. The constant that made this relation into an equation was called the Capacitance C. The Capacitance was different for different Capacitor But it stayed constant no matter how much Voltage was applied. So it predicts how much Electric charge will be stored on Capacitor when different Voltage are applied. 
---------------------------------------------------------

17.5.3|Physical properties of the capacitor and capacitance|404
The Capacitance of Capacitor is proportional to the surface area of the conducting plate and inversely proportional to the Distance between the plates. It is also proportional to the permittivity of the Dielectric The Dielectric is the non-conducting substance that separates the plates. As mentioned before Dielectric can be air paper mica perspex or glass. The Capacitance of parallel-plate Capacitor is given by: where is the permittivity of air is the area of the plates and is the Distance between the plates.
