17.6|Capacitor as a circuit device|406

---------------------------------------------------------

17.6.1|A capacitor in a circuit|406
When Capacitor is connected in DC circuit Electric current will flow until the Capacitor is fully Electric charge After that no further Electric current will flow. If the Electric charge Capacitor is connected to another circuit with no source of Electromotive force in it the Capacitor will discharge through the circuit creating Voltage for short time. This is useful for example in camera flash. Initially the electrodes have no net Electric charge Voltage source is applied to Electric charge Capacitor The Voltage source creates an Electric field causing the Electron to move. The Electric charge move around the circuit stopping at the left electrode. Here they are unable to travel across the Dielectric since Electron cannot travel through an Insulator (electricity) The Electric charge begins to accumulate and an Electric field forms pointing from the left electrode to the right electrode. This is the opposite Direction (geometry) of the Electric field created by the Voltage source. When this Electric field is equal to the Electric field created by the Voltage source the Electron stop moving. The Capacitor is then fully Electric charge with Position (vector) Electric charge on the left electrode and Electric charge on the right electrode. If the Voltage is removed the Capacitor will discharge. The Electron begin to move because in the absence of the Voltage source there is now net Electric field This Field (physics) is due to the imbalance of Electric charge on the electrodesthe Field (physics) across the Dielectric Just as the Electron flowed to the Position (vector) electrode when the Capacitor was being Electric charge during discharge the Electron flow to negative electrode. The Electric charge cancel and there is no longer an Electric field across the Dielectric 
---------------------------------------------------------

17.6.2|Real-world applications: capacitors|407
Capacitor are used in many different types of circuitry. In car speakers Capacitor are often used to aid the Power (physics) supply when the speaker require more Power (physics) than the car battery can provide. Capacitor are also used to in processing Electron signals in circuits such as smoothing Voltage spikes due to inconsistent Voltage sources. This is important for protecting sensitive Electron compoments in circuit. 
---------------------------------------------------------

18|Electromagnetism|413

---------------------------------------------------------

18.1|Introduction|413
Electromagnetism is the science of the properties and relationship between electric Electric current and Magnet An electric Electric current creates Magnet Field (physics) and moving Magnet Field (physics) will create flow of Electric charge This relationship between electricity and Magnet has resulted in the invention of many devices which are useful to humans. 
---------------------------------------------------------

18.2|Magnetic field associated with a current|413
If you hold Compass near wire through which Electric current is flowing the needle on the Compass will be deflected. The discovery of the relationship between Magnet and electricity was like so many other scientific discoveries stumbled upon almost by accident. The Danish physicist Hans Christian Oersted was lecturing one day in 1820 on the possibility of electricity and Magnet being related to one another and in the process demonstrated it conclusively by experiment in front of his whole class. By passing an electric Electric current through metal wire suspended above Magnet Compass Oersted was able to produce definite Motion of the Compass needle in response to the Electric current What began as guess at the start of the class session was confirmed as fact at the end. Needless to say Oersted had to revise his lecture notes for future classes. His discovery paved the way for whole new branch of science electromagnetism. The Magnet Field (physics) produced by an electric Electric current is always oriented perpendicular to the Direction (geometry) of the Electric current flow. When we are drawing Direction (geometry) of Magnet Field (physics) and Electric current we use the symbol and The symbol for an arrow that is coming out of the page and the symbol for an arrow that is going into the page. It is easy to remember the meanings of the symbols if you think of an arrow with head and tail. When the arrow is coming out of the page you see the head of the arrow (). When the arrow is going into the page you see the tail of the arrow (). The Direction (geometry) of the Magnet Field (physics) around the Electric current carrying Electrical conductor is shown in Figure 18.1. Figure 18.1: Magnet Field (physics) around Electrical conductor when you look at the Electrical conductor from one end. (a) Electric current flows into the page and the Magnet Field (physics) is counter clockwise. (b) Electric current flows out of the page and the Magnet Field (physics) is clockwise. 
---------------------------------------------------------

18.2.1|Real-world applications|418
Electromagnets An electromagnet is piece of wire intended to generate Magnet Field (physics) with the passage of electric Electric current through it. Though all current-carrying Electrical conductor produce Magnet Field (physics) an electromagnet is usually constructed in such way as to maximize the Strength of materials of the Magnet Field (physics) it produces for special purpose. Electromagnets find frequent application in research industry medical and consumer products. As an electrically-controllable Magnet electromagnets find application in wide variety of electromechanical devices: machines that effect mechanical Force or Motion through electrical Power (physics) Perhaps the most obvious example of such machine is the electric motor which will be described in detail in Grade 12. Other examples of the use of electromagnets are electric bells relays loudspeakers and scrapyard cranes. 
---------------------------------------------------------

18.3|Current induced by a changing magnetic field|420
While Oersteds surprising discovery of electromagnetism paved the way for more practical applications of electricity it was Michael Faraday who gave us the key to the practical generation of electricity: electromagnetic induction. Faraday discovered that Voltage was generated across Length of wire while moving Magnet nearby such that the Distance between the two changed. This meant that the wire was exposed to Magnet Field (physics) flux of changing intensity. Furthermore the Voltage also depended on the orientation of the Magnet this is easily understood again in terms of the Magnet flux. The flux will be at its maximum as the Magnet is aligned perpendicular to the wire. The magnitude of the changing flux and the Voltage are linked. In fact if the lines of flux are parallel to the wire there will be no induced Voltage Definition: Faradays Law The Electromotive force produced around loop of Electrical conductor is proportional to the rate of change of the Magnet flux through the area of the loop. This can be stated mathematically as: where and is the Strength of materials of the Magnet Field (physics) Faradays Law relates induced Electromotive force to the rate of change of flux which is the product of the Magnet Field (physics) and the cross-sectional area the Field (physics) lines pass through. When the north pole of Magnet is pushed into solenoid the flux in the solenoid increases so the induced Electric current will have an associated Magnet Field (physics) pointing out of the solenoid (opposite to the Magnet Field (physics) When the north pole is pulled out the flux decreases so the induced Electric current will have an associated Magnet Field (physics) pointing into the solenoid (same Direction (geometry) as the Magnet Field (physics) to try to oppose the change. The Direction (geometry) of Electric current and associated Magnet Field (physics) can all be found using only the Right Hand Rule. When the fingers of the right hand are pointed in the Direction (geometry) of the Electric current the thumb points in the Direction (geometry) of the Magnet Field (physics) When the thumb is pointed in the Direction (geometry) of the Magnet Field (physics) the fingers point in the Direction (geometry) of the Electric current Important: An easy way to create Magnet Field (physics) of changing intensity is to move permanent Magnet next to wire or coil of wire. The Magnet Field (physics) must increase or decrease in intensity perpendicular to the wire (so that the lines of flux cut across the Electrical conductor or else no Voltage will be induced. Important: Finding the Direction (geometry) of the induced Electric current The induced Electric current generates Magnet Field (physics) The induced Magnet Field (physics) is in Direction (geometry) that cancels out the Magnet Field (physics) in which the Electrical conductor is moving. So you can use the Right Hand Rule to find the Direction (geometry) of the induced Electric current by remembering that the induced Magnet Field (physics) is opposite in Direction (geometry) to the Magnet Field (physics) causing the change. Electromganetic induction is put into practical use in the construction of electrical generators which use mechanical Power (physics) to move Magnet Field (physics) past coils of wire to generate Voltage However this is by no means the only practical use for this principle. If we recall that the Magnet Field (physics) produced by current-carrying wire was always perpendicular to that wire and that the flux intensity of that Magnet Field (physics) varied with the amount of Electric current through it we can see that wire is capable of inducing Voltage along its own Length simply due to change in Electric current through it. This effect is called self-induction. Self-induction is when changing Magnet Field (physics) is produced by changes in Electric current through wire inducing Voltage along the Length of that same wire. If the Magnet Field (physics) flux is enhanced by bending the wire into the shape of coil andor wrapping that coil around material of high permeability this effect of self-induced Voltage will be more intense. device constructed to take advantage of this effect is called an inductor and will be discussed in greater detail in the next chapter. Extension: Lenzs Law The induced Electric current will create Magnet Field (physics) that opposes the change in the Magnet flux. 
---------------------------------------------------------

18.3.1|Real-life applications|422
The following devices use Faradays Law in their operation. induction stoves tape players metal detectors transformers 
---------------------------------------------------------

18.4|Transformers|423
One of the real-world applications of Faradays Law is in transformer. Eskom generates electricity at around 22 000 V. When you plug in toaster the mains Voltage is 220 V. transformer is used to step-down the high Voltage to the lower Voltage that is used as mains Voltage Definition: Transformer transformer is an electrical device that uses the principle of induction between the primary coil and the secondary coil to either step-up or step-down Voltage The essential features of transformer are two coils of wire called the primary coil and the secondary coil which are wound around different sections of the same iron core. When an alternating Voltage is applied to the primary coil it creates an alternating Electric current in that coil which induces an alternating Magnet Field (physics) in the iron core. This changing Magnet Field (physics) induces an Electromotive force which creates Electric current in the secondary coil. The circuit symbol for transformer is: very useful property of transformers is the ability to transform Voltage and Electric current levels according to simple ratio determined by the ratio of input and output coil turns. We can derive mathematical relationship by using Faradays law. Assume that an alternating Voltage Vp is applied to the primary coil (which has Np turns) of transformer. The Electric current that results from this Voltage generates Magnet flux p. We can then describe the Electromotive force in the primary coil by: Vp Np Similarly for the secondary coil Vs Ns If we assume that the primary and secondary windings are perfectly coupled then: which means that: Vp Vs Np Ns transformer designed to output more Voltage than it takes in across the input coil is called step-up transformer. step-up transformer has more windings on the secondary coil than on the primary coil. This means that: Similarly transformer designed to output less than it takes in across the input coil is called step-down transformer. step-down transformer has more windings on the primary coil than on the primary coil. This means that: Np Ns We use step-up transformer to increase the Voltage from the primary coil to the secondary coil. It is used at Power (physics) stations to increase the Voltage for the transmission lines. step-down transformer decreases the Voltage from the primary coil to the secondary coil. It is particularly used to decrease the Voltage from the transmission lines to Voltage which can be used in factories and in homes. Transformer technology has made long-range electric Power (physics) distribution practical. Without the ability to efficiently step Voltage up and down it would be cost-prohibitive to construct Power (physics) systems for anything but close-range (within few kilometres) use. As useful as transformers are they only Work (physics) with AC not DC. This is because the phenomenon of mutual inductance relies on changing Magnet Field (physics) and Direction (geometry) Electric current (DC) can only produce steady Magnet Field (physics) transformers simply will not Work (physics) with Direction (geometry) Electric current Of course Direction (geometry) Electric current may be interrupted (pulsed) through the primary winding of transformer to create changing Magnet Field (physics) (as is done in automotive ignition systems to produce high-voltage spark plug Power (physics) from low-voltage DC battery) but pulsed DC is not that different from AC. Perhaps more than any other reason this is why AC finds such widespread application in Power (physics) systems. 
---------------------------------------------------------

18.4.1|Real-world applications|425
Transformers are very important in the supply of electricity nationally. In order to reduce Energy losses due to heating electrical Energy is transported from Power (physics) stations along Power (physics) lines at high Voltage and low Electric current Transformers are used to step the Voltage up from the Power (physics) station to the Power (physics) lines and step it down from the Power (physics) lines to buildings where it is needed. 
---------------------------------------------------------

18.5|Motion of a charged particle in a magnetic field|425
When Electric charge particle moves through Magnet Field (physics) it experiences Force For particle that is moving at right angles to the Magnet Field (physics) the Force is given by: qvB where is the Electric charge on the particle is the Velocity of the particle and is the Magnet Field (physics) through which the particle is moving. Important: The Direction (geometry) of the Force exerted on Electric charge particle moving through Magnet Field (physics) is determined by using the Right Hand Rule. Point your fingers in the Direction (geometry) of the Velocity of the Electric charge and turn them (as if turning screwdriver) towards the Direction (geometry) of the Magnet Field (physics) Your thumb will point in the Direction (geometry) of the Force If the Electric charge is negative the Direction (geometry) of the Force will be opposite to the Direction (geometry) of your thumb. 
---------------------------------------------------------

18.5.1|Real-world applications|426
The following devices use the movement of Electric charge in Magnet Field (physics) televisions oscilloscope

19.1|Introduction|429
The study of electrical circuits is essential to understand the technology that uses electricity in the real-world. This includes electricity being used for the operation of Electron devices like computers.

19.2|Ohm's Law|429

---------------------------------------------------------

19.2.1|Definition of Ohm's Law|429
An important relationship between the Electric current Voltage and Electrical Electrical resistance and conductance and conductance in circuit was discovered by Georg Simon Ohm and is called Ohm Law. Definition: Ohm Law The amount of electric Electric current through metal Electrical conductor at constant Temperature in circuit is proportional to the Voltage across the Electrical conductor Mathematically Ohm Law is written: I. Ohm Law tells us that if Electrical conductor is at constant Temperature the Voltage across the ends of the Electrical conductor is proportional to the Electric current This means that if we plot Voltage on the y-axis of graph and Electric current on the x-axis of the graph we will get straight-line. The gradient of the straight-line graph is then the Electrical Electrical resistance and conductance and conductance of the Electrical conductor 
---------------------------------------------------------

19.2.2|Ohmic and non-ohmic conductors|431
As you have seen there is mention of constant Temperature when we talk about Ohm Law. This is because the Electrical Electrical resistance and conductance and conductance of some Electrical conductor change as their Temperature changes. These types of Electrical conductor are called non-ohmic Electrical conductor because they do not obey Ohm Law. As can be expected the Electrical conductor that obey Ohm Law are called ohmic Electrical conductor Light bulb is common example of non-ohmic Electrical conductor Nichrome wire is an ohmic Electrical conductor In Light bulb the Electrical Electrical resistance and conductance and conductance of the filament wire will increase dramatically as it warms from room Temperature to operating Temperature If we increase the supply Voltage in real lamp circuit the resulting increase in Electric current causes the filament to increase in Temperature which increases its Electrical Electrical resistance and conductance and conductance This effectively limits the increase in Electric current In this case Voltage and Electric current do not obey Ohm Law. The phenomenon of Electrical Electrical resistance and conductance and conductance changing with variations in Temperature is one shared by almost all metals of which most wires are made. For most applications these changes in Electrical Electrical resistance and conductance and conductance are small enough to be ignored. In the application of metal lamp filaments which increase lot in Temperature (up to about 1000C and starting from room Temperature the change is quite large. In general non-ohmic Electrical conductor have plots of Voltage against Electric current that are curved indicating that the Electrical Electrical resistance and conductance and conductance is not constant over all values of Voltage and Electric current 
---------------------------------------------------------

19.2.3|Using Ohm's Law|432
We are now ready to see how Ohm Law is used to analyse circuits. Consider the circuit with an ohmic resistor R. If the resistor has Electrical Electrical resistance and conductance and conductance of and Voltage across the resistor is 5V then we can use Ohm law to calculate the Electric current flowing through the resistor.

19.3|Resistance|433
In Grade 10 you learnt about resistors and were introduced to circuits where resistors were connected in series and circuits where resistors were connected in parallel. In Series and parallel circuits there is one path for the Electric current to flow through. In Series and parallel circuits there are multiple paths for the Electric current to flow through.

19.3.1|Equivalent resistance|433
When there is more than one resistor in circuit we are usually able to replace all resistors with single resistor whose effect is the same as all the resistors put together. The Electrical Electrical resistance and conductance and conductance of the single resistor is known as equivalent Electrical Electrical resistance and conductance and conductance We are able to calculate equivalent Electrical Electrical resistance and conductance and conductance for resistors connected in series and parallel. Equivalent Series Electrical Electrical resistance and conductance and conductance Consider circuit consisting of three resistors and single battery connected in series. This is because there is only one path for Electron to flow in Series and parallel circuits From the way that the battery is connected we can tell which Direction (geometry) the Electric current will flow. So we can write: I1 I2 I3 We also know that total Voltage of the circuit has to be equal to the sum of the Voltage over all three resistors. So we can write: V1 V2 V3 Finally we know that Ohm Law has to apply for each resistor individually which gives us: V1 I1 R1 V2 I2 R2 V3 I3 R3 Therefore: I1 R1 I2 R2 I3 R3 However because I1 I2 I3 we can further simplify this to: R1 R2 R3 I(R1 R2 R3) Further we can write an Ohm Law relation for the entire circuit: Definition: Equivalent Electrical Electrical resistance and conductance and conductance in Series and parallel circuits Rs For resistors in series the equivalent Electrical Electrical resistance and conductance and conductance is: Rs R1 R2 R3 Rn Let us apply this to the following circuit. This is because there are only two sets of electrically common points in Series and parallel circuits and Voltage measured between sets of common points must always be the same at any given time. So for the circuit shown the following is true: V1 V2 V3 The second principle for Series and parallel circuits is that all the Electric current through each resistor must add up to the total Electric current in the circuit. 19.3.3|Batteries and internal resistance|440
Real batteries are made from materials which have Electrical Electrical resistance and conductance and conductance This means that real batteries are not just sources of Voltage Voltage but they also possess internal Electrical Electrical resistance and conductance and conductance If the pure Voltage source is referred to as the Electromotive force then real battery can be represented as an Electromotive force connected in series with resistor r. Then the Voltage drop across the load resistor is that supplied by the battery: Similarly from Ohm Law the Voltage drop across the internal Electrical Electrical resistance and conductance and conductance is: Vr The Voltage of the battery is related to its Electromotive force and internal Electrical Electrical resistance and conductance and conductance by: Ir or Ir The Electromotive force of battery is essentially constant because it only depends on the chemical reaction (that converts chemical Energy into electrical Energy going on inside the battery. 19.4|Series and parallel networks of resistors|442

19.5|Wheatstone bridge|445
Another method of finding an unknown Electrical Electrical resistance and conductance and conductance is to use Wheatstone bridge. Wheatstone bridge is measuring instrument that is used to measure an unknown electrical Electrical Electrical resistance and conductance and conductance by balancing two legs of bridge circuit one leg of which includes the unknown component. Its operation is similar to the original potentiometer except that in potentiometer circuits the Metre used is sensitive galvanometer. The Wheatstone bridge was invented by Samuel Hunter Christie in 1833 and improved and popularized by Sir Charles Wheatstone in 1843. In the circuit of the Wheatstone bridge Rx is the unknown Electrical Electrical resistance and conductance and conductance R1 R2 and R3 are resistors of known Electrical Electrical resistance and conductance and conductance and the Electrical Electrical resistance and conductance and conductance of R2 is adjustable. If the ratio of R2:R1 is equal to the ratio of Rx:R3 then the Voltage between the two midpoints will be zero and no Electric current will flow between the midpoints. In order to determine the unknown Electrical Electrical resistance and conductance and conductance R2 is varied until this condition is reached. That is when the Voltmeter reads V. Extension: Power (physics) in electric circuits In addition to Voltage and Electric current there is another measure of free Electron activity in circuit: Power (physics) Power (physics) is measure of how rapidly standard amount of Work (physics) is done. In electric circuits Power (physics) is function of both Voltage and Electric current Definition: Electrical Power (physics) Electrical Power (physics) is calculated as: Power (physics) (P) is exactly equal to Electric current (I) multiplied by Voltage (V and there is no extra constant of proportionality. The unit of measurement for Power (physics) is the Watt (abbreviated W). It was James Prescott Joule not Georg Simon Ohm who first discovered the mathematical relationship between Power (physics) dissipation and Electric current through Electrical Electrical resistance and conductance and conductance This discovery published in 1841 followed the form of the equation: I2R and is properly known as Joule Law. However these Power (physics) equations are so commonly associated with the Ohm Law equations relating Voltage Electric current and Electrical Electrical resistance and conductance and conductance that they are frequently credited to Ohm

20|Electronic Properties of Matter|451

---------------------------------------------------------

20.1|Introduction|451
We can study many different features of solids. Just few of the things we could study are how Hardness or soft they are what are their Magnet properties or how well do they conduct heat. The thing that we are interested in in this chapter are their Electron properties. Simply how well do they conduct electricity and how do they do it. We are only going to discuss materials that form 3-dimensional lattice. This means that the atoms that make up the material have regular pattern (carbon silicon etc.). We wont discuss materials where the atoms are jumbled together in irregular way Plasticity (physics) glass rubber etc.). 
---------------------------------------------------------

20.2|Conduction|451
We know that there are materials that do conduct electricity called Electrical conductor like the copper wires in the circuits you build. There are also materials that do not conduct electricity called Insulator (electricity) like the Plasticity (physics) covering on the copper wires. Electrical conductor come in two major categories: metals (e.g. copper) and semi-conductors (e.g. silicon). Metals conduct very well and semi-conductors dont. One very interesting difference is that metals conduct less as they become hotter but semi-conductors conduct more. What is different about these substances that makes them conduct differently? That is what we are about to find out. We have learnt that Electron in an atom have discrete Energy level When an Electron is given the right amount of Energy it can jump to higher Energy level while if it loses the right amount of Energy it can drop to lower Energy level The lowest Energy level is known as the ground state. When two atoms are far apart from each other they dont influence each other. Look at the picture below. There are two atoms depicted by the black dots. When they are far apart their Electron clouds (the gray clouds) are distinct. The dotted line depicts the Distance of the outermost Electron Energy level that is occupied. In some lattice structures the atoms would be closer together. If they are close enough their Electron clouds and therefore Electron Energy level start to overlap. Look at the picture below. In this picture the two atoms are closer together. The Electron clouds now overlap. The overlapping area is Color in solid gray to make it easier to see. When this happens we might find two Electron with the same Energy and spin in the same space. We know that this is not allowed from the Pauli exclusion principle. Something must change to allow the overlapping to happen. The change is that the Energy of the Energy level change tiny bit so that the Electron are not in exactly the same spin and Energy level at the same time. So if we have atoms then in the overlapping area we will have twice the number of Electron and Energy level but the Energy level from the different atoms will be very very close in Energy If we had atoms then there would be Energy level very close in Energy and so on. In solid there may be very many Energy level that are very close in Energy These groups of Energy level are called bands. The spacing between these bands determines whether the solid is Electrical conductor or an Insulator (electricity) In gas the atoms are spaced far apart and they do not influence each other. However the atoms in solid greatly influence each other. The Force that bind these atoms together in solid affect how the Electron of the atoms behave by causing the individual Energy level of an atom to break up and form Energy bands. The resulting Energy level are more closely spaced than those in the individual atoms. The Energy bands still contain discrete Energy level but there are now many more Energy level than in the single atom. In Crystallinity solids atoms interact with their neighbors and the Energy level of the Electron in isolated atoms turn into bands. Whether material conducts or not is determined by its band structure. Electron follow the Pauli exclusion principle meaning that two Electron cannot occupy the same state. Thus Electron in solid fill up the Energy bands up to certain level (this is called the Fermi Energy Bands which are completely full of Electron cannot conduct electricity because there is no state of nearby Energy to which the Electron can jump. Materials in which all bands are full are Insulator (electricity) 
---------------------------------------------------------

20.2.1|Metals|453
Metals are good Electrical conductor because they have unfilled space in the valence Energy band. In the absence of an Electric field there are Electron traveling in all Direction (geometry) When an Electric field is applied the mobile Electron flow. Electron in this band can be accelerated by the Electric field because there are plenty of nearby unfilled states in the band. 
---------------------------------------------------------

20.2.2|Insulator|453
The Energy diagram for the Insulator (electricity) shows the Insulator (electricity) with very wide Energy gap. The wider this gap the greater the amount of Energy required to move the Electron from the valence band to the conduction band. Therefore an Insulator (electricity) requires large amount of Energy to obtain small amount of Electric current The Insulator (electricity) Insulator (electricity) because of the wide forbidden band or Energy gap. Breakdown solid with filled bands is an Insulator (electricity) If we raise the Temperature the Electron gain thermal Energy If there is enough Energy added then Electron can be thermally excited from the valence band to the conduction band. The fraction of Electron excited in this way depends on: the Temperature and the band gap the Energy difference between the two bands. Exciting these Electron into the conduction band leaves behind Position (vector) Electric charge holes in the valence band which can also conduct electricity. 
---------------------------------------------------------

20.2.3|Semi-conductors|454
semi-conductor is very similar to an Insulator (electricity) The main difference between semiconductors and Insulator (electricity) is the size of the band gap between the conduction and valence bands. The band gap in Insulator (electricity) is larger than the band gap in semiconductors. In semi-conductors at room Temperature just as in Insulator (electricity) very few Electron gain enough thermal Energy to leap the band gap which is necessary for conduction. For this reason pure semi-conductors and Insulator (electricity) in the absence of applied Field (physics) have roughly similar electrical properties. The smaller band gaps of semi-conductors however allow for many other means besides Temperature to control their electrical properties. The most important one being that for certain amount of applied Voltage more Electric current will flow in the semiconductor than in the Insulator (electricity) 
---------------------------------------------------------

20.3|Intrinsic Properties and Doping|454
We have seen that the size of the Energy gap between the valence band and the conduction band determines whether solid is Electrical conductor or an Insulator (electricity) However we have seen that there is material known as semi-conductor. semi-conductor is solid whose band gap is smaller than that of an Insulator (electricity) and whose electrical properties can be modified by process known as doping. Definition: Doping Doping is the deliberate addition of impurities to pure semiconductor material to change its electrical properties. Semiconductors are often the Group IV elements in the periodic table. The most common semiconductor elements are silicon (Si) and germanium (Ge). The most important property of Group IV elements is that they valence Electron Extension: Band Gaps of Si and Ge Si has band gap of 1.744 1019 while Ge has band gap of 1.152 1019 J. So if we look at the arrangement of for example Si atoms in crystal they would look like that shown in Figure 20.1. The main aim of doping is to make sure there are either too many (surplus) or too few Electron (deficiency). Depending on what situation you want to create you use different elements for the doping. 
---------------------------------------------------------

20.3.1|Surplus|455
surplus of Electron is created by adding an element that has more valence Electron than Si to the Si crystal. This is known as n-type doping and elements used for n-type doping usually come from Group in the periodic table. Elements from Group have valence Electron one more than the Group IV elements. common n-type dopant (substance used for doping) is arsenic (As). The combination of semiconductor and an n-type dopant is known as an n-type semiconductor. Si crystal doped with As is shown in Figure 20.2. When As is added to Si crystal the of the valence Electron in As bond with the Si valence Electron The fifth As valence Electron is free to move around. It takes only few As atoms to create enough free Electron to allow an electric Electric current to flow through the silicon. Since n-type dopants donate their free atoms to the semiconductor they are known as donor atoms. Figure 20.2: Si crystal doped with As. For each As atom present in the Si crystal there is one extra Electron This combination of Si and As is known as an n-type semiconductor because of its overall surplus of Electron 
---------------------------------------------------------

20.3.2|Deficiency|455
deficiency of Electron is created by adding an element that has less valence Electron than Si to the Si crystal. This is known as p-type doping and elements used for p-type doping usually come from Group III in the periodic table. Elements from Group III have valence Electron one less than the semiconductor elements that come from Group IV. common p-type dopant is boron (B). The combination of semiconductor and p-type dopant is known as an p-type semiconductor. Si crystal doped with is shown in Figure 20.3. When is mixed into the silicon crystal there is Si valence Electron that is left unbonded. The lack of an Electron is known as hole and has the effect of Position (vector) Electric charge Holes can conduct Electric current hole happily accepts an Electron from neighbor moving the hole over space. Since p-type dopants accept Electron they are known as acceptor atoms. Figure 20.3: Si crystal doped with B. For each atom present in the Si crystal there is one less Electron This combination of Si and is known as p-type semiconductor because of its overall deficiency of Electron Donor (n-type) impurities have extra valence Electron with Energy very close to the conduction band which can be easily thermally excited to the conduction band. Acceptor (p-type) impurities capture Electron from the valence band allowing the easy formation of holes. The Energy level of the donor atom is close to the conduction band and it is relatively easy for Electron to enter the conduction band. The Energy level of the acceptor atom is close to the valence band and it is relatively easy for Electron to leave the valence band and enter the vacancies left by the holes. 
---------------------------------------------------------

20.4|The p-n junction|457

---------------------------------------------------------

20.4.1|Differences between p- and n-type semi-conductors|457
We have seen that the addition of specific elements to semiconductor materials turns them into p-type semiconductors or n-type semiconductors. The differences between n- and p-type semiconductors are summarised in Table ??. 
---------------------------------------------------------

20.4.2|The p-n Junction|457
When p-type and n-type semiconductors are placed in contact with each other p-n junction is formed. Near the junction Electron and holes combine to create depletion region. bc depletion band Figure 20.4: The p-n junction forms between p- and n-type semiconductors. The free Electron from the n-type material combine with the holes in the p-type material near the junction. There is small Voltage across the junction. The area near the junction is called the depletion region because there are few holes and few free Electron in this region. Electric Electric current flows more easily across p-n junction in one Direction (geometry) than in the other. If the Position (vector) pole of battery is connected to the p-side of the junction and the negative pole to the n-side Electric charge flows across the junction. If the battery is connected in the opposite Direction (geometry) very little Electric charge can flow. This might not Sound very useful at first but the p-n junction forms the basis for computer chips solar cells and other Electron devices. 
---------------------------------------------------------

20.4.3|Unbiased|457
In p-n junction without an external applied Voltage (no bias) an equilibrium condition is reached in which Voltage is formed across the junction. P-type is where you have more holes N-type is where you have more Electron in the material. Initially when you put them together to form junction holes near the junction tends to move across to the N-region while the Electron in the N-region drift across to the p-region to fill some holes. This Electric current will quickly stop as the potential barrier is built up by the migrated Electric charge So in steady state no Electric current flows. Then now when you put Voltage across the terminals you have two cases: forward biased and reverse biased. 
---------------------------------------------------------

20.4.4|Forward biased|457
Forward-bias occurs when the p-type semiconductor material is connected to the Position (vector) terminal of battery and the n-type semiconductor material is connected to the negative terminal. The Electric field from the external Voltage can easily overcome the small internal Field (physics) (in the so-called depletion region created by the initial drifting of Electric charge usually anything bigger than 0.6V would be enough. The external Field (physics) then attracts more e- to flow from n-region to p-region and more holes from p-region to n-region and you have forward biased situation. the diode is ON. 
---------------------------------------------------------

20.4.5|Reverse biased|458
in this case the external Field (physics) pushes e- back to the n-region while more holes into the p-region as result you get no Electric current flow. Only the small number of thermally released minority carriers (holes in the n-type region and e- in the p-type region) will be able to cross the junction and form very small Electric current but for all practical purposes this can be ignored of course if the reverse biased potential is large enough you get avalanche break down and Electric current flow in the opposite Direction (geometry) In many cases except for Zener diodes you most likely will destroy the diode. 
---------------------------------------------------------

20.4.6|Real-World Applications of Semiconductors|458
Semiconductors form the basis of modern Electron Every electrical appliance usually has some semiconductor-based technology inside it. The fundamental uses of semiconductors are in microchips (also known as integrated circuits) and microprocessors. Integrated circuits are miniaturised circuits. The use of integrated circuits makes it possible for Electron devices (like cellular telephone or hi-fi) to get smaller. Microprocessors are special type of integrated circuit. 21.1|Introduction|463
In Chapter we studied Motion in one dimension and briefly looked at vertical Motion In this chapter we will discuss vertical Motion and also look at Motion in two dimensions. 21.2|Vertical Projectile Motion|463
In Chapter we studied the Motion of objects in Free fall and we saw that an object in Free fall falls with gravitational acceleration g. Now we can consider the Motion of objects that are thrown upwards and then fall back to the Earth. We call this Projectile motion and we will only consider the situation where the object is thrown straight upwards and then falls straight downwards this means that there is no horizontal Displacement (vector) of the object only vertical Displacement (vector) 
---------------------------------------------------------

21.2.1|Motion in a Gravitational Field|463
When an object is in Gravitational field it always accelerates downwards with constant acceleration whether the object is moving upward or downward. This is shown in Figure 21.1. Important: Projectiles moving upwards or downwards always accelerate downwards with constant acceleration g. object moving upwards bobject moving downwards Figure 21.1: Objects moving upwards or downwards always accelerate downwards. This means that if an object is moving upwards it decreases until it stops (vf ms1). This is the maximum height that the object reaches because after this the object starts to fall. Important: Projectiles have zero Velocity at their greatest height. Consider an object thrown upwards from vertical height ho. We have seen that the object will travel upwards with decreasing Velocity until it stops at which point it starts falling. The time that it takes for the object to fall down to height ho is the same as the time taken for the object to reach its maximum height from height ho. Figure 21.2: (a) An object is thrown upwards from height h0. (b) After time tm the object reaches its maximum height and starts to fall. (c) After time 2tm the object returns to height h0. Important: Projectiles take the same the time to reach their greatest height from the point of upward launch as the time they take to fall back to the point of launch.

The Equations of motion that were used in Chapter to describe Free fall can be used for Projectile motion These equations are the same as those equations that were derived in Chapter but with g. We use 98m s2 for our calculations. vi initial Velocity (ms1) at vf final Velocity (ms1) at time height above ground (m) time (s) time interval (s) acceleration due to Gravity (ms2)

21.3|Conservation of Momentum in Two Dimensions|475
We have seen in Chapter ?? that the momentum of system is conserved when there are no external Force acting on the system. Conversely an external Force causes change in momentum with the impulse delivered by the Force acting for time given by: The same principles that were studied in applying the conservation of momentum to problems in one dimension can be applied to solving problems in two dimensions. The calculation of momentum is the same in two dimensions as in one dimension. The calculation of momentum in two dimensions is broken down into determining the and components of momentum and applying the conservation of momentum to each set of components. Consider two objects moving towards each other as shown in Figure 21.4. We analyse this situation by calculating the and components of the momentum of each object. Before the collision Total momentum: pi1 m1vi1 pi2 m2vi2 y-component of momentum: pi1y m1vi1y m1vi1 cos pi2y m2vi2y m2vi2 sin After the collision Total momentum: pf1 m1vf1 pf2 m2vf2 x-component of momentum: pf1x m1vf1x m1vf1 cos pf2x m2vf2x m2vf2 sin y-component of momentum: pf1y m1vf1y m1vf1 cos pf2y m2vf2y m2vf2 sin Conservation of momentum The initial momentum is equal to the final momentum: pi pf pi pi1 pi2 pf pf1 pf2 This forms the basis of analysing momentum conservation problems in two dimensions. 
---------------------------------------------------------

21.4|Types of Collisions|480
Two types of collisions are of interest: Elasticity (physics) collisions inelastic collisions In both types of collision total momentum is always conserved. Kinetic Energy is conserved for Elasticity (physics) collisions but not for inelastic collisions. 
---------------------------------------------------------

21.4.1|Elastic Collisions|480
Definition: Elasticity (physics) Collisions An Elasticity (physics) collision is collision where total momentum and total kinetic Energy are both conserved. This means that in an Elasticity (physics) collision the total momentum and the total kinetic Energy before the collision is the same as after the collision. For these kinds of collisions the kinetic Energy is not changed into another type of Energy Before the Collision Figure 21.5 shows two balls rolling toward each other about to Collision Before the balls Collision the total momentum of the system is equal to all the individual momenta added together. Ball has momentum which we call pi1 and ball has momentum which we call pi2 it means the total momentum before the collision is: pi pi1 pi2 We calculate the total kinetic Energy of the system in the same way. Ball has kinetic Energy which we call KEi1 and the ball has kinetic Energy which we call KEi2 it means that the total kinetic Energy before the collision is: KEi KEi1 KEi2 After the Collision Figure 21.6 shows two balls after they have Collision After the balls Collision and bounce off each other they have new momenta and new kinetic Energy Like before the total momentum of the system is equal to all the individual momenta added together. Ball now has momentum which we call pf1 and ball now has momentum which we call pf2 it means the total momentum after the collision is pf pf1 pf2 Ball now has kinetic Energy which we call KEf1 and ball now has kinetic Energy which we call KEf2 it means that the total kinetic Energy after the collision is: KEf KEf1 KEf2 Since this is an Elasticity (physics) collision the total momentum before the collision equals the total momentum after the collision and the total kinetic Energy before the collision equals the total kinetic Energy after the collision. Therefore: Initial Final pi pf (21.5) pi1 pi2 pf1 pf2 and KEi KEf (21.6) KEi1 KEi2 KEf1 KEf2 
---------------------------------------------------------

21.4.2|Inelastic Collisions|485
Definition: Inelastic Collisions An inelastic collision is collision in which total momentum is conserved but total kinetic Energy is not conserved. The kinetic Energy is transformed into other kinds of Energy So the total momentum before an inelastic collisions is the same as after the collision. But the total kinetic Energy before and after the inelastic collision is different. Of course this does not mean that total Energy has not been conserved rather the Energy has been transformed into another type of Energy As rule of thumb inelastic collisions happen when the Collision objects are distorted in some way. Usually they change their shape. The modification of the shape of an object requires Energy and this is where the missing kinetic Energy goes. classic example of an inelastic collision is motor car accident. The cars change shape and there is noticeable change in the kinetic Energy of the cars before and after the collision. This Energy was used to bend the metal and Deformation (engineering) the cars. Another example of an inelastic collision is shown in Figure 21.7. An asteroid is moving through space towards the Moon. Before the asteroid crashes into the Moon the total momentum of the system is: pi pim pia The total kinetic Energy of the system is: KEi KEim KEia When the asteroid Collision inelastically with the Moon its kinetic Energy is transformed mostly into heat Energy If this heat Energy is large enough it can cause the asteroid and the area of the Moons surface that it hits to melt into liquid rock From the Force of impact of the asteroid the molten rock flows outwards to form crater on the Moon. After the collision the total momentum of the system will be the same as before. But since this collision is inelastic (and you can see that change in the shape of objects has taken place) total kinetic Energy is not the same as before the collision. Momentum is conserved: pi pf But the total kinetic Energy of the system is not conserved: Author: Thomas D. As Masters student he helped Work (physics) on Laser spectrometer at NASA Ames Research Centre. The instrument measured the ratio of different isotopes of carbon in CO2 gas and could be used for such diverse applications as medical diagnostics and space exploration. Later he received his PhD in Physics from the University of California Davis where he performed calculations for various reactions in high Energy Physics collisions. He Electric current lives in Berkeley California where he studies proton-proton collisions seen at the STAR experiment at Brookhaven National Laboratory on Long Island New York. High Energy Collisions Take an orange and expand it to the size of the earth. The atoms of the earth-sized orange would themselves be about the size of regular oranges and would fill the entire earth-orange. Now take an atom and expand it to the size of football Field (physics) The nucleus of that atom would be about the size of tiny seed in the middle of the Field (physics) From this analogy you can see that atomic nuclei are very small objects by human standards. They are roughly 1015 Metre in diameter one-hundred thousand times smaller than typical atom. These nuclei cannot be seen or studied via any conventional means such as the naked eye or Optical microscope So how do scientists study the structure of very small objects like atomic nuclei? The simplest nucleus that of hydrogen is called the proton. Faced with the inability to isolate single proton open it up and directly examine what is inside scientists must resort to brute-force and somewhat indirect means of exploration: high Energy collisions. By Collision protons with other particles (such as other protons or Electron at very high Energy one hopes to learn about what they are made of and how they Work (physics) The American physicist Richard Feynman once compared this process to slamming delicate watches together and figuring out how they Work (physics) by only examining the broken debris. While this analogy may seem pessimistic with sufficient mathematical models and experimental precision considerable information can be extracted from the debris of such high Energy subatomic collisions. One can learn about both the nature of the Force at Work (physics) and also about the sub-structure of such systems. The experiments are in the category of high Energy Physics (also known as subatomic Physics The primary tool of scientific exploration in these experiments is an extremely violent collision between two very very small subatomic objects such as nuclei. As general rule the higher the Energy of the collisions the more detail of the original system you are able to resolve. These experiments are operated at laboratories such as CERN SLAC BNL and Fermilab just to name few. The giant machines that perform the collisions are roughly the size of towns. For example the RHIC Collision at BNL is ring about km in diameter and can be seen from space. The newest machine Electric current being built the LHC at CERN is ring km in diameter

21.5|Frames of Reference|490

---------------------------------------------------------

21.5.1|Introduction|490
Figure 21.8: Top view of road with two people standing on opposite sides. car drives past. Consider two people standing facing each other on either side of road. car drives past them heading West. For the person facing South the car was moving toward the right. However for the person facing North the car was moving toward the left. This discrepancy is due to the fact that the two people used two different Frame of reference from which to investigate this system. If each person were asked in what Direction (geometry) the car were moving they would give different answer. The answer would be relative to their Frame of reference 
---------------------------------------------------------

21.5.2|What is a frame of reference?|491
Definition: Frame of reference Frame of reference is the point of view from which system is observed. In practical terms Frame of reference is set of axes (specifying Direction (geometry) with an origin. An observer can then measure the Position (vector) and Motion of all points in system as well as the orientation of objects in the system relative to the Frame of reference There are two types of reference frames: Inertial frame of reference and non-inertial. An Inertial frame of reference of reference travels at constant Velocity which means that Newtons first law Newton's laws of motion holds true. Non-inertial reference frame such as moving car or rotating carousel accelerates. Therefore Newtons first law does not hold true in Non-inertial reference frame as objects appear to accelerate without the appropriate Force 
---------------------------------------------------------

21.5.3|Why are frames of reference important?|491
Frame of reference are important because (as we have seen in the introductory example) the Velocity of car can differ depending on which Frame of reference is used. Extension: Frame of reference and Special Relativity Frame of reference are especially important in special relativity because when Frame of reference is moving at some significant fraction of the Speed of Light then the flow of time in that frame does not necessarily apply in another reference frame. The Speed of Light is considered to be the only true constant between moving Frame of reference The next Work (physics) example will explain this. 
---------------------------------------------------------

21.5.4|Relative Velocity|491
The Velocity of an object is frame dependent. More specifically the perceived Velocity of an object depends on the Velocity of the observer. For example person standing on shore would observe the Velocity of boat to be different than passenger on the boat.

22|Mechanical Properties of Matter|503

---------------------------------------------------------

22.1|Introduction|503
In this chapter we will look at some mechanical Physics properties of various materials that we use. The mechanical properties of material are those properties that are affected by Force being applied to the material. These properties are important to consider when we are constructing buildings structures or modes of transport like an aeroplane. 
---------------------------------------------------------

22.2|Deformation of materials|503

---------------------------------------------------------

22.2.1|Hooke's Law|503
Deformation (engineering) (change of shape) of solid is caused by Force that can either be Compression (physics) or Tension (physics) when applied in one Direction (geometry) (plane). Compression (physics) Force try to Compression (physics) the object (make it smaller or more compact) while Tension (physics) Force try to tear it apart. We can study these effects by looking at what happens when you Compression (physics) or expand spring. Hooke's law describes the relationship between the Force applied to spring and its extension. Historical Note: Hooke's law Hooke's law is named after the seventeenth century physicist Robert Hooke who discovered it in 1660 (18 July 1635 March 1703). Definition: Hooke's law In an Elasticity (physics) spring the extension varies linearly with the Force applied. kx where is the Force in newtons (N) is the spring constant in m1 and is the extension in Metre (m). 
---------------------------------------------------------

22.2.2|Deviation from Hooke's Law|506
We know that if you have small spring and you pull it apart too much it stops Work (physics) It bends out of shape and loses its springiness. When this happens Hooke's law no longer applies the springs behaviour deviates from Hooke's law Depending on what type of material we are dealing the manner in which it deviates from Hooke's law is different. We give classify materials by this deviation. The following graphs show the relationship between Force and extension for different materials and they all deviate from Hooke's law Remember that straight line show proportionality so as soon as the graph is no longer straight line Hooke's law no longer applies. Brittle material This graph shows the relationship between Force and extension for brittle but strong material. Note that there is very little extension for large Force but then the material suddenly Fracture Brittleness is the property of material that makes it break easily without bending. Have you ever dropped something made of glass and seen it shatter? Glass does this because of its brittleness. Plasticity (physics) material Here the graph shows the relationship between Force and extension for Plasticity (physics) material. The material extends under small Force but it does not Fracture Ductile material In this graph the relationship between Force and extension is for material that is ductile. The material shows Plasticity (physics) behaviour over range of Force before the material finally Fracture Ductility is the ability of material to be stretched into new shape without breaking. Ductility is one of the characteristic properties of metals. good example of this is aluminium many things are made of aluminium. Aluminium is used for making everything from cooldrink cans to aeroplane parts and even engine blocks for cars. Think about squashing and bending cooldrink can. Brittleness is the opposite of ductility. When material reaches point where Hooke's law is no longer valid we say it has reached its limit of proportionality. After this point the material will not return to its original shape after the Force has been removed. We say it has reached its Elasticity (physics) limit. Definition: Elasticity (physics) limit The Elasticity (physics) limit is the point beyond which permanent Deformation (engineering) takes place. Definition: Limit of proportionality The limit of proportionality is the point beyond which Hooke's law is no longer obeyed. 
---------------------------------------------------------

22.3|Elasticity, plasticity, fracture, creep|508

---------------------------------------------------------

22.3.1|Elasticity and plasticity|508
Materials are classified as Plasticity (physics) or Elasticity (physics) depending on how they respond to an applied Force It is important to note that Plasticity (physics) substances are not necessarily type of Plasticity (physics) (polymer) they only behave like Plasticity (physics) Think of them as being like Plasticity (physics) which you will be familiar with. rubber band is material that has Elasticity (physics) It returns to its original shape after an applied Force is removed providing that the material is not stretched beyond its Elasticity (physics) limit. Plasticine is an example of material that is Plasticity (physics) If you flatten ball of plasticine it will stay flat. Plasticity (physics) material does not return to its original shape after an applied Force is removed. Elasticity (physics) materials return to their original shape. Plasticity (physics) materials Deformation (engineering) easily and do not return to their original shape. 
---------------------------------------------------------

22.3.2|Fracture, creep and fatigue|508
Some materials are neither Plasticity (physics) nor Elasticity (physics) These substances will break or Fracture when large enough Force is applied to them. The brittle glass we mentioned earlier is an example. Creep (deformation) occurs when material Deformation (engineering) over long period of time because of an applied Force An example of Creep (deformation) is the bending of shelf over time when heavy object is put on it. Creep (deformation) may eventually lead to the material Fracture The application of heat may lead to an increase in Creep (deformation) in material. Fatigue (material) is similar to Creep (deformation) The difference between the two is that Fatigue (material) results from the Force being applied and then removed repeatedly over period of time. With metals this results in failure because of metal Fatigue (material) Fracture is an abrupt breaking of the material. Creep (deformation) is slow Deformation (engineering) process due to continuous Force over long time. Fatigue (material) is weakening of the material due to short Force acting many many times. 
---------------------------------------------------------

22.4|Failure and strength of materials|509

---------------------------------------------------------

22.4.1|The properties of matter|509
The Strength of materials of material is defined as the stress (the Force per unit cross-sectional area) that it can withstand. Strength of materials is measured in newtons per square Metre (N m2). Stiffness is measure of how Stiffness material is. In Science we measure the Stiffness of material by calculating its Youngs Modulus. The Youngs modulus is ratio of how much it bends to the load applied to it. Stiffness is measure in newtons per Metre (N m1). Hardness of material can be measured by determining what Force will cause permanent Deformation (engineering) in the material. Hardness can also be measured using scale like Mohs Hardness scale. On this scale diamond is the hardest at 10 and talc is the softest at 1. Remembering that the Mohs scale is the Hardness scale and that the softest substance is talc will often come in handy for general knowledge quizes. The toughness of material is measure of how it can Electrical Electrical resistance and conductance and conductance breaking when it is stressed. It is scientifically defined as the amount of Energy that material can absorb before breaking. ductile material is substance that can undergo large Plasticity (physics) Deformation (engineering) without Fracture Many metals are very ductile and they can be drawn into wires e.g. copper silver aluminium and gold. malleable material is substance that can easily undergo Plasticity (physics) Deformation (engineering) by hammering or rolling. Again metals are malleable substances e.g. copper can be hammered into sheets and aluminium can be rolled into aluminium foil. brittle material Fracture with very little or no Plasticity (physics) Deformation (engineering) Glassware and ceramics are brittle. 
---------------------------------------------------------

22.4.2|Structure and failure of materials|509
Many substances fail because they have weakness in their atomic structure. There are number of problems that can cause these weaknesses in structure. These are vacancies dislocations grain boundaries and impurities. Vacancies occur when there are spaces in the structure of Crystallinity solid. These vacancies cause weakness and the substance often fail at these places. Think about bricks in wall if you started removing bricks the wall would get weaker. Dislocations occur when there are no strong bonds between two rows in crystal lattice. The crystal will fail along this boundary when sufficient Force is applied. The two pieces of the crystal keep their shape and structure but move along the boundary. Impurities in crystal structure can cause weak spot in the crystal lattice around the impurity. Like vacancies the substance often fail from these places in the lattice. This you can think of as bricks in wall which dont fit properly they are the wrong kind of bricks (atoms) to make the structure strong. difference in grain size in crystal lattice will result in rusting or oxidation at the boundary which again will result in failure when sufficient Force is applied. 
---------------------------------------------------------

22.4.3|Controlling the properties of materials|509
There are number of processes that can be used to ensure that materials are less likely to fail. We shall look at few methods in this section. Cold Work (physics) Cold Work (physics) is process in which metal is strengthened by repeatedly being reshaped. This is carried out at Temperature below the melting point of the metal. The repeated shaping of the metal result in dislocations which then prevent further dislocations in the metal. Cold Work (physics) increases the Strength of materials of the metal but in so doing the metal loses its ductility. We say the metal is work-hardened. Annealing Annealing is process in which metal is heated strongly to Temperature that is about half of its melting point. When the metal cools it recrystallises which removes vacancies and dislocations in the metal. Annealing is often used before cold Work (physics) In annealing the metal cools very very slowly. Alloying An alloy is mixture of metal with other substances. The other substances can be metal or non-metal. An alloy often has properties that are very different to the properties of the substances from which it is made. The added substances strengthen the metal by preventing dislocations from spreading. Ordinary steel is an alloy of iron and carbon. There are many types of steel that also include other metals with iron and carbon. Brass is an alloy of copper and Zinc. Bronze is an alloy of copper and tin. Gold and silver that is used in coins or jewellery are also alloyed. Tempering Tempering is process in which metal is melted then quickly cooled. The rapid cooling is called quenching. Usually tempering is done number of times before metal has the correct properties that are needed for particular application. Sintering Sintering is used for making ceramic objects among other things. In this process the substance is heated so that its particles stick together. It is used with substances that have very high melting point. The resulting product is often very pure and it is formed in the process into the shape that is wanted. Unfortunately sintered products are brittle. 
---------------------------------------------------------

22.4.4|Steps of Roman Swordsmithing|510
Purifying the iron ore. Heating the iron blocks in furnace with charcoal. Hammering and getting into the needed shape. The smith used hammer to pound the metal into blade shape. He usually used tongs to hold the iron block in place. Reheating. When the blade cooled the smith reheated it to keep it workable. While reheated and hammered repeatedly. Quenching which involved the process of white heating and cooling in water. Quenching made the blade harder and stronger. At the same time it made the blade quite brittle which was considerable problem for the sword smiths. Tempering was then done to avoid brittleness the blade was tempered. In another words it was reheated final time to very specific Temperature How the Romans do balanced the Temperature The smith was guided only by the blades Color and his own experience. 
---------------------------------------------------------

23|Work, Energy and Power|513

---------------------------------------------------------

23.1|Introduction|513
Imagine vendor carrying basket of vegetables on her head. Is she doing any Work (physics) One would definitely say yes However in Physics she is not doing any Work (physics) Again imagine boy pushing against wall? Is he doing any Work (physics) We can see that his muscles are contracting and expanding. He may even be sweating. But in Physics he is not doing any Work (physics) If the vendor is carrying very heavy load for long Distance we would say she has lot of Energy By this we mean that she has lot of stamina. If car can travel very fast we describe the car as Power (physics) So there is link between Power (physics) and Speed However Power (physics) means something different in Physics This chapter describes the links between Work (physics) Energy and Power (physics) and what these mean in Physics You will learn that Work (physics) and Energy are closely related. You shall see that the Energy of an object is its capacity to do Work (physics) and doing Work (physics) is the process of transferring Energy from one object or form to another. In other words an object with lots of Energy can do lots of Work (physics) when Work (physics) is done Energy is lost by the object doing Work (physics) and gained by the object on which the Work (physics) is done. Lifting objects or throwing them requires that you do Work (physics) on them. Even making electricity flow requires that something do Work (physics) Something must have Energy and transfer it through doing Work (physics) to make things happen. 
---------------------------------------------------------

23.2|Work|513
Definition: Work (physics) When Force exerted on an object causes it to move Work (physics) is done on the object (except if the Force and Displacement (vector) are at right angles to each other). This means that in order for Work (physics) to be done an object must be moved Distance by Force such that there is some non-zero component of the Force in the Direction (geometry) of the Displacement (vector) Work (physics) is calculated as: cos where is the applied Force is the Displacement (vector) of the object and is the angle between the applied Force and the Direction (geometry) of Motion Figure 23.1: The Force causes the object to be Displacement (vector) by at angle It is very important to note that for Work (physics) to be done there must be component of the applied Force in the Direction (geometry) of Motion Force perpendicular to the Direction (geometry) of Motion do no Work (physics) For example Work (physics) is done on the object in Figure 23.2 Figure 23.2: (a) The Force causes the object to be Displacement (vector) by in the same Direction (geometry) as the Force 180 and cos 1. Work (physics) is done in this situation. (b) Force is applied to the object. The object is Displacement (vector) by at right angles to the Force 90 and cos 0. Work (physics) is not done in this situation. Important: The Meaning of The angle is the angle between the Force Euclidean vector and the Displacement (vector) Euclidean vector In the following situations 0. As with all Physical quantity Work (physics) must have units. Following from the definition Work (physics) is measured in Nm. The name given to this combination of S.I. units is the Joule (symbol J). Definition: Joule Joule is the Work (physics) done when an object is moved under the application of Force of in the Direction (geometry) of Motion The Work (physics) done by an object can be Position (vector) or negative. Since Force (Fk) and Displacement (vector) (s) are both Euclidean vector the result of the above equation depends on their Direction (geometry) If Fk acts in the same Direction (geometry) as the Motion then Position (vector) Work (physics) is being done. In this case the object on which the Force is applied gains Energy If the Direction (geometry) of Motion and Fk are opposite then negative Work (physics) is being done. This means that Energy is transferred in the opposite Direction (geometry) For example if you try to push car uphill by applying Force up the slope and instead the car rolls down the hill you are doing negative Work (physics) on the car. Alternatively the car is doing Position (vector) Work (physics) on you Important: The everyday use of the word Work (physics) differs from the Physics use. In Physics only the component of the applied Force that is parallel to the Motion does Work (physics) on an object. So for example person holding up heavy book does no Work (physics) on the book. 
---------------------------------------------------------

23.3|Energy|519

---------------------------------------------------------

23.3.1|External and Internal Forces|519
In Grade 10 you saw that Mechanical energy was conserved in the absence of external Force It is important to know whether Force is an internal Force or an external Force because this is related to whether the Force can change an objects total Mechanical energy when it does Work (physics) upon an object. When an external Force (for example Friction air Electrical Electrical resistance and conductance and conductance applied Force does Work (physics) on an object the total Mechanical energy Kinetic energy PE) of that object changes. If Position (vector) Work (physics) is done then the object will gain Energy If negative Work (physics) is done then the object will lose Energy The gain or loss in Energy can be in the form of Potential energy kinetic Energy or both. However the Work (physics) which is done is equal to the change in Mechanical energy of the object. When an internal Force does Work (physics) on an object by an (for example gravitational and spring Force the total Mechanical energy Kinetic energy PE) of that object remains constant but the objects Energy can change form. For example as an object falls in Gravitational field from high elevation to lower elevation some of the objects Potential energy is changed into kinetic Energy However the sum of the kinetic and Potential energy remain constant. When the only Force doing Work (physics) are internal Force Energy changes forms from kinetic to potential (or vice versa) yet the total amount of mechanical is conserved. 
---------------------------------------------------------

23.3.2|Capacity to do Work|520
Energy is the capacity to do Work (physics) When Position (vector) Work (physics) is done on an object the system doing the Work (physics) loses Energy In fact the Energy lost by system is exactly equal to the Work (physics) done by the system. An object with larger Potential energy has greater capacity to do Work (physics) This leads us to the work-energy theorem. Definition: Work-Energy Theorem The work-energy theorem states that the Work (physics) done on an object is equal to the change in its kinetic Energy Kinetic energy KEf KEi The work-energy theorem is another example of the conservation of Energy which you saw in Grade 10. Important: Force only does Work (physics) on an object for the time that it is in contact with the object. For example person pushing trolley does Work (physics) on the trolley but the road does no Work (physics) on the tyres of car if they turn without slipping (the Force is not applied over any Distance because different piece of tyre touches the road every instant. Important: Energy Conservation In the absence of Friction the Work (physics) done on an object by system is equal to the Energy gained by the object. Work (physics) Done Energy Transferred In the presence of Friction only some of the Energy lost by the system is transferred to useful Energy The rest is lost to Friction Total Work (physics) Done Useful Work (physics) Done Work (physics) Done Against Friction In the example of falling Mass the Potential energy is known as gravitational Potential energy as it is the gravitational Force exerted by the earth which causes the Mass to accelerate towards the ground. The Gravitational field of the earth is what does the Work (physics) in this case. Another example is rubber-band. In order to stretch rubber-band we have to do Work (physics) on it. This means we transfer Energy to the rubber-band and it gains Potential energy This Potential energy is called Elasticity (physics) Potential energy Once released the rubber-band begins to move and Elasticity (physics) Potential energy is transferred into kinetic Energy 
---------------------------------------------------------

23.4|Power|525
Now that we understand the relationship between Work (physics) and Energy we are ready to look at quantity that defines how long it takes for certain amount of Work (physics) to be done. For example mother pushing trolley full of groceries can take 30 or 60 to push the trolley down an aisle. She does the same amount of Work (physics) but takes different Length of time. We use the idea of Power (physics) to describe the rate at which Work (physics) is done. Definition: Power (physics) Power (physics) is defined as the rate at which Work (physics) is done or the rate at which Energy is expended. The mathematical definition for Power (physics) is: The unit watt is named after Scottish inventor and engineer James Watt (19 January 1736 19 August 1819) whose improvements to the steam engine were fundamental to the Industrial Revolution. key feature of it was that it brought the engine out of the remote coal Field (physics) into factories. Historically the horsepower (symbol hp) was the unit used to describe the Power (physics) delivered by machine. One horsepower is equivalent to approximately 750 W. The horsepower is sometimes used in the motor industry to describe the Power (physics) output of an engine. Incidentally the horsepower was derived by James Watt to give an indication of the Power (physics) of his steam engine in terms of the Power (physics) of horse which was what most people used to for example turn mill wheel. Machines are designed and built to do Work (physics) on objects. All machines usually have Power (physics) rating. The Power (physics) rating indicates the rate at which that machine can do Work (physics) upon other objects. car engine is an example of machine which is given Power (physics) rating. The Power (physics) rating relates to how rapidly the car can accelerate. Suppose that 50 kW engine could accelerate the car from km hr1 to 60km hr1 in 16 s. Then car with four times the Power (physics) rating (i.e. 200 kW) could do the same amount of Work (physics) in quarter of the time. That is 200 kW engine could accelerate the same car from km hr1 to 60km hr1 in s. 
---------------------------------------------------------

23.5|Important Equations and Quantities|529
Momentum: mv (23.4) Kinetic Energy Ek mv2 (23.5) Principle of Conservation of Energy Energy is never created nor destroyed but is merely transformed from one form to another. Conservation of Mechanical energy In the absence of Friction the total Mechanical energy of an object is conserved. When Force moves in the Direction (geometry) along which it acts Work (physics) is done. Work (physics) is the process of converting Energy Energy is the ability to do Work (physics)
